Ruling on Combining Waqf and Owned Property in One Sale
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of combining waqf and owned property in one sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of selling owned property when combined with waqf in a single contract, and distinguish the case of a mosque from non-mosque waqf.

Core Idea
Waqf is property; its inalienability does not spread to invalidate the owned portion.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when a seller combines waqf property with his own owned property in one sale, does the invalidity of selling waqf spread to the owned part? The correct view, based on the nature of waqf as property (māl), holds that the owned portion remains valid unless the waqf is a mosque.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two riwayat
Determine that there are two narrations on this issue: one invalidating the entire sale, another validating the owned part. Identify which is considered correct (sahih) by the author.
The author states that the correct view is the one that validates the owned property.
2
Understand the analogy to mudabbar
Recognize that waqf is compared to a mudabbar (a slave whose emancipation is tied to death). Both are property with a restriction on sale, but the restriction does not negate property status.
The invalidity of selling the mudabbar/waqf does not spread to the owned property combined in the same contract.
3
Distinguish mosque waqf
Note that a mosque is an exception: it is like a free person (ḥurr), so combining it with owned property invalidates the entire sale for both.
This exception applies only to an occupied (ʿāmir) mosque, not a ruined one.
4
Analyze the Constantinople fatwa controversy
Examine the mufti's fatwa that the sale of owned property is invalid when combined with waqf that has been ruled binding by a judge. Then see Qadi Khan's rebuttal that even after adjudication, waqf is not like a free person.
Qadi Khan's fatwas show that waqf remains open to sale under certain conditions (substitution, weak yield, usurpation, Hanbali judge).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Conclude that the relied-upon position is the validity of the owned property's sale when combined with non-mosque waqf. The waqf portion itself cannot be sold, but its presence does not spoil the owned part.
Only a mosque triggers complete invalidity. Other waqf types are treated like mudabbar.

Then know that an incident occurred in Constantinople involving combining waqf and owned property and selling them as one bundle. He answered that it is understood to refer to a waqf whose validity and bindingness have not been ruled upon, so it becomes subject to ijtihad like the mudabbar. Thus in the Hashiyah of al-Ramli. And in al-Shurnubalāliyya, he explicitly stated the voidness of selling waqf and did well thereby, placing it in the category of void sale. Since there is disagreement on voidness of selling waqf because it does not accept granting ownership or taking ownership, and he considered mistaken whoever considered it invalid (fāsid) and gave fatwa accordingly among scholars of the tenth century. His statement was refuted in his time by several treatises, and we wrote a treatise on it, which is Hāsam al-Hukkām, containing a statement of the invalidity of his saying and the voidness of his fatwa. Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a sale that combines waqf property with owned property in a single transaction. The author of al-Bahr al-Rā'iq is commenting on the matn of Kanz al-Daqā'iq. He begins by stating that there are two narrations on this issue, but the correct one (according to the author) is that the sale of the owned property is valid. The reasoning is that waqf, though inalienable, is still considered property (māl), so it does not render the entire contract invalid by association. This is analogous to a mudabbar (a slave whose emancipation is tied to the master's death), which is also property but cannot be sold. However, a mosque is different – it is like a free person (ḥurr), so if it is included in the sale, the entire transaction is invalid for both components. The discussion then moves to a fatwa incident in Constantinople: a mufti ruled the sale invalid, but this is challenged by the correct view. The mufti's response is that his fatwa only applies to a waqf that has not been ruled valid and binding by a judge; once a judge rules, the waqf becomes like a free person. But Qadi Khan's fatwas contradict this, stating that even after adjudication, a waqf is not like a free person because claims of ownership can still be heard and its combination with owned property does not invalidate the sale of the latter. The passage then lists various reasons that allow the sale of waqf after its bindingness, including substitution, weak yield, usurpation, or a Hanbali judge's ruling. The commentator concludes that waqf cannot be equated to a free person, and the correct position is that the owned part remains valid. The subsequent hashya notes further refine the discussion, noting that some scholars considered the sale of waqf as fāsid (invalid) while others as bāṭil (void), and that the latter is more correct because waqf cannot be owned. The passage ends with a reference to a treatise refuting a mistaken fatwa.

Key Terms
وقف (waqf): Endowment; a property dedicated for charitable purposes, inalienable but still considered property (māl)
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose emancipation is tied to the master's death; considered property but cannot be sold during master's lifetime
ملك (milk): Owned property; full ownership that can be sold

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in the Hanafi school, what happens if a person sells a shop that is partially owned and partially waqf (non-mosque) in one contract?
A) Both the owned and the waqf parts are invalidly sold.
B) The owned part is validly sold, but the waqf part is invalid.
C) The entire sale is void.
D) The sale is valid for both parts if the buyer agrees.

Key Takeaway
The sale of owned property combined with non-mosque waqf is valid; only a mosque voids the entire contract because it is like a free person.

Ownership in Fasid Sale Upon Possession
Explains ownership in fasid sale and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership in a fasid sale upon possession and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, the buyer owns the item by possession, because prohibition does not annul the contract's validity.

This passage addresses a key inter-madhhab dispute: does the buyer own the item in a defective (fasid) sale after taking possession? The Hanafi school affirms ownership upon possession, while the Shafi'i school denies it. Understanding this difference is crucial for determining when a sale is salvageable versus completely void.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the buyer owns the item by its value upon possession with the seller's permission.
Possession (qabd) is the critical condition.
2
Identify the Shafi'i opposition
Al-Shafi'i holds that no ownership transfers because the prohibition nullifies the contract ab initio.
For Shafi'i, fasid equals batil.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The pillar of sale (exchange of property) is valid; prohibition only renders the act sinful, not the contract invalid.
Analogy to sale during Friday adhan: valid but sinful.
4
Evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument
Shafi'i argues that prohibition abrogates validity, analogous to selling carrion or wine.
Hanafi responds that carrion and wine are not property (mal).
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi, the buyer becomes owner and pays value; in Shafi'i, the sale is void and no ownership passes.
The difference affects remedies for defective sales.

Chapter on the invalid sale, i.e., an explanation of the rulings of the invalid sale. We have mentioned that doing it is a sin, so he must repent. It is rescinded as will come. His statement: (The buyer's taking possession of the sold item in an invalid sale by the seller's order, and both of its countervalues are money, he owns the sold item by its value.) Al-Shafi'i said: He does not own it even if he takes possession, because it is prohibited, so the blessing of ownership is not attained through it, and because the prohibition abrogates the legal validity due to contradiction. Hence it does not confer ownership before possession. It becomes like when one sells for a carcass or sells wine for dirhams. Our evidence: The pillar of sale issued from its qualified person, applied to its subject, so it must be said to be concluded. There is no concealment of eligibility and suitability. Its pillar is exchange of property for property, which involves speech and prohibition. Prohibition establishes legality in our school because it necessitates the concept. Thus the sale itself is legal and through it the blessing of ownership is attained; only what is adjacent is prohibited, as in the sale at the time of the call to prayer. Ownership is not established before possession only to avoid confirming the adjacent invalidity, since it must be removed by reclamation; so abstaining from demanding is more proper. And because the cause has weakened due to its association with badness, reinforcement by possession is required for the ruling, similar to a gift. A carcass is not property, so the pillar is absent. And that mere delivery (takhliya) is not sufficient. Al-‘Imādī in al-Fuṣūl corrected it, and Qadi Khan in his fatwas corrected that delivery is possession and chose it in al-Khulāṣa. He generalized it, so it includes the possession of an agent. He said in al-Quniya: Appointment of agency for an invalid purchase is valid like agency for purchase at harvest and threshing, and the agent's possession for the principal makes it guaranteed by value. End. And what is before possession is excluded, so no ownership. He generalized it, so it includes constructive possession, as in al-Ẓahīriyya: If one buys a slave with an invalid purchase and does not take possession, and the seller orders him to emancipate, he emancipates, the emancipation is valid on behalf of the buyer because it is like the buyer's possession. If the buyer himself emancipates, it is not valid due to lack of ownership. This is wondrous: the one ordered owns by proxy while the one ordering does not own. He said in al-Nahr: I say: What is in Jam‘ al-Tafārīq must be based on the view that delivery is possession, so he restricted it to being present; otherwise, possession of a trust does not substitute for possession of the sold item, so pay attention. His statement: (And this is wondrous...) has already passed in the matter of a dhimmī selling wine and pork as an analogy. His statement: (And if the father buys...
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi‘i on the issue of ownership in a fasid (defective/invalid) sale. The matn states that if a buyer takes possession of the item in a fasid sale with the seller's permission, and both countervalues are money (i.e., the item is not something like wine which is not considered property), then the buyer owns the item by its value (i.e., he is liable to pay the value, not the price). Al-Shafi‘i argues that the buyer does not own it even after possession because the prohibition abrogates the legal validity of the contract. The Hanafi school responds that the prohibition does not negate the validity of the sale itself; rather, the sale still has its pillars and is considered concluded, but the defect (fasād) is attached to it. Therefore, the buyer can own the item by value, similar to how a sale at the time of the Friday call is valid but sinful. The reason ownership is not established before possession is to facilitate rescission. The passage then elaborates on related issues: if the item was already in the buyer's possession as a trust (amāna), he owns it upon acceptance of the offer; mere delivery (takhliya) is considered possession according to the preferred view; constructive possession (qabd ḥukmī) through an agent or through ordering the seller to do something (like emancipation) is also valid. The passage also discusses the case of wine as a price, where the value is due, not the wine itself. The overall ruling: in a fasid sale, ownership transfers upon valid possession with the seller's permission, and the buyer is liable for the value (qīma), not the agreed price.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bay‘ fāsid): A sale that is valid in terms of pillars but contains a deficiency in a condition, such as uncertainty or illegality in a non-essential aspect; it is sinful but can be rectified and ownership can transfer upon possession
قبض (qabd): Possession; physical or constructive taking of the sold item. In fasid sale, it is a condition for ownership transfer
قيمة (qīma): Value; the market worth of the item at the time of possession, which the buyer owes in a fasid sale instead of the agreed price

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to ownership in a fasid sale if the buyer takes possession of the item with the seller's permission?
A) The buyer does not own the item at all; it must be returned.
B) The buyer owns the item and must pay the agreed price.
C) The buyer owns the item by its value and must pay the market value.
D) The buyer owns the item but must return it because the sale is sinful.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a fasid sale validly transfers ownership upon possession, with the buyer obligated to pay the item's market value rather than the invalidated price.

Ownership Through Possession in a Fasid Sale
Explains the Hanafi ruling on qabd in fasid sale with Shafi'i refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership upon qabd in a fasid sale, explain the Hanafi proof, and compare it to the Shafi'i position, identifying the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, possession transfers ownership but only by paying value – the prohibition does not void the contract.

We previously established that a fasid sale is sinful yet legally effective. Now the key question: does the buyer actually become owner of the item? The Hanafi school answers yes, but with conditions. The Shafi'i school disagrees entirely. In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq presents the Hanafi ruling and refutes the Shafi'i argument, revealing the precise mechanism that makes the fasid sale effective upon possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Locate the statement: 'قبض المشتري المبيع في البيع الفاسد بأمر البائع... ملك المبيع بقيمته.' The ruling is: if the buyer takes possession by the seller's command and both the item and price are valid property (mal), the buyer becomes owner but must pay the item's market value (qimah), not the agreed price.
The value is assessed at the time of qabd.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi argument: the contract's pillars (offer, acceptance) come from capable parties and concern valid property, so the contract is concluded (mun'aqid). The prohibition does not cancel the contract; it only makes performance sinful. The prohibition actually presupposes the contract's existence. Ownership is deferred to qabd to avoid confirming the sin.
Analogy: selling during Friday call to prayer is sinful but the contract is valid.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that no ownership transfers because the sale is prohibited (mahzur) and thus cannot confer the 'blessing of ownership'. The prohibition abrogates legality. He analogizes to carrion and wine, which cannot be sold.
The Shafi'i position collapses the distinction between fasid and batil.
4
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response: the prohibition only relates to an external element (the defect), not the essence of exchange. Carrion is not mal, so there is no valid subject matter. Wine as thaman is accepted but it is valued, not sold as muthman. The true analogy is to hibah (gift), where the cause is weak and needs qabd to perfect ownership.
In hibah, the weak cause is the absence of consideration; here it is the prohibited element.
5
Apply the exceptions and nuances
Not all fasid sales convey ownership by qabd: e.g., the sale of a jester (hazl) does not. Also, mere takhliya (vacating) does not count as qabd per the correct view. Constructive qabd (e.g., ordering the seller to emancipate) does transfer ownership. Qabd by an agent is valid. If the item was already in the buyer's hand as a deposit (wadi'ah), ownership transfers by acceptance alone.
These details are drawn from the later part of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في البيع الفاسد أي في بيان أحكام البيع الفاسد قدمنا أن فعله معصية فعليه التوبة منها يفسخه كما سيأتي. قوله : (قبض المشتري المبيع في البيع الفاسد بأمر البائع وكل من عوضيه مال ملك المبيع بقيمته) وقال الشافعي لا يملكه وإن قبضه لأنه محظور فلا تنال به نعمة الملك ولأن النهي نسخ للمشروعية للتضاد ولهذا لا يفيده قبل القبض، وصار كما إذا باع بالميتة أو باع الخمر بالدراهم. ولنا أن ركن البيع صدر من أهله مضافاً إلى محله فوجب القول بانعقاده، ولا خفاء الأهلية والمحلية وركنه مبادلة المال بالمال وفيه الكلام والنهي يقرر المشروعية عندنا لاقتضائه التصور فنفس البيع مشروع وبه تنال نعمة الملك إنما المحظور ما يجاوره كما في البيع وقت النداء، وإنما لا يثبت الملك قبل القبض كيلا يؤدي إلى تقرير الفساد المجاور إذ هو واجب الرفع بالاسترداد فبالامتناع عن المطالبة أولى ولأن السبب قد ضعف لمكان اقترانه بالقبح فيشترط اعتضاده بالقبض في إفادة الحكم بمنزلة الهبة والميتة ليست بمال فانعدم الركن، ولو كان الخمر مثمناً فقد ذكرناه أول الباب وشيء آخر إن في الخمر الواجب هو القيمة وهي تصلح ثمناً لا مثمناً، أشار المصنف رحمه الله تعالى بذكر القبض إلى أنه ليس مقبوضاً في يده فلو كان في يده وديعة ملكه بمجرد القبول كما في فتح القدير، وإلى أن التخلية فيه لا تكفي وصححه العمادي في الفصول وصحح قاضيخان في فتاواه في باب قبض المبيع أنها قبض فيه واختاره في الخلاصة. وأطلقه فشمل قبض الوكيل قال في القنية : التوكيل بالشراء الفاسد صحيح كالتوكيل بالشراء إلى الحصاد والدياس وقبض الوكيل للموكل فيصير مضموناً بالقيمة .اهـ. وخرج ما قبل القبض فلا ملك له . وأطلقه فشمل القبض الحكمي لما في الظهيرية : لو اشترى عبداً شراء فاسداً ولم يقبضه فأمر البائع بإعتاقه فأعتقه عتقه صح عن المشتري لأنه بمنزلة قبض المشتري، ولو أعتقه المشتري بنفسه لا يصح لعدم الملك، وهذه عجيبة حيث ملك المأمور بمنزلة ما لم يملك الآمر . وقيد بقوله في البيع الفاسد للاحتراز عن الباطل فإنه لا يفيده ولكن ليس كل فاسد يملك بالقبض فقد كتبنا في الفوائد الفقهية أن بيع الهازل لا يملك بالقبض كما ذكره البزدوي في الأصول.
Chapter on irregular sale: meaning explaining the rulings of irregular sale. We have mentioned that performing it is a sin, so repentance is required and it is rescinded as will come. His statement: (The buyer's taking possession of the sold item in an irregular sale by the seller's command, and each of its two compensations is property, he owns the sold item for its value). Al-Shafi'i said: He does not own it even if he took possession, because it is prohibited so no blessing of ownership is attained, and because the prohibition abrogates the legality due to contradiction, hence it does not benefit before possession, and it becomes like when one sells carrion or sells wine for dirhams. Our argument: The pillar of sale originated from its qualified person related to its locus, so it must be said it is concluded. There is no hiddenness of capacity and suitability, and its pillar is exchange of property for property, and therein is speech, and the prohibition establishes legality according to us because it necessitates conceptualization, so the sale itself is legitimate and through it the blessing of ownership is attained. The prohibited is only what adjoins it, as in sale at the time of the call (to Friday prayer). And possession is not established before qabd in order not to lead to confirmation of the adjacent corruption, since it is obligatory to remove it by recovery, so refraining from demand is more appropriate. Also because the cause has weakened due to its association with ugliness, so it requires support by qabd to produce the ruling, like a gift. Carrion is not property, so the pillar is absent. If wine is the priced (thaman), we have mentioned it at the beginning of the chapter, and another thing: in wine the obligatory is the value, and it is suitable as thaman but not as muthman. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) indicated by mentioning qabd that it was not possessed in his hand; if it was in his hand as a deposit, he owns it merely by acceptance as in Fath al-Qadir. He also indicated that takhliya (mere release) does not suffice, and al-Imadi in al-Fusul corrected it, and Qadi Khan in his Fatawa in the chapter of qabd al-mabee' corrected that it is qabd therein, and it was chosen in al-Khulasa. And he released it so it includes qabd of the agent: it says in al-Qunyah: Agency for an irregular purchase is valid like agency for purchase until harvest and threshing, and the agent's qabd is for the principal, so it becomes guaranteed by value. And what is before qabd is excluded, so no ownership. And he released it so it includes constructive qabd: as in al-Dhahiriyyah: if one buys a slave with an irregular purchase and does not take qabd, and then orders the seller to emancipate him and he emancipates, the emancipation is valid on behalf of the buyer because it is like the buyer's qabd. If the buyer himself emancipates, it is not valid due to lack of ownership. This is astonishing: the commanded one owns as if the commander did not own. And he restricted by saying 'in an irregular sale' to avoid the void (batil) sale, for it does not benefit. But not every irregular sale becomes owned by qabd; we have written in al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyyah that the sale of a jester (hazl) is not owned by qabd, as al-Bazdawi mentioned in al-Usul.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling on possession (qabd) in an irregular (fasid) sale and engages in an inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i. The author begins by stating the ruling: In a fasid sale—where the contract is defective in a way that does not affect the essential pillars—if the buyer takes possession of the item by the seller's command, and both the item and the price are valid property (mal), then the buyer becomes the owner of the item, but he must pay its current value (qimah), not the agreed price. This is because the contract is valid in its essence but prohibited due to an external factor. The Shafi'i position is presented: the buyer does not own the item even after qabd, because the sale is prohibited (mahzur) and thus cannot confer the blessing of ownership; the prohibition abrogates the legality of the contract. Shafi'i analogizes to the sale of carrion or wine, which are not valid property. The Hanafi response follows: The pillar of the sale (offer and acceptance) came from a capable person (the contracting parties) and related to a valid locus (the item and price are property), so the contract must be considered concluded. The prohibition does not negate the contract's constitution; rather, it only affects the permissibility of the act. The prohibition actually affirms the contract's existence (since prohibition presupposes the act's conceptualization). The Hanafis illustrate this using the example of selling during the Friday call to prayer—the act is sinful, but the contract is still valid. Ownership is delayed until qabd to avoid confirming the adjacent corruption (the sinful element), which must be removed by recovery. The cause of ownership is weak due to the ugly element, so it needs the support of qabd, analogous to a gift (hibah), where ownership is not complete without possession. The analogies to carrion and wine are rejected because carrion is not property at all, and wine as a price (thaman) yields value, not the wine itself. The author then derives from the matn's mention of 'qabd' several points: (1) the item must not already be in the buyer's hand; if it is a deposit (wadi'ah), he owns it by mere acceptance. (2) Mere consignment (takhliya) does not count as qabd, according to the correct view (as per al-Imadi and Qadi Khan, though a contrary view exists). (3) Qabd by an agent counts as the principal's qabd, as an agent for a fasid purchase is valid. (4) Constructive qabd is valid, as in the case where the buyer orders the seller to emancipate the slave and the seller does so; this counts as qabd on behalf of the buyer. It is a curious case where the commanded person (the seller) owns on behalf of the commander (buyer) even though the commander did not own. Finally, the author clarifies that this ruling applies only to fasid sales, not batil (void) sales, which convey no ownership. Moreover, not all fasid sales confer ownership by qabd; for example, the sale of a jester (hazl) does not, as mentioned by al-Bazdawi in al-Usul.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fāsid): A sale that is valid in its essential pillars (offer, acceptance, subject matter, price) but has a defect that is not fundamental, e.g., a prohibited condition or lack of possession at the time of contract. It is morally sinful but legally actionable; the contract can be rescinded, but if possession occurs, ownership transfers.
القبض (al-qabḍ): Taking physical or constructive possession of the sold item. In fasid sales, it is the key event that transfers ownership.
القيمة (al-qīmah): The market value of the item at the time of qabd, which the buyer must pay to the seller in a fasid sale, not the agreed price.
التخلية (al-takhliyah): The seller vacating the item for the buyer without physically handing it over. According to the correct view in this text, it does not count as qabd in a fasid sale.
الهبة (al-hibah): A gift contract. Used as an analogy: in hibah, ownership is not complete without qabd; similarly, in fasid sale, qabd is needed due to the weak cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal effect if the buyer takes possession (qabd) with the seller's permission in a fasid sale?
A) The buyer does not own the item and the contract is void.
B) The buyer becomes owner of the item but must pay its market value (qimah).
C) The buyer becomes owner of the item at the agreed price.
D) The buyer must return the item and is entitled to a refund.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a fasid sale can transfer ownership upon possession, but the buyer must pay market value, not the agreed price.

Exception to Qabd: Father's Fasid Sale with Minor Son
States an exception to the rule of qabd in fasid sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the additional condition required for ownership transfer in a fasid sale between a father and his minor son, and distinguish it from the general rule.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, a father's purchase from his minor son's property requires both qabd and isti'mal for ownership to transfer.

The general rule in fasid sales is that a buyer becomes owner upon taking possession (qabd). However, the Hanafi school recognizes an important exception when the buyer is a father purchasing from his minor son's estate. In that case, the father's conflict of interest necessitates a stricter condition. This lesson unpacks that ruling from al-Muhit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and contract type
The buyer is the father, the seller is the minor son's estate, and the contract is fasid (e.g., price not fixed).
2
Recall the general rule on qabd in fasid sales
Normally, qabd transfers ownership to the buyer in a fasid sale.
3
Recognize the exception stated in the text
The text says qabd alone is not sufficient for this specific case.
4
Extract the additional requirement
The minor only becomes owner (through his father) after both qabd and isti'mal (actual use).
5
Note the source and authority
The ruling is from al-Muhit, a later Hanafi compendium, and is considered mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن الأب إذا اشترى ماله لابنه الصغير فاسداً أو باع كذلك فالقبض لا يكفي ولا يملكه إلا بقبضه واستعماله؛ في المحيط .
His statement: (And that the father, if he buys property for his minor son in a fasid manner, or sells likewise, then qabd is not sufficient and he does not own it except by his possession and usage; in al-Muhit).
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This is a specific ruling within the chapter on fasid sales. The passage presents the rule that when a father buys an item from his minor son's property for himself in a fasid manner, or sells the son's property in a fasid manner, the ordinary rule of qabd transferring ownership does not apply. Instead, the minor (through his father) does not become owner just by taking the item into his hand; rather, ownership requires both actual possession (qabd) and use (isti'mal). This is a precaution because the contract involves a potential conflict of interest (the father is buying from his own minor child) and the fasid nature makes it even more suspect. The source cited is al-Muhit.

Key Terms
الاستعمال (al-isti'māl): Actual use or utilization of the purchased item by the minor (e.g., using the slave, wearing the garment). It is required in addition to qabd for ownership to transfer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only qabd (possession) of the item.
B) Only the value (qimah) payment.
C) Both qabd and isti'mal (possession and use).
D) The father's permission alone.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale involving a father and his minor son, ownership transfers only upon both possession (qabd) and actual use (isti'mal), not upon qabd alone.

Talji'ah and Hazl Sales: Batil or Fasid? Effect on Qabd
intra-Hanafi dispute over talji'ah vs hazl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on talji'ah sales, distinguish between batil and fasid classifications, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf regarding qabd.

Core Idea
Talji'ah sales: some Hanafis rule batil (void), others fasid (corrupt) — the difference determines ownership on qabd.

This lesson examines a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute over collusive sales (talji'ah) and jest sales (hazl). While the school uniformly deems hazl batil, there is disagreement on talji'ah — some treat it as batil like hazl, others as fasid allowing ownership transfer via qabd. Understanding this difference is crucial for determining the buyer's rights and the validity of subsequent acts like emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views
The text presents a dispute: some say talji'ah is batil (Qadi Khan, al-Qunyah), others consider it fasid (the author's inclination).
2
Examine the basis for each view
The batil camp relies on lack of genuine intent; the fasid camp focuses on practical consequences (harm prevention) and treats it like a fasid sale where qabd conveys ownership with payment of value.
3
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
If batil, qabd does not transfer ownership (so buyer does not own, emancipation invalid); if fasid, qabd transfers ownership (buyer owns and must pay value).
4
Consider the author's tarjih
The author prefers the fasid view in cases like waqf sale with gross fraud, to avoid harm, but notes the inconsistency with evidentiary rules.
5
Reflect on the unresolved point
The passage ends with a puzzle: why is the claimant of talji'ah not believed (requires evidence) while claimant of hazl is believed? This hints at deeper issues of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم رأيت في القنية أن التلجئة باطل فحينئذ لا يرد على المصنف لأن كلامه في بيع الفاسد، وفي آخر القنية من الوصايا باع الوصي مال اليتيم بغبن فاحش فهو باطل لا يملك بالقبض ثم رقم آخر بل فاسد اهـ. أقول : ينبغي أن يجري القولان في بيع الوقف المشروط استبداله أو الخراب الذي جاز استبداله إذا بيع بغبن فاحش، وينبغي ترجيح الثاني. فيهما لأنه إذا ملك بالقبض وجبت قيمته فلا ضرر على اليتيم والوقف. وقيد بأمر البائع أي بإذنه لأنه بلا إذنه لا يفيد الملك وإنما ذكر والإذن دون الرضا لأنه لا يشترط في بعض أفراده في التصريح . ثم رأيت في قاضيخان ببطلانه حيث قال : فإن اختلفا فادعى أحدهما أن البيع تلجئة والآخر ينكر التلجئة لا يقبل قول مدعي التلجئة إلا ببينة ويستحلف الآخر. وصورة التلجئة في البيع أن يقول الرجل إني أبيع داري منك بكذا وليس ذلك ببيع في الحقيقة بل هو تلجئة ويشهد على ذلك ثم يبيع في الظاهر من غير شرط فهذا البيع يكون باطلاً بمنزلة بيع الهازل وعن محمد رحمه الله تعالى : بيع التلجئة إذا قبض المشتري العبد وأعتقه لا ينفذ إعتاقه ولا يشبه المشتري من المكره لأنه في الحكم بمنزلة البيع بشرط الخيار لهما اهـ. من الغزي. وفي قاضيخان أيضاً وذكر في إقرار الأصل أن بيع الهازل باطل اهـ. ويمكن أن يجاب عن إشكاله بأنه وإن كان كل من عوضيه مالاً لكن ليس ببيع حقيقة لعدم الاعتداد بما ذكرا من الإيجاب والقبول مع الهزل فكأنهما لم يوجدا، وإنما جاز إذا جعلاه جائزاً بعد ذلك بطريق جعله إنشاء، وإنما كان القول لمدعي الهزل لأنه ينكر وجود البيع ولا إشكال في ذلك على هذا القول لأن القول قول مدعي البطلان لكن ذكروا في التلجئة أنه لا يقبل قول مدعيها فهو مشكل لأنه يدعي البطلان وقالوا فيه إنه هزل، فما الفرق بين التلجئة والهزل في ذلك فتأمل . اهـ ملخصاً. وقوله «لأن القول قول مدعي البطلان أي لو اختلفا فيه وفي الصحة أما لو اختلفا في الصحة
Then I saw in al-Qunyah that talji'ah is batil, so then there is no objection to the author because his speech is about the fasid sale. And at the end of al-Qunyah on wills: the executor sold the orphan's property with gross fraud, it is batil, not owned by qabd. Then another note: rather fasid. I say: It is appropriate that the two views apply in the sale of waqf that is conditional to be replaced or ruined where replacement is permitted, if sold with gross fraud, and the second should be preferred in both because if it is owned by qabd, its value becomes wajib, so there is no harm to the orphan and waqf. And he restricted by 'with the seller's command' i.e., with his permission, because without his permission it does not confer ownership. He mentioned permission but not consent because it is not required in some of its parts in the explicit statement. Then I saw in Qadi Khan about its nullity, where he said: if they differ, one claims the sale is talji'ah and the other denies it, the claimant of talji'ah is not believed except with evidence, and the other is sworn. The form of talji'ah in sale is a man saying: I am selling my house to you for such and such, but that is not a sale in reality, rather it is talji'ah, and he witnesses on that, then he sells openly without condition. Thus this sale is batil like the sale of a jester (hazl). And from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): The sale of talji'ah, if the buyer takes possession of the slave and emancipates him, his emancipation is not effective, and it is not like the buyer from a coerced person because it is in ruling like a sale with a condition of option for both. Extracted from al-Ghazzi. And in Qadi Khan also: It is mentioned in Iqrar al-Asl that the sale of hazl is batil. It is possible to answer the objection by saying: although each of its two compensations is property, it is not a sale in reality due to the disregard of the offer and acceptance done in jest, as if they did not exist. It is only valid if they make it permissible afterwards by way of making it a new contract. And the claim is given to the claimant of hazl because he denies the existence of sale. There is no problem in this according to this view because the claim is given to the claimant of nullity. But in talji'ah, they said that the claimant is not believed, which is problematic because he claims nullity, and they said it is hazl. So what is the difference between talji'ah and hazl in that regard? Reflect. Summarized. And his statement 'because the claim is given to the claimant of nullity' i.e., if they differ about its nullity and validity. But if they differ about validity...
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of talji'ah (collusive sale) and hazl (jest sale) within Hanafi fiqh. The author begins by citing al-Qunyah, which states that talji'ah is batil (void), so it does not conflict with the earlier ruling that qabd confers ownership in fasid sales, because talji'ah is not fasid but batil. However, there is a difference of opinion: some consider certain cases (like the executor selling at a gross fraud) as batil, while others say it is fasid. The author suggests that in similar cases of waqf sales with gross fraud, the second view (fasid) should be preferred because if qabd transfers ownership, the value is paid, causing no harm. He then notes the restriction that qabd only works with the seller's permission (idhn), not necessarily consent (rida). Next, he references Qadi Khan, who states emphatically that talji'ah is batil, and in a dispute, the claimant of talji'ah must bring evidence. The picture of talji'ah is: a man says he is selling his house but it is not a real sale, just a collusion (e.g., to protect property from creditors), and he secretely witnesses that it is not a real sale. Such a sale is batil, like a jest sale. Imam Muhammad is reported to say that if the buyer in a talji'ah sale takes possession and emancipates a slave, the emancipation is ineffective, unlike in a sale of a coerced person, because it is treated like a sale with a khyar (option) for both parties. The author then addresses a possible objection: why is hazl considered batil even though both compensations are property? The answer is that hazl (jest) negates the seriousness of the contract, so the offer and acceptance are disregarded as if they never occurred; the contract can only be valid if they later renew it as a serious transaction. In hazl, the claimant who says it was in jest is believed because he denies the existence of a valid sale, similar to claiming nullity. But in talji'ah, the claimant is not believed without evidence, which seems contradictory because both are claims of nullity. The author leaves this as a point for reflection. Finally, he clarifies that the principle 'the claim is given to the claimant of nullity' applies when the dispute is about validity versus nullity; if they differ on validity itself (e.g., both claim it is valid but differ on terms), other rules apply.

Key Terms
التلجئة (al-talji'ah): A collusive sale where the parties outwardly contract but secretly agree it is not a real sale, often to protect property from creditors or for other deceptive purposes.
الهزل (al-hazl): A sale made in jest or joke, where the parties do not seriously intend to exchange property.
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghubn al-fahish): Gross fraud or overpayment in a sale, where the price is significantly unfair.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession of the sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Qadi Khan and some Hanafi sources, what is the legal status of a talji'ah (collusive) sale?
A) It is fasid and qabd transfers ownership.
B) It is batil and qabd does not transfer ownership.
C) It is sahih (valid) if the price is fair.
D) It is makruh but still transfers ownership upon qabd.

Key Takeaway
Talji'ah sales are not uniformly batil; the Hanafi school has a dispute, and the preferred view (fasid) allows qabd to transfer ownership to prevent harm in certain cases.

Fasid Sale and Ownership Through Possession
Expands the rule on fasid sales via qabd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which fasid sales do not confer ownership by possession and explain the role of intent.

Core Idea
Not every fasid sale grants ownership through possession—intent matters.

In Hanafi fiqh, a fasid (invalid) sale differs from a batil (void) sale in that possession (qabd) can sometimes transfer ownership. However, this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that this rule has exceptions. We will explore which fasid sales do not give ownership via qabd, such as the sale of a jester and certain guardian transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling: fasid vs batil
The chapter is on fasid sales to distinguish from batil. The author states that not every fasid sale confers ownership by possession.
Focus on the exceptions.
2
Analyze the example of bai' al-hazil
Al-Bazdawi and the author rule that a sale made in jest does not give ownership even after possession, due to lack of real intent.
Intent is crucial.
3
Examine the guardian's sale
If a father buys for his minor child fasidly, mere possession is insufficient; ownership requires actual use (isti'mal).
Protection of minor's property.
4
Distinguish talji'ah (protective sale)
Talji'ah is batil, not fasid, so it is outside the scope. The author notes this to avoid confusion.
Batil sales never give ownership.
5
Apply the two opinions to waqf sale
For waqf sold with gross fraud, there are two views: batil (no ownership) or fasid (ownership via qabd). The author prefers fasid to protect the waqf interest.
Tarjih based on maslahah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب البيع الفاسد
للإحتراز عن الباطل فإنه لا يفيده ولكن ليس كل فاسد يملك بالقبض فقد كتبنا في الفوائد الفقهية أن بيع الهازل لا يملك بالقبض كما ذكره البزدوي في الأصول، أن الأب إذا اشترى ماله لابنه الصغير فاسداً أو باع كذلك فالقبض لا يكفي ولا يملكه إلا بقبضه واستعماله؛ في المحيط .
من [؟] كذا [؟] هو
ثم رأيت في القنية أن التلجئة باطل فحينئذ لا يرد على المصنف لأن كلامه في بيع الفاسد، وفي آخر القنية من الوصايا باع الوصي مال اليتيم بغبن فاحش فهو باطل لا يملك بالقبض ثم رقم آخر بل فاسد اهـ. أقول : ينبغي أن يجري القولان في بيع الوقف المشروط استبداله أو الخراب الذي جاز استبداله إذا بيع بغبن فاحش، وينبغي ترجيح الثاني. فيهما لأنه إذا ملك بالقبض وجبت قيمته فلا ضرر على اليتيم والوقف.
Book of Sales, Chapter of Invalid Sale: To distinguish it from the void (batil) for it does not benefit it, but not every invalid (fasid) sale is owned by possession. We have written in al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyya that the sale of a jester is not owned by possession as mentioned by al-Bazdawi in al-Usul, that if a father buys for his minor son invalidly or sells likewise, possession is not sufficient and he does not own it except by taking possession and using it; in al-Muhit. ... Then I saw in al-Qunyah that a protective sale (talji'ah) is void, so then it does not refute the author because his speech is about the invalid sale. And at the end of al-Qunyah from the chapter of wills: if a guardian sells the orphan's property with gross fraud, it is void, not owned by possession, then another marginal note says rather invalid. I say: It is appropriate that the two opinions apply in the sale of an endowment (waqf) that is conditional to be replaced or ruined that is permissible to replace if sold with gross fraud, and the second should be preferred. In both, because if it is owned by possession, its value becomes obligatory, so there is no harm on the orphan and the endowment.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding on the ruling of fasid (invalid) sale. The author begins by clarifying that while the chapter is about fasid sale (to distinguish it from batil, or void), not every fasid sale confers ownership simply by taking possession (qabd). He cites examples from earlier texts: the sale of a jester (bai' al-hazil) does not give ownership via qabd, as stated by al-Bazdawi. Similarly, if a father buys something from his minor son's property in a fasid manner, or sells to him, mere possession is insufficient; ownership only occurs after actual use or consumption (qabd wa isti'mal). The author then references al-Qunyah, where a protective sale (talji'ah) is ruled batil, but he notes this does not contradict the author (al-Marghinani?) because the discussion here is on fasid, not batil. He adds an analogy: the same two opinions (fasid vs batil) apply to the sale of waqf property under certain conditions, and he prefers the view that it is fasid so that the buyer owns through qabd, thus protecting the orphan's or waqf's interests.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bai' fasid): An invalid sale that fulfills the essential elements but has a deficiency in a condition; may or may not confer ownership via qabd.
بيع الهازل (bai' al-hazil): A sale made in jest, which does not give ownership through possession according to the author.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical or constructive possession of the sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is an example of a fasid sale that does NOT give ownership through possession (qabd)?
A) Sale of a garment for a known price with mutual consent
B) Sale made by a jester (hazl) without real intent
C) Sale of a valid commodity with the option to return
D) Sale by a father of his own property to his adult son

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a fasid sale does not automatically transfer ownership by possession; exceptions like bai' al-hazil require real intent.

Bai' al-Talji'ah: Void or Invalid? The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on classification of protective sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between batil and fasid in the context of bai' al-talji'ah and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (burden of proof) between the two views.

Core Idea
In bai' al-talji'ah, 'batil' in some texts means 'resembles void in effect', not absolute nullity.

The protective sale (bai' al-talji'ah) is a transaction where the owner sells ostensibly to protect property without genuine intent. Hanafi scholars debated whether it is void (batil) or invalid (fasid). This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores the intra-madhhab disagreement and its practical consequences, particularly regarding ownership by possession and burden of proof.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The dominant Hanafi view from usul and furu' works is that talji'ah is fasid (invalid), not batil, because both countervalues are property. It does not give ownership by possession.
This view is attributed to general books of usul and furu'.
2
Identify the opposing view
Some scholars (author of al-Qunyah, Qadi Khan) consider it batil. Qadi Khan reports from Muhammad that it is void and the claimant of talji'ah must bring evidence.
The term batil is used in al-Qunyah but the author clarifies its intended meaning.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response reinterprets 'batil' in al-Qunyah to mean it shares the effect of voidness (no ownership by qabd), not that it is absolutely void. The fasid category is divided into two sub-types: those that give ownership via qabd and those that do not.
This reconciles the apparent contradiction.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference lies in the burden of proof. If talji'ah is batil, the claimant (asserting voidness) must prove it. If fasid, the default is fasid, shifting the burden to the one claiming validity.
This is illustrated by the rules on talji'ah vs. hazl (jest).
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The author leans toward the fasid view, as it aligns with the majority of Hanafi texts. The mufta bihi is that talji'ah is fasid, not batil.
This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم رأيت في القنية أن التلجئة باطل فحينئذ لا يرد على المصنف لأن كلامه في بيع الفاسد، وفي آخر القنية من الوصايا باع الوصي مال اليتيم بغبن فاحش فهو باطل لا يملك بالقبض ثم رقم آخر بل فاسد اهـ. قال الرملي : ما ذكر في القنية مشكل لأن كلاً من عوضي بيع الهازل مال فكيف يكون باطلاً وقد صرح عامة كتب الأصول والفروع أنه ينعقد فاسداً لا يفيد الملك بالقبض. وممن صرح بذلك ابن ملك في شرح المجمع ومن ثم صرحوا أن بيع المكره يقع فاسداً لكنه ينقض تصرف المشتري منه لعدم الرضا، فعلى هذا يكون معنى قول صاحب القنية إن بيع التلجئة باطل أي يشبه الباطل في عدم إفادته الملك فعلى هذا يكون الفاسد على نوعين : نوع يفيد الملك ونوع لا يفيده. ثم رأيت في قاضيخان ببطلانه حيث قال : فإن اختلفا فادعى أحدهما أن البيع تلجئة والآخر ينكر التلجئة لا يقبل قول مدعي التلجئة إلا ببينة ويستحلف الآخر. وصورة التلجئة في البيع أن يقول الرجل إني أبيع داري منك بكذا وليس ذلك ببيع في الحقيقة بل هو تلجئة ويشهد على ذلك ثم يبيع في الظاهر من غير شرط فهذا البيع يكون باطلاً بمنزلة بيع الهازل وعن محمد رحمه الله تعالى : بيع التلجئة إذا قبض المشتري العبد وأعتقه لا ينفذ إعتاقه ولا يشبه المشتري من المكره لأنه في الحكم بمنزلة البيع بشرط الخيار لهما اهـ. من الغزي. وفي قاضيخان أيضاً وذكر في إقرار الأصل أن بيع الهازل باطل اهـ. ويمكن أن يجاب عن إشكاله بأنه وإن كان كل من عوضيه مالاً لكن ليس ببيع حقيقة لعدم الاعتداد بما ذكرا من الإيجاب والقبول مع الهزل فكأنهما لم يوجدا، وإنما جاز إذا جعلاه جائزاً بعد ذلك بطريق جعله إنشاء، وإنما كان القول لمدعي الهزل لأنه ينكر وجود البيع ولا إشكال في ذلك على هذا القول لأن القول قول مدعي البطلان لكن ذكروا في التلجئة أنه لا يقبل قول مدعيها فهو مشكل لأنه يدعي البطلان وقالوا فيه إنه هزل، فما الفرق بين التلجئة والهزل في ذلك فتأمل . اهـ ملخصاً.
Then I saw in al-Qunyah that the protective sale (talji'ah) is void, so then it does not refute the author because his speech is about the invalid sale. And at the end of al-Qunyah from the chapter of wills: if a guardian sells the orphan's property with gross fraud, it is void, not owned by possession, then another marginal note says rather invalid. Al-Ramli said: What is mentioned in al-Qunyah is problematic because each of the two compensations of the joking sale is property, so how can it be void? While the general books of usul and furu' have stated that it is concluded as invalid (fasid) and does not benefit ownership by possession. Among those who stated that is Ibn Malik in Sharh al-Majma'. And from then they stated that the sale of the coerced one occurs as invalid, but it negates the transaction of the buyer due to lack of consent. So on this, the meaning of the author of al-Qunyah's statement that bai' al-talji'ah is void is that it resembles the void in not benefiting ownership. So on this, the invalid is of two types: a type that benefits ownership and a type that does not. Then I saw in Qadi Khan its voidness where he said: If they differ, one claiming the sale is a protective one and the other denying the protection, the claim of the one alleging protection is not accepted except with evidence, and the other is sworn. And the form of protective sale in sale is that a man says: I sell my house to you for such and such, and it is not a sale in reality but a protective measure, and he witnesses that, then he sells apparently without condition; so this sale is void, like the sale of a jester. And from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): The protective sale, if the buyer takes possession of the slave and emancipates him, his emancipation does not take effect, and he is not like the buyer from the coerced one because in ruling it is like a sale with option for both. End quote from al-Ghazzi. And in Qadi Khan also, it is mentioned in Iqrar al-Asl that the sale of a jester is void. And it is possible to answer his problem that although each of its compensations is property, it is not truly a sale due to lack of consideration of the offer and acceptance with jest, as if they did not exist. And it is permissible if they make it permissible later by making it a new contract. And the statement belongs to the claimant of jest because he denies the existence of the sale, and there is no problem in that on this view because the statement belongs to the claimant of voidness. But they mentioned in talji'ah that the statement of the one claiming it is not accepted, so it is problematic because he claims voidness. And they said it is jest, so what is the difference between talji'ah and jest in that? So reflect. End summarized.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the legal classification of bai' al-talji'ah (a protective sale where the owner sells ostensibly to protect property, e.g., from confiscation, but without real intent). The debate involves whether it is batil (void) or fasid (invalid). The author notes a contradiction: al-Qunyah says it is batil, but standard usul and furu' texts (al-Majma', etc.) treat it as fasid. Al-Ramli resolves that the term 'batil' here means it shares the effect of voidness (no ownership by qabd), not that it is absolutely void. The passage also cites Qadi Khan that both parties' statements differ (the claimant of talji'ah must bring proof). Imam Muhammad is quoted that even if the buyer takes possession and frees a slave, the emancipation does not take effect, likening it to a sale with option for both. The author ends by questioning the difference between talji'ah and hazl in terms of burden of proof.

Key Terms
تلجئة (talji'ah): A protective sale where the owner sells ostensibly without real intent, often to prevent confiscation or for security.
بيع الهازل (bai' al-hazil): A sale made in jest, considered fasid and does not give ownership by qabd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Invalid (fasid) and does not give ownership by qabd

Key Takeaway
The dominant Hanafi view classifies bai' al-talji'ah as fasid (invalid) rather than batil (void), but both agree it does not transfer ownership by possession.

Conditions for Qabd in Fasid Sale: Idhn and Mal
Stipulates conditions for qabd to be effective in fasid sale: seller's permission and both countervalues being property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions required for qabd (possession) to confer ownership in a fasid sale and explain why each condition is necessary based on Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, possession gives ownership only with seller's permission and both items being property.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that not all fasid sales confer ownership upon taking possession. The buyer must have the seller's explicit permission (idhn), and both items exchanged must be recognized as property (mal). These conditions ensure that the legal effect of possession aligns with the incomplete nature of the fasid contract. The text also notes the broader debate about the term 'fasid' itself, distinguishing it from 'batil' (void sales), with some scholars like al-Zayla'i using it more generally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Context
Recognize that the passage is about the legal effect of qabd (taking possession) in a fasid (invalid) sale. The ruling is that qabd can give ownership, but only under conditions.
The sale itself is fasid, meaning it is valid in essence but defective in some description (e.g., lack of consent in one party).
2
Extract the First Condition: Seller's Permission (Idhn)
The text states that qabd without the seller's permission does not give ownership. Note that the term used is 'idhn' (permission) rather than 'rida' (consent), as idhn is broader and sufficient even when rida is not explicitly present.
This condition prevents the buyer from acquiring ownership through mere physical possession without the seller's authorization.
3
Extract the Second Condition: Both Countervalues Are Property (Mal)
The condition that both the price and the commodity must be property (mal) ensures that the essential elements of a sale exist. The text mentions al-Hidayah's statement that this condition realizes the pillar (rukn) of the sale.
If either countervalue is not mal (e.g., wine, pork), the sale is batil (void), not fasid, and qabd cannot confer ownership.
4
Analyze the Proof and Intra-Hanafi Debate
The proof for these conditions comes from the general Hanafi principle that a fasid sale is not automatically completed by possession. The text cites earlier works like al-Hidayah and al-Qunyah, and discusses the disagreement among Hanafi scholars about the terminology of fasid vs batil.
Al-Zayla'i used 'fasid' broadly to include batil, but the author, following al-Hamawi, maintains a strict distinction. This affects which conditions are necessary for a fasid sale.
5
Apply the Conditions to a Case
Consider a scenario: A buyer purchases a car in a fasid contract (e.g., without proper specification). The seller gives permission for the buyer to take the car. Both the car and the price are property (mal). Qabd will confer ownership despite the fasid nature.
If the seller did not permit, or if the car was not property (e.g., stolen goods not recognized as mal), qabd would not give ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بأمر البائع أي بإذنه لأنه بلا إذنه لا يفيد الملك وإنما ذكر والإذن دون الرضا لأنه لا يشترط في بعض أفراده في التصريح . ماله لابنه الصغير فاسداً صواب العبارة : إذا اشترى من مال ابنه الصغير لنفسه فاسداً أو باع من من ماله لابنه كذلك. قال في النهر وفي المحيط : باع عبداً من ابنه الصغير فاسداً أو اشترى عبده لنفسه فاسداً لا يثبت الملك حتى يقبضه ويستعمله .اهـ. قوله : (ثم رأيت في القنية أن بيع التلجئة باطل) قال الرملي : ما ذكر في القنية مشكل لأن كلاً من عوضي بيع الهازل مال فكيف يكون باطلاً وقد صرح عامة كتب الأصول والفروع أنه ينعقد فاسداً لا يفيد الملك بالقبض. وممن صرح بذلك ابن ملك في شرح المجمع ومن ثم صرحوا أن بيع المكره يقع فاسداً لكنه ينقض تصرف المشتري منه لعدم الرضا، فعلى هذا يكون معنى قول صاحب القنية إن بيع التلجئة باطل أي يشبه الباطل في عدم إفادته الملك فعلى هذا يكون الفاسد على نوعين : نوع يفيد الملك ونوع لا يفيده. ثم رأيت في قاضيخان ببطلانه حيث قال : فإن اختلفا فادعى أحدهما أن البيع تلجئة والآخر ينكر التلجئة لا يقبل قول مدعي التلجئة إلا ببينة ويستحلف الآخر. وصورة التلجئة في البيع أن يقول الرجل إني أبيع داري منك بكذا وليس ذلك ببيع في الحقيقة بل هو تلجئة ويشهد على ذلك ثم يبيع في الظاهر من غير شرط فهذا البيع يكون باطلاً بمنزلة بيع الهازل وعن محمد رحمه الله تعالى : بيع التلجئة إذا قبض المشتري العبد وأعتقه لا ينفذ إعتاقه ولا يشبه المشتري من المكره لأنه في الحكم بمنزلة البيع بشرط الخيار لهما اهـ. من الغزي. وفي قاضيخان أيضاً وذكر في إقرار الأصل أن بيع الهازل باطل اهـ. ويمكن أن يجاب عن إشكاله بأنه وإن كان كل من عوضيه مالاً لكن ليس ببيع حقيقة لعدم الاعتداد بما ذكرا من الإيجاب والقبول مع الهزل فكأنهما لم يوجدا، وإنما جاز إذا جعلاه جائزاً بعد ذلك بطريق جعله إنشاء، وإنما كان القول لمدعي الهزل لأنه ينكر وجود البيع ولا إشكال في ذلك على هذا القول لأن القول قول مدعي البطلان لكن ذكروا في التلجئة أنه لا يقبل قول مدعيها فهو مشكل لأنه يدعي البطلان وقالوا فيه إنه هزل، فما الفرق بين التلجئة والهزل في ذلك فتأمل . اهـ ملخصاً. وقوله «لأن القول قول مدعي البطلان أي لو اختلفا فيه وفي الصحة أما لو اختلفا في الصحة والفساد فالمختار أن القول لمدعي الفساد كما في شرح مجمع قوله: (لأن الملك حصل بدونه) أي بدون القبض والأولى لأن الملك حصل به أي بالإيجاب . قوله : (اللهم إلا أن يقال أن بعض البيوع الخ) قال في النهر وأقول : هذا مما لا حاجة إليه بل الفاسد أعم على ما التزموه في أول الباب وحينئذ فلا بد من التصريح بهذا العقد لإخراج الباطل وهذا مما يجب أن يفهم من كلامهم في هذا المقام ومن تأمل ما في الهداية وغيرها وجده كالصريح به. ثم رأيته في الحواشي السعدية قال: في قول صاحب الهداية شرط أن يكون العوضان كل منهما مال ليتحقق ركن البيع يعني ليظهر تحققه فإن الفاسد قد يستعمل في المعنى العام للباطل أيضاً، وهذا طبق ما فهمته فتنبه له، وعلى هذا فقول الشارح أي الزيلعي إن قوله في البيع الفاسد احتراز عن الباطل مما لا ينبغي إذ الباطل إنما خرج بقوله وكل من عوضيه مال كما قد علمت .اهـ. وتعقبه الحموي بأن من أفراد الباطل ما لا يخرج بهذا القيد هو بيع الخمر والخنزير بالدراهم فإنه باطل مع أن كلاً من عوضيه مال، وعلى هذا فلا بد
And it is restricted by the command of the seller, i.e., his permission, because without his permission it does not give ownership. And permission (idhn) is mentioned instead of consent (rida) because it is not required in some of its individuals in the explicit statement. ... His statement: (Then I saw in al-Qunyah that bai' al-talji'ah is void) ... (And his statement: 'Because the statement belongs to the claimant of voidness' meaning if they differ regarding it and correctness, but if they differ regarding correctness and invalidity, then the chosen view is that the statement belongs to the claimant of invalidity as in Sharh al-Majma'. His statement: (Because ownership occurs without it) i.e., without possession, and better: because ownership occurs with it, i.e., with the offer. His statement: (Unless it is said that some sales etc.) He said in al-Nahr: I say: This is unnecessary, rather the invalid is more general as they committed in the beginning of the chapter. And then it is necessary to explicitly mention this contract to exclude the void, and this must be understood from their speech in this context. Then I saw in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyya: He said regarding the author of al-Hidayah's statement: 'The condition is that both countervalues are property to realize the pillar of sale' meaning to show its realization, for the invalid may also be used in the general sense of the void. And this matches what I understood, so be alert. On this, the commentator al-Zayla'i's statement that his saying 'in the invalid sale' is a precaution against the void is improper, because the void is only excluded by his saying 'both of whose countervalues are property' as you have learned. Al-Hamawi criticized him: Among the individuals of the void are those not excluded by this condition, such as the sale of wine and pork for dirhams, for it is void despite both countervalues being property, and on this it is necessary...
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This composite passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq covers multiple conditions for a fasid sale to confer ownership through qabd. First, the seller's permission (idhn) is required; without it, qabd does not give ownership. The author clarifies that idhn is broader than rida (consent) because in some cases idhn suffices even without explicit rida. The condition of both countervalues being property (mal) is also discussed: the author of al-Hidayah stated it as a condition for the pillar of sale, but some scholars like al-Zayla'i used the term 'fasid' broadly to mean 'batil'. Al-Hamawi objects that some void sales (like wine) have both countervalues as property, so the condition does not exclude all void cases. The text also revisits the talji'ah and hazl discussion to illustrate the dispute about who bears the burden of proof.

Key Terms
إذن (idhn): Permission: the seller's authorization for the buyer to take possession; broader than rida (consent).
مال (mal): Property: something of value that can be traded; both countervalues in a sale must be mal.
فاسد (fasid): Invalid: a sale that is valid in essence but defective in a description; distinct from batil (void).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is the condition of seller's permission (idhn) necessary for qabd to give ownership in a fasid sale?
A) Without idhn, the sale is automatically void
B) The buyer obtains ownership by force without idhn
C) Without idhn, the buyer only acquires possession but not ownership
D) Idhn is required for the offer to be valid

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, qabd confers ownership only if the seller gave permission and both countervalues are property.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
12 min
Ownership in a Fasid Sale: The Buyer Owns the 'Ayn
Explaining the extent of ownership in fasid sale and refuting the Iraqis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership in a fasid sale, distinguish it from the Iraqi view, and apply it to determine legal consequences (e.g., validity of freeing, shuf'a, theft).

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, the buyer owns the physical object upon possession, not just the right to dispose.

The Hanafi school classifies sales into sahih, fasid, and batil. One key difference is that in a fasid sale (e.g., when the price is not specified), the buyer acquires ownership of the physical object upon taking possession. However, some early Hanafis from Iraq argued that the buyer only acquires the right to dispose (tasarruf), not the 'ayn itself. This lesson examines the debate and establishes the accepted position with proofs.

Guided Path
1
Define fasid sale and its distinction from batil
Understand that a fasid sale is one deficient in a non-essential condition (e.g., unknown price). Unlike a batil sale, it can transfer ownership upon possession. Identify the requirement that both countervalues must be property (mal).
Fasid is between sahih and batil in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Identify the two competing intra-Hanafi views
The Iraqis (e.g., Abu Bakr al-Razi) held that the buyer only owns the right to dispose. The people of Balkh, following Imam Muhammad, held that the buyer owns the 'ayn (physical object).
3
Examine the proofs for the Balkh view (ownership of 'ayn)
Review the evidence from the source: the buyer can free the slave (wala goes to him), sell it and keep the price, has shuf'a rights for adjacent property, the seller is amputated if he steals it, the seller's freeing is invalid, the seller must observe istibra if the slave girl is returned, and the executor's fasid sale confirms ownership.
These are called 'fruits of ownership' (thamarāt al-milk).
4
Analyze the Iraqi counter-arguments and Hanafi responses
The Iraqis pointed out that the buyer cannot eat food, wear clothes, or have intercourse; 'aqr is due upon rescission; no shuf'a for the neighbor. The Hanafi response: prohibition of use does not negate ownership (e.g., foster sister owned but not permissible; profit of what is not guaranteed owned but not permissible). Shuf'a depends on the seller's right being cut off, not on buyer's ownership.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical implications
The Balkh view is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). Practical consequences: the buyer's freeing of a slave is valid, he can sell the item, he is not liable for theft by the seller during possession, etc.
This is the position in Zahir al-Riwayah.

From [that?]: like the sale of the coerced person, as is not hidden, and he made it absolute so it includes permission explicitly or by indication, so his silence at the buyer's possession in the session is permission by indication because the sale is an authorization. From it upon possession because he intended that the buyer own it, contrary to a valid sale because the offer is not an authorization because ownership occurs without it. And if they separate from the session, explicit permission is necessary, except if the seller receives the price and it is something he can own, then it is permission for possession by indication. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: if the buyer ordered the seller to perform an action on the sold item that decreases it or does not, like pressing and washing with or without wage, then what decreases is possession and what does not is not, and the seller has the wage in both cases whether the sold item perishes or not. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: if he [the buyer] freed [the slave] and the seller mixed it with the buyer's food by his order before possession, he becomes a possessor and owes the like. He restricted by his saying 'and each of its two countervalues is property' to exclude the sale of a dead animal and every void sale like sale with negation of price, for it is void, and with silence about it, it is fasid and he owns the sold item by possession, and there is no doubt that the void is excluded first by his saying 'in the fasid sale' so there is no need to exclude it a second time, unless it is said that some void sales are called fasid so it might be thought that the sold item in them is owned by possession, so he stated what excludes them. So if he sells a commodity for wine or a mudabbar or umm walad, he owns the commodity by possession, not its countervalue, although some called wine, mudabbar, and umm walad 'fasid sales', but he should have said 'valuable property'. It was mentioned in Idah al-Islah that there is no need for this restriction because the fasidness of the sale does not exist without this condition; it should not be said that it exists without it in case of sale with silence about mentioning the price because then one countervalue is the price which is mentioned virtually as stated in al-Dhakhira, and the condition is the existence of value in both countervalues. As restricted in al-Jawhara. And in his saying 'ownership by sale' is a refutation of those who say he only owns the right to dispose, not the 'ayn, and they are the Iraqis, and what he mentioned is the statement of the people of Balkh, and it is the text in the speech of Muhammad, and it is the correct and chosen opinion. And the fasidness: the chosen opinion is that the claim is for the one who claims fasid, as in Sharh Majma'. In his statement: (because ownership occurs without it) i.e. without possession, and the better is because ownership occurs by it i.e. by the offer. His statement: (unless it is said that some void sales...) He said in al-Nahr: I say: this is unnecessary, rather fasid is more general as they adopted at the beginning of the chapter, and then this restriction is necessary to exclude the void, and this must be understood from their words in this context. And whoever examines al-Hidaya and others finds it as if explicit. Then I saw it in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyya: he said in the author of al-Hidaya's statement 'condition that both countervalues are property to realize the rukn of sale' meaning to show its realization, for fasid is sometimes used in the general sense for void as well, and this matches what I understood, so pay attention to this. Thus the commentator al-Zayla'i's statement that his saying 'in the fasid sale' is a precaution against the void is improper, since the void is excluded by his saying 'and each of its two countervalues is property' as you have learned. Al-Hamawi objected that among the individuals of the void is what is not excluded by this restriction, namely the sale of wine and pig for dirhams, for it is void even though each countervalue is property, so this is necessary. As he said: indeed the buyer is a contestant against anyone who claims it because he owns its physical self, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn, by evidence that if the buyer frees it after possession it is valid and the wala is for him, and if he sells it the price is for him, and if a house adjacent to it is sold, the shuf'a is for the buyer, and if the seller frees it it does not free, and if the seller steals it from the buyer after possession he is amputated in al-Jawhara, so these are all fruits of ownership. And by evidence of the obligation of istibra on the seller if the slave girl is returned to him, and had she not left his ownership it would not be obligatory. And their statement that he only owns the right to dispose by the seller's authorization is rebutted by the case where the seller is the executor of an orphan who sold his slave fasidly and the buyer freed him, then it is valid. If it were on the basis of authorization, it would not be valid. As in Jami' al-Fusulayn. As for what the Iraqis used as evidence: the impermissibility of eating if it were food, impermissibility of wearing if it were a shirt, impermissibility of intercourse if she were a slave girl and istibra, and if he had intercourse the 'aqr is obligatory upon rescission, and lack of shuf'a for its shafi', this is not evidence because impermissibility does not indicate lack of ownership, as evidenced by the fact that profit of what is not guaranteed is owned but not permissible, and a foster sister if owned is not permissible to have intercourse with. And shuf'a is not obligatory because the seller's right has not been cut off from it, and it is only obligatory by the cutting off of his right, not by the buyer's ownership, as evidenced by the fact that if someone acknowledges the sale of his house and the purchaser denies, shuf'a becomes obligatory. This, and al-Amadi mentioned in his Fusul a difference of opinion regarding the prohibition of intercourse with her: it is said it is dislike (makruh) not haram, and it is said haram, and there is also an indication that the seller owns the price with the condition of possession because it is like the sold item as in al-Qunyah. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: if she became pregnant from him, she becomes umm walad and he owes her value, not her 'aqr, and it is said he owes her 'aqr and value, and it is said the buyer may perform every transaction in which permissibility applies, otherwise no, and direct consumption is not permitted, like juice in which a mouse fell, its sale is permissible but not its direct consumption like eating it. In al-Qunyah: the seller's freeing of the sold item after the buyer's possession without his presence is void, and in his presence is valid and is a rescission. This is a specification of their general statement that his freeing is void. In al-Zahiriyya: from the chapter of marriage of slave and slave girl: he sold a slave girl in a fasid sale and the buyer possessed her, then the seller married her, it is not permissible. If the buyer had not possessed her and the seller married her to the buyer, it is valid; as in al-Qunyah. I say: then what we quoted from al-Jawhara about cutting the seller's hand for stealing the sold item is problematic, because cutting requires that he have no ownership or ambiguity in it, and their statement about the impermissibility of her marriage to the seller requires the continuation of his ownership or ambiguity, so the seller should not be cut due to ambiguity. It was also mentioned in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and I did not see it from other than al-Haddadi. It seems he said it as a legal inference from himself, not as a transmission of the madhhab, for he said: among the benefits of his statement 'he owns it' is that if the seller steals it after the buyer's possession, he is amputated. And Allah knows best what is correct. From 'I say': it can be refuted that both are not absolute property, for the Shari'ah negated their value. His statement: (but he said in Jami' al-Fusulayn 'if he said...') He omitted what the understanding of the ruling depends on, and the text of his phrase is: if a slave, they mutually possess then the seller releases him from his value, then the slave dies, he owes his value; and if he said 'I release you from the slave'
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling of ownership in a fasid (invalid but not void) sale. The author begins by detailing how permission for the buyer to take possession can be explicit or implicit, such as the seller's silence in the session. For a fasid sale, the buyer's possession is key; without possession, ownership does not transfer. The passage then clarifies the requirement that both countervalues must be property (mal) to distinguish fasid from batil (void) sales. Void sales (e.g., selling a dead animal, wine, mudabbar, umm walad) do not transfer ownership even with possession, while fasid sales do. The author refutes the Iraqi opinion that the buyer only owns the right to dispose (tasarruf) but not the 'ayn (physical object). The correct Hanafi position, attributed to the people of Balkh and corroborated by Muhammad al-Shaybani's texts, is that the buyer owns the 'ayn itself. The evidence includes: the buyer can free the slave and the wala' (allegiance) goes to him; he can sell it; he has shuf'a rights; the seller's freeing after possession is invalid; the seller is liable for theft (hadd) if he steals it back; and the buyer's ownership is confirmed by the fact that if the seller repossesses the item upon rescission, he must observe istibra' (waiting period) for a slave girl, which would not be necessary if she had left his ownership. The Iraqis' counterarguments (e.g., the buyer cannot eat food, wear clothes, or have intercourse with a slave girl) are refuted by examples where ownership exists but certain uses are prohibited (like profit from what is not guaranteed, or a foster sister). Thus, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view is that the buyer owns the physical object in a fasid sale upon possession. The passage also discusses the seller's ownership of the price, which is conditional upon his possession. Additional furu' (branches) include cases of freeing, marriage, pregnancy, and theft, with some differences in transmission and opinion. The text ends with a note of caution regarding the amputation of the seller for theft, suggesting it may be a personal inference rather than a transmitted ruling.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is deficient in a condition that is not essential to the contract, such as silence about the price; it transfers ownership upon possession but is not valid without it.
ملك البيع (milk al-bay'): Ownership acquired through a sale contract; in fasid sale, it requires possession (qabd) and grants ownership of the 'ayn.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession or control of the sold item; in fasid sale, it is a condition for ownership to transfer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a consequence of the buyer owning the 'ayn in a fasid sale?
A) The buyer cannot sell the item until the contract is validated.
B) The buyer's freeing of a slave purchased in a fasid sale is invalid.
C) The seller is amputated if he steals the item from the buyer after possession.
D) The buyer must return the item immediately even without rescission.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, the buyer owns the physical object ('ayn) upon possession, not merely the right to dispose.
