Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Ownership Through Possession in a Fasid Sale
Explaining buyer's ownership through possession in a fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that a buyer acquires ownership of the specific item (raqabah) through valid possession in a fasid sale and distinguish this ownership from mere permissibility of disposal using the text's evidence.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, possession transfers ownership of the item itself, not just the right to use it.

The distinction between a void (bātil) and voidable (fāsid) sale is central to Hanafi fiqh. In a fasid sale, although the contract is defective, valid possession gives the buyer full ownership of the specific item, subject to the seller's right to rescind. This lesson unpacks the proofs from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq, showing how practical legal effects—freeing a slave, resale, pre-emption, even amputation for theft—demonstrate that the buyer is the true owner. We also address the Iraqis' objection that certain prohibitions (like eating food) negate ownership, and clarify the nuanced Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its practical consequences
The passage begins by asserting that the buyer 'owns the person' (raqabah) of the item. Note the list of consequences: freeing the slave is valid, proceeds of resale belong to buyer, pre-emption is established, seller's freeing is invalid, and seller is amputated for theft. These are called 'fruits of ownership' (thamarāt al-milk).
Each consequence alone might be explained away, but their convergence strengthens the proof.
2
Refute the objection from prohibitions
The Iraqis argued that because the buyer cannot eat the food, wear the garment, or have intercourse with the slave-girl, he cannot be the owner. The author rebuts by showing that ownership and permissibility are separate: profit on unguaranteed items is owned but impermissible, and a foster sister is owned but intercourse is prohibited.
The key is that prohibitions address permissibility, not ownership.
3
Understand the seller's rights and the valuation
The seller retains a right to demand return; if return becomes impossible (e.g., destruction), the buyer owes the value (qīmah). The text notes intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf: majority say value at time of possession; Imam Muhammad says at time of loss. The seller can also rescind by certain actions (e.g., receiving the item back via any transaction).
The value is not due unless return is impossible; the buyer is liable for the item as if it were a trust (amānah) before possession.
4
Apply the principles to resolve apparent contradictions
The author raises a tension: if the buyer truly owns the item, why is the seller's marriage to the slave-girl invalid (implying seller still has some right)? And why is amputation for theft upheld if the seller has a semblance (shubhah) of ownership? The resolution is that the buyer's ownership is complete for most purposes, but the seller retains a residual right that can affect certain rulings (like marriage). Amputation applies because the thief (seller) has no right at all after possession.
This shows the need to carefully apply the ownership concept to individual issues.

من غير بائعه أو باعه فوهبه ذلك الرجل من البائع الأول وسلمه لا يبرأ المشتري عن قيمته، ولم تعتبر العين واصلاً إلى البائع بالجهة المستحقة لما وصل من جهة أخرى، والمهر لو عيناً فوهبته من غير زوجها وهو وهبه إلى آخره. قوله : ( وفي بعض الحواشي إنما تجب قيمته إذا هلك قال الرملي : لا يذهب عليك أن مرادهم بالهلاك هنا الهلاك حقيقة أو ما في معناه من تعذر الرد وإلا يلزم الإصرار بالبائع حيث تعذر الرد لأنه لم يهلك حقيقة فلا تجب عليه قيمته، ولا يجوز رده مع التعذر وأمره بالتربص إلى الهلاك منافٍ
He said: The buyer is an opponent to whoever claims it because he owns its person; as in Jami' al-Fusulayn, evidenced by the fact that if the buyer frees it after taking possession, it is valid and the patronage belongs to him; if he sells it, the price belongs to him; if a house next to it is sold, pre-emption belongs to the buyer; if the seller frees it, it is not valid; if the seller steals it from the buyer after possession, he is punished by amputation according to al-Jawharah. These are all fruits of ownership. And by the evidence that istibra' is obligatory upon the seller if the slave-girl is returned to him; otherwise, it would not be obligatory. Their statement that he only has the right to dispose by the seller's permission is refuted by the case where the seller is an executor of an orphan who sold his slave invalidly and the buyer freed him, then it is valid. If it were by permission, it would not be valid; as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. As for what the Iraqis argued—that it is not permissible to eat if it is food, not permissible to wear if it is a shirt, not permissible to have intercourse if it is a slave-girl and to perform istibra', and if he has intercourse, the indemnity is obligatory upon rescission, and the lack of pre-emption for the one entitled—this is not proof because the lack of permissibility does not indicate lack of ownership, as evidenced by the profit on what is not guaranteed is owned but not permissible, and a foster sister if he owns her, intercourse is not permissible. Pre-emption is not obligatory because the seller's right is not severed from it, and it is only obligatory upon severance of his right, not due to the buyer's ownership, evidenced by the fact that if one confesses to selling his house and the buyer denies, pre-emption becomes obligatory. This has been mentioned by al-Imadi in his Fusul with a disagreement on the prohibition of intercourse: some say it is disliked but not prohibited, others say it is prohibited, and there is also an indication that the seller owns the price on condition of possession because it is like the sold item as in al-Qunyah. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: If she becomes pregnant from him, she becomes an umm walad, and he owes her value, not indemnity. Some say he owes indemnity and value. Some say the buyer may do any disposal that is permissible, otherwise not, and direct contact is not permissible, like juice in which a mouse fell: its sale is permissible but not direct consumption like eating. End. In al-Qunyah: The seller's freeing of the sold item after the buyer's possession without his presence is void, but in his presence it is valid and constitutes rescission. End. This is a specification of their statement that his freeing is void. In al-Zahiriyyah from the section on marriage of slave and slave-girl: He sold a slave-girl invalidly and the buyer took possession, then the seller married her: it is not valid. End. If the buyer had not taken possession and the seller married her to the buyer, it is valid; as in al-Qunyah. End. I say: This creates a problem with what we transmitted from al-Jawharah about cutting off the hand for theft of the sold item, because amputation requires that he has no ownership or semblance of it, and their statement about the invalidity of marriage to the seller implies the continuation of his ownership or semblance, so the seller should not be cut off due to the semblance. It has been mentioned in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj also, and I have not seen it from anyone other than al-Haddadi. Apparently, he said it as a juristic opinion from himself, not as a transmission of the madhhab, because he said: Among the benefits of his statement 'he owns it' is that if the seller steals it after the buyer's possession, he is cut off. And Allah knows best the truth.
Removing the restriction because it would imply that this type of void (sale) is fasid and owned by possession, but it is not so. End. I said: It can be rebutted that they are not absolute property because the Law has removed their value.
His statement: (But he said in Jami' al-Fusulayn: if he said ...) He omitted what is necessary to understand the ruling. The text is as follows: If a slave, they exchanged then he (the seller) released him from the value, then the slave dies, the value is due. If he said: I release you from the slave.
The qualification of ownership for the buyer in Fath al-Qadir is that there should be no option of stipulation, because ownership is prevented in a valid sale, so also in a fasid one. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: The option of stipulation and inspection are established in it. By 'value' in the author's words is meant the substitute for the sold item to include fungibles, because he owns it by its like, and value is only for non-fungibles. The statement in both is from the buyer with his oath because he denies liability, and the proof is on the seller; as in al-Jawharah. Since he made the value dependent on possession, it indicates that he meant he owns it by its value on the day of possession. If its value increases in his hand and he destroys it, it does not change like in usurpation. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Its value on the day he destroys it because it is determined by destruction; as in al-Kafi. But in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If the seller said 'I release you from the slave', then he dies with the buyer, he is released because the value is due upon destruction of the sold item, so before that the release is not valid. If he released him from the slave, then he has removed it from being guaranteed. Similarly, if a usurper releases from the value while the usurped item still exists, it is not valid; if he releases him from the usurped item, it is valid. End. Thus, the value is not due unless return to the seller becomes impossible due to death or other reasons. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: This is apparent from the texts of the companions. In some marginal notes: The value is only due if it perishes. End. As for the buyer depositing with the seller, it is not valid. He said in al-Qunyah: If one takes possession of cloth in a fasid sale by his order and cuts it, then deposits it with the seller and it perishes in his hand, it is from him, and the buyer owes the decrease from cutting. Every item sold in a fasid sale, if the buyer returns it to the seller by gift, charity, sale, or any means like deposit, lease, borrowing, usurpation, purchase, and it falls into the seller's hand, it is a cancellation of the sale and the buyer is free from liability. End. Similarly, if the seller's agent buys it, the buyer is free when he hands it over. Similarly, if he returns it to the seller as a pledge. Similarly, in a suspended sale: If one usurps a slave and sells him to a man, then the usurper buys him for less than he sold, it is a rescission of the first sale, and the increase is for the buyer, not for the usurper or the owner. From Muhammad: If he buys it with invalid dirhams, then sells it to his seller for dinars, it is a rescission if he takes possession, not before; as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. Then he said: The principle is that if an entitlement reaches the entitled person through another means, it is considered to have reached by an entitled means only if it reached from the obligated person. If it reaches from someone else, then not. Thus, if the buyer in a fasid sale gives it as a gift to someone other than his seller, or sells it, and that person gives it to the original seller, the buyer is not free from the value, and the item is not considered to have reached the seller by the entitled means because it came from another. Regarding the dower, if it is a specific item and she gives it as a gift to someone other than her husband, and he gives it to the husband, then he divorces her before consummation, the husband owes half the value of the item to her. If she gave it to her husband, he does not owe anything. End.
Up to here. His statement: (In some marginal notes: The value is only due if it perishes. Al-Ramli said: Do not be unaware that their intent by 'perished' here is actual destruction or its equivalent like impossibility of return; otherwise, it would require that the seller be obstinate when return is impossible, because it has not actually perished, so he does not owe its value, and returning it with impossibility is not permissible, and commanding him to wait until destruction is contradictory.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explains the legal effects of a fasid (voidable) sale after the buyer has taken possession. The author first establishes that the buyer acquires ownership of the specific item (raqabah) through valid possession in a fasid sale, not merely the right to dispose. He supports this with multiple practical consequences (thamarāt): the buyer can free the slave and the wala' belongs to him; the proceeds of resale are his; he has the right of pre-emption; the seller's freeing is invalid; and if the seller steals the item after possession, he is subject to amputation. These all indicate true ownership. The author then refutes the objection of the Iraqis who argued that certain prohibitions (like eating food, wearing a garment, or intercourse with a slave-girl) show lack of ownership. The response uses parallels: profit on what is not guaranteed is owned but impermissible, and a foster sister is owned but intercourse is prohibited. Pre-emption is not obligatory because the seller retains a right, not because the buyer lacks ownership. The passage then discusses the seller's ownership of the price, the istibra' obligation, and the seller's right to rescind by certain actions. The author notes a potential contradiction regarding amputation and marriage, suggesting the ruling on amputation may be a personal juristic opinion. Later, the text addresses the obligation of the value (qimah): it is due at the value on the day of possession, but Muhammad (al-Shaybani) says at the time of loss. The value is only due if return becomes impossible (e.g., destruction). Returning the item to the seller by any means (gift, sale, etc.) cancels the sale and frees the buyer from liability. The final sections discuss the principle that property returned to the seller must come from the obligated party to count as a valid return.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fāsid): A sale that is legally defective in its essence (e.g., missing a condition) but not void; it becomes effective upon possession and is subject to rescission.
ثمرات الملك (thamarāt al-milk): The practical legal consequences that flow from ownership, used here as circumstantial evidence (istidlāl bi al-āthār) to prove that the buyer is the owner.
القبض (al-qabd): Possession taken by the buyer with the seller's consent; it triggers ownership in a fasid sale and the buyer's liability for loss.
القيمة (al-qīmah): The monetary value of the item at a specified time (possession or loss), due to the seller if return becomes impossible.
الاستبراء (al-istibrā'): A waiting period to ensure a slave-girl is not pregnant before returning her to the seller; its obligation indicates the item was in the buyer's ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is evidence in the text that the buyer acquires ownership of the specific item in a fasid sale?
A) The buyer cannot eat the food if it is a food item.
B) The buyer's freeing of the slave after possession is valid and the wala' belongs to him.
C) The seller must perform istibra' upon return of the slave-girl.
D) The buyer cannot sell the item without the seller's permission.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, valid possession transfers ownership of the specific item to the buyer, evidenced by multiple legal consequences, and the seller's right is limited to demanding return or value if return is impossible.

Ownership and Value in Invalid Sale (Bay' Fasid)
Commentary on ownership and value in invalid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on ownership and value in an invalid sale, distinguish between fungible and specific goods, and apply the rule that value is assessed at time of receipt.

Core Idea
In invalid sale, the buyer owns the item at receipt but must return it or pay value assessed at time of receipt.

In an invalid sale, the buyer takes possession and becomes owner, but the sale is legally defective. The key obligation is to return the item or compensate if return is impossible. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains when value is determined and clarifies that value is only due when physical return becomes impossible, not immediately upon receipt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its trigger
The passage states that the buyer owns the item upon receipt (qabd) in an invalid sale. Note the condition: no option of stipulation if it prevents ownership.
Ownership in invalid sale is similar to valid sale but with restrictions.
2
Distinguish between mithli and qimi
For fungibles (mithli), the buyer owes a like item; for specific goods (qimi), he owes monetary value (qimah). The term 'value' in the text covers both.
This distinction affects the type of compensation.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on timing of value
Abu Hanifa holds that value is assessed at time of receipt; Muhammad holds at time of destruction. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's.
The passage cites sources for both views.
4
Determine when value becomes due
Value is not due immediately; it only becomes obligatory when physical return is impossible due to destruction or equivalent (e.g., death of a slave).
This accords with al-Ramli's clarification.
5
Apply to return scenarios
If the buyer returns the item to the seller by any means (gift, sale, etc.), the liability is discharged. If the item reaches the seller indirectly through a third party, discharge does not occur.
Considers various methods of return.

Chapter of Sale: Chapter of Invalid Sale. And the restriction of ownership for the buyer in Fath al-Qadir is that there is no option of stipulation in it, because ownership is prevented in a valid sale, so likewise in an invalid sale. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: The option of stipulation and inspection is established in it. And what is meant by 'value' in the author's speech is the substitute for the sold item, to include if it is a fungible, for he owns it by its like, and value is only in a specific item. The statement regarding both is the buyer's with his oath because he denies liability, and the evidence is for the seller; as in al-Jawharah. And since he placed value upon receipt, it indicates that his intent is that he owns it by its value on the day of receipt. If its value increased in his hand and he destroyed it, it does not change, like usurpation. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Its value is on the day he destroyed it, because it is determined by destruction; as in al-Kafi. But he said in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If the seller said 'I have discharged you from the slave' and then it died with the buyer, he is discharged, for the value is due by the destruction of the sold item, so before that discharge is not valid. But if he discharged him from the slave, he has removed it from being guaranteed. On this, if the usurper discharged from the value while the usurped object remains, it is not valid; if he discharged him from the usurped object, it is valid. End quote. Based on this, value is not due unless returning it to the seller becomes impossible due to death or otherwise. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: This is apparent from the texts of the companions. And in some footnotes: Value is only due when it perishes. End quote. As for the buyer's deposit with the seller, it is not valid. He said in al-Qunyah: The buyer's taking of coarse cloth in an invalid sale by his command, cutting it, then depositing it with the seller, and it perishes in his hand: it perishes from him, and the buyer owes the decrease from the cuts. And every sold item in an invalid sale that the buyer returns to the seller by gift, charity, sale, or any means like deposit, lease, loan, usurpation, purchase, and it comes into the seller's hand, it is an abandonment of the sale and the buyer is discharged from liability. End quote. Likewise, if the seller's agent bought it, the buyer is discharged if he hands it over to him. Likewise, if he returned it to the seller as a pledge. Likewise, in a suspended sale: if someone usurps a slave and sells him to a man, then the usurper buys him for less than what he sold, it is a rescission of the first sale, and the increase goes to the buyer, not the usurper nor the owner. From Muhammad: He bought it with corrupt dirhams, then sold it for dinars to his seller: it is a rescission if he takes possession, not before; as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. Then he said: The principle is that the entitled person, when it reaches him by one means, is only considered arriving by an entitled means if it reaches him from the one obligated. But if it reaches him from someone else, then no. So if the buyer in an invalid sale gives a gift to someone other than his seller, or sells it, then that man gives it as a gift to the first seller and hands it over, the buyer is not discharged from its value, and the item is not considered as having reached the seller by the entitled means because it came from another means. And regarding the dowry if it is a specific item: if she gives it as a gift to someone other than her husband, and he gives it as a gift to her husband, then he divorces her before consummation, the husband is entitled to half the value of the item from her; if she gave it as a gift to her husband, he does not recourse against her for anything. End quote. Up to here. His statement: (And in some footnotes, its value is only due when it perishes.) Al-Ramli said: Let it not escape you that their meaning by 'perish' here is literal perishing or what is in its meaning of impossibility of return; otherwise, it would entail insistence by the seller when return is impossible, so its value would not be due, and returning it with impossibility is not permissible, and ordering him to wait until perishing is contrary.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the chapter of invalid sale (bay' fasid). It begins by discussing the ownership of the buyer in an invalid sale: the buyer becomes owner upon receipt (qabd) but unlike a valid sale, ownership is subject to certain conditions. The commentary cites Fath al-Qadir, which restricts ownership such that there is no option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart) because such an option would prevent ownership even in a valid sale. Then it addresses the valuation: the term 'value' (qimah) in the author's speech means the substitute for the sold item, applying to fungibles (mithliyyat) where the buyer pays a similar item, and to specific goods (qimiyyat) where he pays monetary value. The determination of value is subject to dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that value is assessed at the time of receipt, while Muhammad says at the time of destruction. The passage then discusses scenarios of discharge from liability, return of the item, and the principle that if the item reaches the seller through an indirect chain, the buyer is not discharged. The final part clarifies that the obligation of value arises only when physical return is impossible due to destruction or its equivalent, as stated by al-Ramli. Throughout, the commentary relies on Hanafi sources (Fath al-Qadir, Jami' al-Fusulayn, al-Kafi, al-Jawharah, etc.) and presents the Hanafi position with its internal differences.

Key Terms
قيمة (qimah): Monetary compensation due in invalid sale when return of the item is impossible.
مثلي (mithli): Fungible goods that can be substituted by similar items (e.g., grains).
قيمي (qimi): Specific goods without standard market substitutes (e.g., animals, real estate).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when is the value of a specific good (qimi) assessed in an invalid sale?
A) At the time of the contract.
B) At the time of receipt (qabd).
C) At the time of destruction.
D) At the time of court judgement.

Key Takeaway
In an invalid sale, the buyer must return the item or pay its value assessed at the time of receipt; value becomes due only when return is impossible.

The Obligation to Rescind an Invalid Sale: Authority and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on rescission authority in invalid sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rescission of invalid sales, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding who may rescind, and evaluate the practical consequences of the different positions.

Core Idea
Rescinding an invalid sale is wajib, and both parties have the authority, but who may rescind alone depends on the type of invalidity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the right—and obligation—to rescind an invalid sale. It addresses a key grammatical nuance: the lam in 'li-kullin minhuma' is interpreted as 'upon' (alayhi) to establish obligation. The commentary then explores how this applies when the invalidity arises from a condition benefiting one party, highlighting a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. You will learn the fiqh ruling, the reasoning behind it, and the practical differences in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its linguistic basis
The matn states 'And each of them has the right to rescind it.' Determine the hukm level (permission or obligation) by analyzing the lam. Al-Zayla'i interprets it as alayhi (upon) making rescission wajib.
This step establishes that rescinding an invalid sale is not optional but obligatory.
2
Distinguish between types of invalidity
The passage differentiates invalidity in the core of the contract (e.g., riba, selling forbidden items) from invalidity due to an extra condition. For core invalidity, both parties have the right to rescind absolutely. For conditional invalidity, an intra-Hanafi dispute arises.
Understanding the type of invalidity is critical to applying the correct rule.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that both parties can rescind regardless of who benefits from the condition. Muhammad holds that only the party benefiting from the condition may rescind independently. Examine the reasoning: Muhammad argues that the benefiting party can fix the contract by dropping the condition, so if they rescind, they waive that right.
This step highlights the ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and practical effect
Al-Hidayah restricts to Muhammad's view, which becomes the relied-upon position. The practical consequence: if a sale is invalid due to a condition benefiting the buyer, only the buyer can unilaterally rescind; the seller cannot. Abu Yusuf's view gives both parties the right. Also note that knowledge of the other party is required according to the majority, but Abu Yusuf does not require it.
The mufta bihi is Muhammad's view in most later sources.

Chapter of Sale: Chapter of Invalid Sale. And each of them has the right to rescind it unless the buyer sells, gives as a gift, emancipates, or builds; and he may prevent the sold item from the seller. His statement: (And each of them has the right to rescind it) i.e., it is permissible for both the seller and the buyer in an invalid sale to rescind it to remove the invalidity. Al-Zayla'i mentioned that the lām is in the meaning of 'upon', because removing invalidity is obligatory upon both; there is no need for it because it is another ruling; he only intended to clarify that each has the authority of rescission to prevent the misunderstanding that if ownership is acquired by receipt, it becomes binding. If before receipt, each may do so with the knowledge of the other, not with his consent; if after receipt, then if the invalidity is in the core of the contract (pertaining to the two countervalues, e.g., selling one dirham for two, or selling wine or pork), then likewise. If it is due to an extra condition, like a sale with an unknown term or a condition for one of the parties, then likewise according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) due to non-bindingness; and according to Muhammad, it is for the one who benefits from the condition. Abu Yusuf did not require the knowledge of the other. Al-Hidayah restricted itself to Muhammad's statement and did not mention a difference. Know that his statement 'for the one who benefits from the condition' implies that the person who is the object of the contract (a human) may rescind if the condition is in his favor, as we mentioned, but it is far-fetched because they said each has the right to rescind, so reflect. In al-Qunyah: The buyer returned it due to invalidity of sale, and he (the seller) did not accept it, so the buyer returned it to his home, and it perished with him: he is not liable for the price nor the value. Ibn Salam restricted it to be [؟] benefit [؟] he is silent [؟] for the law, so the statement of obligation of value when return is impossible, whether by destruction or otherwise, became certain, as is apparent from their words, reflect. His statement: (and Al-Zayla'i mentioned that the lām is in the meaning of 'upon' etc.) Al-Nahr said: And each of them has the right to rescind it to remove invalidity; as in al-Hidayah. This implies that the obligatory statement is 'and upon each of them is its rescission', but he intended to clarify the establishment of the authority of rescission, so his reasoning is more specific than his claim; as in al-Fath. The commentator made the lām in the meaning of 'upon', from His statement 'and if you do evil, it is against you' (Quran 17:7). The author of al-Hidayah intended this meaning, so he gave the reasoning as you heard; thus the reasoning is not more specific than the claim. By this it is known that this assignment is necessary in the speech of al-Hidayah, and it is the preponderant in the speech of the author (al-Musannif). Because even if it is permissible that he intended to clarify the establishment of the authority of rescission, then it is deficient in conveying its obligation; on that assignment, the speech becomes beneficial for two matters, for obligation is an additional degree over the establishment of authority, so ponder it. His statement: (and know that his statement 'for the one who benefits from the condition' etc.) The origin is from Ibn al-Kamal who said in al-Islah: Another possibility remains here: that the invalidity is due to an extra condition and the one with the condition is other than the two contracting parties, and Qadikhan's portrayal of the issue in his Fatawa encompasses it. End quote. Al-Nahr said after mentioning what is in al-Hidayah: He reasoned it in al-Dhakhirah that he is able to drop the condition, so the contract becomes valid; so if he rescinds, he has nullified his right, because he has the ability to correct the contract; and when the contract is non-binding, each of them can rescind it. End quote. This indicates that the benefit that necessitates independent rescission is particular to the two contracting parties. End quote. His statement: (Then the buyer returned it to his home etc.) He said in al-Khaniyyah in the chapter on what removes liability in invalid and disliked sales, verbatim: The buyer in an invalid purchase, if he brings the sold item to the seller and the seller does not accept it, and the buyer returns it to his home and it perishes, he is not liable. But if the buyer placed it in front of the seller or the usurped-from person and he did not accept it, then he carried it to his home and it perished, he is liable. In usurpation and invalid sale. And some said: If the invalidity of the sale is undisputed, the answer is the same; if it is disputed, and he brought it to the seller...
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn 'And each of them has the right to rescind it...' regarding invalid sale. The commentator opens by explaining the right of both parties to rescind the invalid sale to remove its invalidity. He then discusses the grammatical interpretation of the lam in 'lahu' – Al-Zayla'i states it means 'upon' (obligation), not 'for' (permission), as removing invalidity is wajib. The passage then distinguishes between invalidity in the core of the contract (like riba or selling forbidden items) where the right to rescind exists absolutely, and invalidity due to an extra condition (like unknown term), where according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad the right is the same, but Muhammad restricts it to the party benefiting from the condition. Abu Yusuf does not require the other's knowledge. The commentary then addresses a potential misunderstanding: if the buyer returns the item and the seller refuses, and the buyer takes it home and it perishes, he is not liable. Ibn Salam restricts this to cases where the invalidity is textually clear; if disputed, the liability differs. The passage ends with a discussion on the obligation of value when return is impossible, and the authority of rescission being specific to the contracting parties. Throughout, the commentary is based on Hanafi sources like Al-Hidayah, Al-Fath, Al-Zayla'i's commentary, Al-Nahr, Al-Dhakhirah, and Al-Khaniyyah.

Key Terms
ولاية الفسخ (wilayat al-faskh): The legal authority of each contracting party to rescind an invalid sale unilaterally.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission: the act of nullifying a contract retroactively, as if it never existed.
لمن له منفعة الشرط (li-man lahu manfa'at al-shart): The party who benefits from a stipulation that makes the sale invalid (e.g., a condition for a third party).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Zayla'i and the preponderant view in al-Hidayah, what does the lam in 'li-kullin minhuma faskhuhu' indicate?
A) Permission (ibahah) to rescind.
B) Obligation (wujub) to rescind.
C) Emphasis only.
D) It indicates that only one party can rescind.

Key Takeaway
Rescinding an invalid sale is wajib on both parties, and the authority to do so belongs to both, but when invalidity is due to a condition, only the benefiting party can rescind unilaterally according to the relied-upon view.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Rulings on Void Sale: Right to Rescind and Exceptions
Commentary on the initial ruling: each party may rescind, exceptions, and intra-Hanafi differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the nature of the right to rescind an invalid sale (bay' fasid) in the Hanafi school, identify the intra-madhhab differences, and apply the exceptions to the right of rescission.

Core Idea
The right to rescind an invalid sale is a permission, not an obligation — but certain actions of the buyer can prevent it.

In the Hanafi school, a sale that is fasid (defective in description) can be rectified by rescission (faskh). The matn states that each party has the right to rescind, with exceptions if the buyer sells, gifts, manumits, or builds. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq unpacks the nature of this right, the debate over whether it is wajib or ja'iz, and the intra-Hanafi differences on who may rescind when the defect is due to an extra condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is rescission (faskh) of an invalid sale (bay' fasid). According to the matn, each party (buyer and seller) has the authority to rescind. Note that the ruling on the rescission itself is debated: is it a duty (wajib) or a right (ja'iz)?
The lam in 'lahu' may indicate obligation, but the author of al-Hidayah intended to establish authority, not obligation.
2
Analyze the lam vs 'ala debate
Al-Zayla'i interpreted the lam in 'lahu' as meaning 'ala (upon), implying that rescission is wajib because removing corruption is wajib. The commentator of al-Bahr al-Raiq argues that the author of al-Hidayah only meant to affirm the authority (wilaya) of rescission, not its obligation. The obligation to remove corruption can be fulfilled by other means, such as mutual agreement or judicial intervention.
This debate affects the legal status: if wajib, parties sin if they delay; if ja'iz, they may wait but the judge can force them.
3
Examine intra-Hanafi differences
When the corruption is due to an extra condition (e.g., selling with an unknown term), there is a disagreement: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad both allow each party to rescind, but Muhammad adds that the party benefiting from the condition can rescind independently. Abu Hanifa's view is not explicit here, but al-Hidayah prefers Muhammad's position.
The practical effect: the one benefiting from the condition can unilaterally rescind, while the other party also retains the right.
4
Recognize the exceptions
The right of rescission is lost if the buyer (after taking possession) sells the item with a valid sale, gifts it, manumits a slave, or builds on the land. This is because the buyer's action transfers ownership or creates a right for a third party, which takes precedence over the right of the Law (haqq al-shar') that necessitates rescission.
The exception applies only to valid dispositions; an invalid sale by the buyer does not prevent rescission.
5
Understand the practical consequences
The passage also discusses scenarios: if the buyer returns the item and the seller refuses, liability for loss depends on whether the invalidity is agreed upon. The judge may rescind the sale if both parties persist, without requiring a judicial decree. Additionally, the buyer has priority over other creditors if the seller dies, and vice versa.
These details show the nuanced application of the right of rescission in various situations.

Book of Sale, Chapter of Invalid Sale. And each of them has the right to rescind it, except if the buyer sells, gifts, manumits, or builds, and he has the right to prevent the sold item from the seller. His saying: (And each of them has the right to rescind it) means that it is permissible for each of the seller and buyer in an invalid sale to rescind it to remove the corruption. Al-Zayla'i mentioned that the lam is in the meaning of 'upon', because removing corruption is obligatory upon them, and there is no need for it because it is a different ruling. Rather, his intent is to state that each of them has the authority of rescission to dispel the assumption that if ownership is established by taking possession, the sale becomes binding. So if it is before taking possession, then each has that with the knowledge of the other, not with his consent. And if it is after taking possession: if the corruption is in the substance of the contract, such that it pertains to the two compensations, the sold item and the price, like selling a dirham for two dirhams, or selling for wine or swine, so likewise. And if it is due to an extra condition, like selling with an unknown term or a condition therein for one of them, so likewise according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) due to lack of bindingness, and according to Muhammad it is for the one who benefits from the condition. Abu Yusuf did not require the knowledge of the other. Al-Hidayah confined itself to the statement of Muhammad and did not mention a disagreement. And know that his statement 'for the one who benefits from the condition' necessitates that the person contracted with (the human) may rescind if the condition is for him, as we previously stated, and that is far because their statement is 'each of them has the right to rescind', so reflect. And in al-Qunyah: The buyer returns it due to the invalidity of the sale, and he (the seller) does not accept it, so the buyer returns it to his home and it perishes with him, the price and value are not incumbent upon him. Ibn Salam restricted it by saying that the corruption must be [such that the benefit is silent regarding the Law?] so the statement of obligation of value when return is impossible, either by destruction or otherwise, is determined, as is apparent from their words, reflect. His saying: (And al-Zayla'i mentioned that the lam is in the meaning of 'upon' etc.) He said in al-Nahr: And each of them has the right to rescind to remove the corruption; thus in al-Hidayah. This necessitates that the obligatory is to say 'and upon each of them is its rescission' except that he intended to state the establishment of the authority of rescission, so his reasoning became more specific than his claim; thus in al-Fath, and the commentator made the lam in the meaning of 'upon', and from it (the Quranic verse) 'And if you do evil, it is against yourselves' [Isra 17:7]. And the author of al-Hidayah intended this meaning, so he reasoned as you heard, and thus the reasoning is not more specific than the claim, and by this it is known that this assignment is necessary in the text of al-Hidayah, and it is the more correct in the speech of the author because even if it is permissible that he intended to state the establishment of the authority of rescission, his statement at that time would be lacking in expressing its obligation, and upon that assignment, the speech becomes beneficial for two things, as obligation is an addition over the establishment of authority, so reflect. His saying: (And know that his statement 'for the one who benefits from the condition' etc.) Its origin is from Ibn al-Kamal, where he said in al-Islah: There remains here another possibility: that the corruption is due to an extra condition and the one to whom the condition belongs is not one of the two contractors, and the depiction of Qadikhan in his Fatawa encompasses it. And he said in al-Nahr after mentioning what is in al-Hidayah: And he reasoned in al-Dhakhirah that he is able to drop the condition, so the contract becomes valid, so if he rescinds, he has nullified his right due to his ability to correct the contract, and when the contract is not binding, each can rescind it. And this indicates that the benefit that necessitates independence in rescission is specific to the two contractors. His saying: (So he returned it to his home etc.) He said in al-Khaniyyah in the chapter on what removes liability in an invalid and disliked sale, its text: The buyer bought invalidly; when he brings the sold item to the seller and the seller does not accept it, and the buyer returns it to his home and it perishes, he is not liable. But if the buyer placed it before the seller or the person from whom it was usurped and he did not accept it, then he carried it to his home and it perished, he would be liable — in usurpation and invalid sale. And some said: If the invalidity of the sale is not disputed, then the answer is likewise; and if it is disputed and he brought it to the seller, the sale is agreed upon; if it is disputed, he is not exempt except by his acceptance or by the judge's ruling. And Abu Bakr al-Isfaf said: He is exempt in both cases, and what Ibn Salam said is more appropriate, like the option of puberty and rescission of lease due to an excuse. And in it: They made an invalid transaction, then one of them died, so if his inheritance is canceled? And in al-Bazzaziyyah: He sold from him validly, then he sold from him invalidly, the first is rescinded because if the second were valid, it would rescind the first, so likewise if it is invalid, because it is attached to the valid in many rulings. Likewise, if the lessor sold to the lessee from the lessee invalidly, the lease is rescinded as if he sold it validly. Then he said: And if he sold invalidly and delivered, then sold to another and claimed that the second was before the rescission of the first and its possession, and the second buyer claimed that it was after the rescission and possession in the first, the statement is his (the second buyer's), not the seller's, and the first is rescinded by the possession of the second. Then he said: If the seller dies and he has another debt, the buyer has more right to it than the creditors, as in the valid (sale) after rescission; and if the buyer dies, the seller has more right than all other creditors to its value. Then he said: The judge's ruling is not required for rescission of an invalid sale. And the author did not mention that the judge rescinds the invalid forcibly on both. He said in al-Bazzaziyyah: When the seller and buyer persist in holding the sold item invalidly and the judge knows of it, he has the right to rescind it as a right of the Law, so by any means the buyer returns the sold item to the seller, he becomes a leaver of the sold item and is freed from liability. His saying: (Except if the buyer sells) meaning each of them does not have the right to rescind, and rather his sale is effective because he owns it with the right of disposal of it, and the right of recovery falls due to the attachment of the right of the servant (the second buyer) to it, and the nullification of the first was only due to the right of the Law, and the right of the servant takes precedence due to his need. And because the first is legislated in its origin without its description, and the second is legislated in its origin and description, so mere description does not oppose it. And because it occurred by authorization from the seller, unlike the buyer's disposal in the house subject to preemption, because each of them (the buyer and the preemptor) has a right of the servant, so they are equal in legislation and it did not occur by authorization from the preemptor. He intended by 'sell' a valid sale, because if he sells it invalidly, it does not prevent rescission. And he made it unrestricted, so it includes when the second buyer took possession first, but it is restricted to when there is no option of condition in it, because it is not binding. And in al-Bazzaziyyah and Jami' al-Fusulayn: The buyer establishes evidence of his sale to so-and-so who is absent, it is not accepted; so the seller takes it, but if he (the buyer) confirms him (the absent buyer), then he has its value. And if the sale is rescinded after his possession by a judge's ruling, then the seller has the right of rescission if the judge did not rule for its value, due to the removal of the impediment. And if he returns it due to a defect without a judge's ruling, the right of rescission does not return, as if...
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (sharh) on the initial ruling of the matn regarding the invalid sale (al-bay' al-fasid) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The matn states: 'And each of them has the right to rescind it, except if the buyer sells, gifts, manumits, or builds, and he has the right to prevent the sold item from the seller.' The commentary expands on several aspects. First, it clarifies that 'each of them' means both the buyer and seller in an invalid sale have the authority to rescind the contract to remove the corruption. Al-Zayla'i noted that the Arabic particle 'lam' in 'lahu' (for him) is actually in the meaning of 'ala (upon), implying obligation, because removing corruption is obligatory. However, the author of al-Hidayah intended to establish the existence of the authority of rescission, not necessarily obligation. The commentary then discusses the difference between pre- and post-possession rescission. If the corruption is in the substance of the contract (like selling a dirham for two dirhams or for wine), the right to rescind remains. If the corruption is due to an extra condition (like an unknown term or a condition for one party), there is a dispute: According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), both parties can rescind because the contract is not binding. Muhammad adds that the party benefiting from the condition can also rescind. Abu Hanifa's view is not explicitly stated here, but the commentary refers to a difference. The relied-upon position is Muhammad's, as in al-Hidayah. The commentary also delves into a scenario where the buyer returns the item after the sale is found invalid, but the seller refuses, and the item perishes. The ruling on liability varies based on whether the invalidity is agreed upon or disputed. Multiple authorities are cited, including al-Qunyah, al-Khaniyyah, al-Bazzaziyyah, and Qadikhan. The exception to the general right of rescission is then explained: if the buyer sells the item to a third party (with a valid sale), or gifts it, manumits a slave, or builds on the land, then the right of rescission is lost because the buyer's ownership has been transferred or modified, and the third party's right (haqq al-'abd) takes precedence over the right of the Law (haqq al-shar') that necessitates rescission. The commentary clarifies that this exception applies only to valid dispositions; if the buyer sells invalidly, the rescission is not prevented. It also discusses special cases: if the buyer dies, the seller has priority over other creditors for the value of the item; if the seller dies, the buyer has priority. The judge's involvement is discussed: no judicial decree is required for rescission, but if the judge learns of the continuance of the invalid sale, he can rescind it as a right of the Law. The passage is rich in intra-Hanafi differences and practical applications of the rules of invalid sales.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is valid in its essence (asl) but defective in its description (wasf), such as selling with an unknown term or selling for something illegal. It requires rescission to remove the defect.
فسخ (faskh): The act of annulling or rescinding a contract to remove its invalidating defect. In this context, it is a unilateral right (for each party) to terminate the fasid sale.
شرط (shart): A condition inserted into the contract that may make it fasid if it is not in conformity with the Law, such as a condition benefiting one party without countervalue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In an invalid sale (bay' fasid), which action by the buyer will prevent the seller's right to rescind?
A) The buyer sells the item to another person with a valid sale.
B) The buyer returns the item to the seller before delivery.
C) The buyer pays the price to the seller.
D) The buyer requests the seller to cancel the sale.

Key Takeaway
The right to rescind an invalid sale is a permission for each party, but it can be lost if the buyer transfers ownership by valid sale, gift, manumission, or building.

Rulings on Fāsid Sales: Priority, Annulment, and the Qadi's Role
Subsidiary rulings on fāsid sales from al-Bazzāziyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the effects of a fāsid sale, including the need for qadi's judgment, priority rights upon death, and analogical annulment.

Core Idea
A fāsid sale is not void; it has legal consequences, including priority rights and the need for annulment.

This passage from al-Bazzāziyya presents several subsidiary rulings on fāsid (corrupted) sales. Understanding these rulings is crucial for distinguishing between void and voidable transactions in Hanafi fiqh and for resolving disputes arising from defective sales. The rulings cover who has the right to annul, what happens when one party dies, and the qadi's authority.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish agreed vs. disputed sales
Identify the first ruling: a sale that is mutually agreed upon (muttafaq) is distinguished from a disputed one. For a disputed sale, absolution requires the buyer's acceptance or a qadi's judgment. Note the ikhtilaf with Abu Bakr al-Iskāf who held that absolution occurs in both cases without qadi.
This sets the stage for understanding when qadi intervention is needed.
2
Analyze the analogy of annulment
Examine the analogy (qiyās) that if a valid second sale would annul a first valid sale, then a fāsid second sale also annuls the first. Similarly, a fāsid sale by a lessor to the lessee annuls the lease. Understand the principle: fāsid is attached to valid in many rulings.
This shows how Hanafi fiqh extends rules from valid to fāsid by similarity.
3
Examine priority rules upon death
Review the two death scenarios: (a) If the seller dies with other debts, the buyer in a fāsid sale has priority over creditors for the item. (b) If the buyer dies, the seller has priority over creditors for the price. Understand that fāsid sale creates a right akin to ownership until annulled.
Priority is given to the innocent party who has a claim on the specific item or price.
4
Understand the qadi's authority
Note that a qadi's judgment is not required to annul a fāsid sale; the parties can annul it themselves. However, if they persist, the qadi may annul it by force as a right of the Sharia. The buyer becomes free of liability once he returns the item.
This clarifies the qadi's role: permissive, not mandatory, but with coercive power if needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب البيع الفاسد البيع متفقاً عليه فإن كان مختلفاً فيه لا يبرأ إلا بقبوله أو قضاء القاضي. وقال أبو بكر الإسكاف : يبرأ في الوجهين وما قاله ابن سلام أشبه كخيار البلوغ وفسخ الإجازة للعذر اهـ. وفيها : تبايعا فاسداً ثم مات أحدهما فلو رثته النقض .اهـ. وفي البزازية : باع منه صحيحاً ثم باعه فاسداً منه انفسخ الأول لأن الثاني لو كان صحيحاً ينفسخ الأول به فكذا لو كان فاسداً لأنه ملحق بالصحيح في كثير من الأحكام، وكذا لو باع المؤجر المستأجر من المستأجر فاسداً تنفسخ الإجارة كما إذا باعه صحيحاً .اهـ. ثم قال ولو باع فاسداً وسلم ثم باع من غيره وادعى أن الثاني كان قبل فسخ الأول وقبضه وزعم المشتري الثاني أنه كان بعد الفسخ والقبض في الأول فالقول له لا للبائع وينفسخ الأول بقبض الثاني ثم قال : لو مات البائع وعليه دين آخر فالمشتري أحق به من الغرماء كما في الصحيح بعد الفسخ، ولو مات المشتري فالبائع أحق من سائر الغرماء بماليته اهـ. ثم قال : ولا يشترط القضاء في فسخ البيع الفاسد اهـ. ولم يذكر المصنف أن القاضي فسخ الفاسد جبراً عليهما. قال في البزازية : وإذا أصر البائع والمشتري على إمساك المشتري فاسداً وعلم به القاضي له فسخه حقاً للشرع فبأي طريق رده المشتري إلى البائع صار تاركاً للمبيع وبرئ عن ضمانه اهـ .
Book of Sales, Chapter of Fāsid Sale. The sale which is agreed upon – if it is disputed, he is not absolved except by his acceptance or the qadi’s judgment. Abu Bakr al-Iskāf said: he is absolved in both cases, and what Ibn Sallām said is more appropriate, like the option of puberty and the annulment of permission due to an excuse, end. In it: They traded fāsidly then one of them died – would they inherit the annulment? End. And in al-Bazzāziyya: He sold validly then sold it fāsidly from him – the first is annulled because if the second were valid it would annul the first, so too if it was fāsid because it is attached to the valid in many rulings. Similarly, if a lessor sold to the lessee fāsidly, the lease is annulled just as if he sold it validly, end. Then he said: And if he sold fāsidly and delivered, then sold to another and claimed that the second was before the annulment of the first and its possession, and the second buyer claimed it was after the annulment and possession of the first – the word is his, not the seller’s, and the first is annulled by the second’s possession. Then he said: If the seller dies and he has another debt, the buyer has more right to it than the creditors, just as in the valid after annulment. And if the buyer dies, the seller has more right than the other creditors to its price, end. Then he said: Qadi’s judgment is not required in annulling a fāsid sale, end. And the author did not mention that the qadi annuls the fāsid compulsorily upon both. It is said in al-Bazzāziyya: If the seller and buyer persist in holding a fāsid purchase and the qadi learns of it, he may annul it as a right of the Sharia; by whichever way the buyer returns it to the seller, he becomes a deserter of the sold item and is free of its liability, end.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of several subsidiary rulings regarding fāsid (corrupted) sales from earlier Hanafi authorities, particularly al-Bazzāziyya. It begins by stating that a sale agreed upon (muttafaq) is distinguished from a disputed one where the qadi's judgment is needed. Abu Bakr al-Iskāf and Ibn Sallām are cited on the conditions for absolution. Then it addresses specific scenarios: if both parties engage in a fāsid sale and one dies, the ruling on inheriting the right to annul; if a seller makes a valid sale and then a fāsid sale of the same item to the same person, the valid sale is annulled by analogy (qiyās) since a valid second sale would annul the first; similarly for a lessor selling to a lessee fāsidly. Next, the rule that if the seller sells fāsidly and delivers, then sells to another and there is a dispute over whether the second sale occurred before or after annulment of the first, the second buyer’s word is taken. Regarding death: if the seller dies with other debts, the fāsid buyer has priority over creditors; if the buyer dies, the seller has priority over other creditors for the price. Finally, it clarifies that qadi’s judgment is not required to annul a fāsid sale, but if the parties persist, the qadi may annul it. All rulings here are from the Hanafi school, drawing on the authoritative books of the madhhab.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bayʿ al-fāsid): A sale that is valid in its essence (asl) but defective in its description (wasf), such as a sale with an unknown price or without delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a seller sells fāsidly and delivers, then sells the same item to another buyer, and the seller and second buyer dispute whether the second sale was before or after annulment of the first?
A) The seller's word is taken.
B) The second buyer's word is taken.
C) The first buyer's word is taken.
D) The qadi decides.

Key Takeaway
A fāsid sale has legal effects: the buyer's priority upon the seller's death, the seller's priority upon the buyer's death, and the qadi's authority to compel annulment if parties persist.

The Fāsid Buyer's Valid Sale Annuls the Original Seller's Right of Reclaim
Expansion of the rule: buyer's sale in fāsid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that a fāsid buyer's valid sale to a third party annuls the original sale and cuts off the original seller's right of reclaim, and identify the underlying qiyas and the principle of haqq al-ʿabd.

Core Idea
A fāsid buyer's subsequent valid sale defeats the original seller's reclaim because the second buyer's individual right takes precedence.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq, the author expands on the matn phrase 'إلا أن يبيع المشتري' (unless the buyer sells). He explains that if the buyer in a fāsid sale sells the item to a third party with a valid sale, the original seller loses his right to reclaim the item, and the first fāsid sale is effectively annulled. The reasoning combines a qiyas from a valid second sale and the prioritization of the second buyer's right (haqq al-ʿabd) over the Sharia's right to annul. We will walk through the ruling, its proof, and important exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Locate the act being ruled upon: the fāsid buyer sells the item to a third party with a valid sale. The ruling: the original seller has no right to annul, the second sale is effective, and the right of reclaim falls.
The ruling is positive: the second sale is valid and binding.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
Find the textual proof. The author uses qiyas: just as a valid second sale annuls the first, so does a fāsid second sale, because the fāsid is attached to the valid in many rulings. This is supported by the principle of haqq al-ʿabd: the second buyer's right takes precedence.
The proof is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala.
3
Analyze the qiyas and the underlying illa
Break down the analogy: asl = a valid second sale (annuls first), far' = a fāsid second sale, illa = being a sale that effects ownership even if defective. The Hanafi response to objections is that the fāsid is attached to the valid in many rulings, and the seller's right is not absolute.
The qiyas is jaliyyah (clear analogy).
4
Note the exceptions and conditions
The text mentions exceptions: (1) if the buyer sells back to the original seller, it is a return/annulment; (2) if the sale was due to coercion (ikrāh), all buyer's acts are annulled. Also, if the sale includes a khiyar shart (option), it does not prevent annulment until the option is exercised. The principle of takhliya as possession is also relevant.
These exceptions show the boundaries of the ruling.
5
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and tarjih
There is ikhtilaf on whether takhliya (vacating) counts as possession. Qādīkhān and the author of al-Khulāsa say yes; others say no. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is Qādīkhān's correction because he is 'faqīh al-nafs'.
This tarjih strengthens the ruling in practice.

ستأتي المسألة أيضاً في القولة الثانية. قوله : ولو مات المشتري فالبائع أحق) قال أبو السعود في حاشية مسكين : قيده شيخنا عن شيخه الشيخ شاهين بما إذا مات قبل القبض، اشتراه ثانياً وسيأتي في الضابط. وقيد His statement: (Unless the buyer sells) – meaning neither of them has the right to annul, and his sale is executed because he owns it by the right to dispose of it, and the right of reclaim falls due to the right of the servant (buyer) being attached to the second; the annulment of the first was only due to the right of the Sharia, and the right of the servant is prioritized because of his need. And because the first (sale) is lawful in its essence without its description, and the second is lawful in its essence and its description, so mere description does not oppose it. And because it occurred with authorization from the seller, unlike the buyer's disposal in the pre-empted house because each is a right of the servant, so they are equal in lawfulness and did not occur with authorization from the pre-emptor. He meant by the valid sale because if he sold it fāsidly, it does not prevent annulment. He made it absolute, so it includes if the second buyer took possession first, but it is restricted to when there is no option of stipulation because it is not binding. In al-Bazzāziyya and Jāmiʿ al-Fuṣūlayn: The buyer produced evidence of his sale to an absent person – it is not accepted; the seller may take it, but if he (seller) confirms him, then he has its value, end. And if he annulled the sale after possession by judgment, the seller has the right of annulment if he did not judge its value due to the removal of the impediment. And if he returned it due to a defect without judgment, the right of annulment does not return, as if the seller did not accept it and he returned it to his house and it perished – he is not free from liability. The correct view is that he is freed in both cases except if he placed it before him and he did not accept and he took it to his house and it perished, then he is liable because he becomes a usurper anew, end. And it is established that the correction of Qādīkhān is given precedence over others because he is a jurist of soul, and it is based on that takhliya (vacating) is possession, and it passed at the beginning of the chapter the difference in correction about that, and that Qādīkhān and the author of al-Khulāsa correctly held it is possession. His statement: (Then he said, the issue will also come in the second statement. His statement: And if the buyer dies, the seller has more right) – Abū al-Saʿūd in Hāshiyat Miskīn said: Our teacher restricted it based on his teacher al-Shaykh Shāhīn to when he died before possession, he bought it second, and it will come in the rule. And restricted by the sale of the buyer because if the seller sold it after the buyer's possession and claimed that the second was before the annulment of the first and its possession, and the second buyer claimed it was after the annulment and possession from the first, the word is his, not the seller's, and the first is annulled by the second's possession; thus in al-Bazzāziyya. And there are two exceptions to its binding by sale: first, if he sold it to his seller, we stated that it is a return and annulment of the sale. Second, if it was fāsid due to coercion, then all the buyer's acts are annulled, contrary to other fāsid sales; thus in al-Bazzāziyya. He restricted by the fāsid sale to exclude the fāsid lease, as in Jāmiʿ al-Fuṣūlayn: It was said that the lessee of a fāsid lease may not lease it to another with a valid lease, by way of inference from what was mentioned, etc. And it was said he owns it after possession like a fāsid purchaser who may sell validly, and that is correct except that the first lessor may annul the second because it is annulled by excuses.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn phrase 'إلا أن يبيع المشتري' (unless the buyer sells). It explains that if the buyer of a fāsid sale sells the item to a third party, the original seller loses his right to reclaim the item, and the first sale is effectively annulled. The reasoning is that the second buyer acquires a valid ownership right (haqq al-ʿabd) which takes priority over the Sharia's right to annul the fāsid sale. The seller's authorization is also considered. The commentary then contrasts with the case of pre-emption (shufʿa) where the pre-emptor's right is not subject to the buyer's sale. It also addresses various subsidiary issues: if the buyer sells with an option (khiyār shart), it does not prevent annulment until the option is exercised; if the buyer claims to have sold to an absent person, his word is not accepted without evidence; if the qadi annuls the sale after possession, the seller's right to annul revives; if the buyer returns the item due to a defect without qadi judgment, the right does not return. The discussion then moves to the death of the buyer: if he dies before possession, the seller's priority is affected. Finally, exceptions to the rule that the buyer's sale prevents annulment are given: (1) if the buyer sells back to the original seller, it is considered a return and annulment; (2) if the sale was due to coercion (ikrāh), all the buyer's acts are annulled. The passage ends by distinguishing fāsid lease from fāsid sale: the lessee in a fāsid lease may not sublease validly, though there is dispute, and the correct view is that he may, but the original lessor can annul the sublease if an excuse arises.

Key Terms
حق العبد (haqq al-ʿabd): The right of an individual (servant) as opposed to the right of God; here, the second buyer's right to the item he purchased.
التخلية قبض (al-takhliyah qabd): The principle that vacating the item (takhliya) by the seller constitutes possession (qabd) for the buyer.
خيار شرط (khiyār sharṭ): An option stipulated in a sale contract allowing one or both parties to confirm or rescind the sale within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) If the second sale is also fāsid.
B) If the second sale includes a khiyar shart.
C) If the buyer sells back to the original seller.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
A fāsid buyer's subsequent valid and binding sale to a third party annuls the original fāsid sale and cuts off the original seller's right of reclaim, prioritizing the second buyer's individual right.

Gift in Fāsid Sale and Purity of Ownership
Explains gift effect in fāsid sale and purity vs. war booty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the effect of a gift in a fāsid sale and distinguish between the purity of ownership in fāsid sale and war booty.

Core Idea
A gift from the fāsid buyer removes annulment, but ownership remains impure; war booty from betrayal never becomes pure.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq delves into two interrelated issues: first, the effect of a gift (hiba) by the buyer of a fāsid sale—how it lifts the seller's right to annul, provided the gift is delivered; second, the concept of 'purity' (tayyib) in ownership and how it applies differently to fāsid acquisitions versus war booty taken in betrayal of trust. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping Hanafi rulings on the permissibility of using fāsidly acquired property and the obligation to return war booty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the effect when the buyer of a fāsid sale gives the item as a gift? The passage states the corruption is removed and the original seller cannot annul the sale.
This is the core ruling on the gift.
2
Locate the condition
Al-Hidāya requires delivery (taslīm) for the gift to be effective, because a gift only transfers ownership through possession. Without delivery, the gift is incomplete.
Compare with sale where ownership transfers by contract alone.
3
Analyze the concept of purity
The fāsid buyer's ownership is not pure (tayyib) because his contract is corrupt, even though he may dispose of it. However, a second buyer who buys with a valid contract obtains pure ownership.
Purity here means freedom from sin in using the item.
4
Compare with war booty
If a person in dār al-ḥarb with amān takes property without consent, he owns it but it is not pure. If he sells it, the second buyer also does not get purity; both are ordered to return it to the original owners.
The cause is the betrayal of amān, which persists.
5
Note the Ramlī correction
Al-Ramlī corrects the earlier statement that the fāsid buyer 'is allowed to dispose' (yahill) to 'is not allowed' (lā yahill), indicating that while ownership is established, using it is actually prohibited.
This shows the nuanced view that fāsid ownership is valid but not pure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أو يهب) يعني إذا وهبه المشتري ارتفع الفساد ولا يفسخ لما قدمناه في البيع . وشرط في الهداية التسليم فيها لأنها لا تفيد الملك إلا به بخلاف البيع. وفي جامع الفصولين : ثم الأصل أن المانع إذا زال كفك رهن ورجوع هبة وعجز مكاتب ورد مبيع على المشتري بعيب بعد قبضه بقضاء فللبائع حق الفسخ لو لم يقض بقيمة لأن هذه العقود لم توجب الفسخ من كل وجه في حق الكل اهـ. ولا فرق في الرجوع في الهبة بين القضاء وغيره كما في فتح القدير . ثم أعلم أن المشتري فاسداً يطيب للمشتري ويطيب لمن انتقل الملك منه إليه لكون الثاني ملكه بعقد صحيح بخلاف المشتري الأول فإنه يحل له التصرف فيه ولا يطيب له لأنه ملكه بعقد فاسد، ولو دخل دار الحرب بأمان وأخذ مال الحربي بغير طيبة من نفسه وأخرجه إلى دار الإسلام ملكه ولا يطيب له ويفتي بالرد ويقضى له صح بيعه ولا يطيب للمشتري كما لا يطيب للأول بخلاف البيع الفاسد؛ كذا ذكره الاسبيجابي. وأما بعده فهو كسائر الغرماء كما صرحوا بذلك في الحجر .اهـ. فإن قلت : إذا مات المشتري بعد قبض البائع لم يبق له شيء جهة الميت حتى يكون كسائر الغرماء فيه قلت يحمل على ما إذا كان الذي قبضه البائع وهو المسمى دون قيمته فيكون أسوة الغرماء فيما بقي له من تمام القيمة لأن الواجب في البيع الفاسد إنما هو القيمة لا الثمن؟ هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم هذا وإلا فهو مشكل اهـ. قوله : ( على المشتري) أي المشتري شراء فاسداً . قوله : ( فإنه يحل له التصرف) قال الرملي: صوابه الا يحل قوله : (ولا يطيب للمشتري (الخ ذكر الإمام السرخسي في شرح السير الكبير في الباب الخامس بعد المائة : وإن اشترى إنسان منه ذلك جاز الشراء وإن كان مسيئاً لأنه باع ملك نفسه فإن فساد السبب لا يمنع ثبوت الملك ثم يؤمر المشتري بمثل ما كان يؤمر به البائع من الرد على أهل الحرب بخلاف المشتري شراء فاسداً إذا باعه من غيره بيعاً صحيحاً فإن الثاني لا يؤمر بالرد وإن كان البائع مأموراً به لأن الموجب للرد قد زال ببيعه لأن وجوب الرد بفساد البيع حكمه مقصور على المشتري وقد انعدم مثله بالبيع من غيره أما هنا وجوب الرد إنما كان لمراعاة ملكهم ولغدر الأمان وهذا المعنى قائم في ملك المشتري كما في ملك البائع الذي أخرجه فلهذا يفتي بالرد كما يفتي به
His statement: (or gives as a gift) – meaning if the buyer gives it as a gift, the corruption is removed and it is not annulled, as we mentioned in sales. In al-Hidāya, delivery (taslīm) is required in it because it does not give ownership except by it, unlike sale. In Jāmiʿ al-Fuṣūlayn: The principle is that when the impediment is removed, like freeing a pawn, revoking a gift, a mukātab becoming unable, or returning a sold item to the buyer due to a defect after possession by judgment – the seller has the right of annulment if he did not judge its value, because these contracts did not necessitate annulment in every respect regarding everyone, end. There is no difference in revoking a gift between judgment and other, as in Fatḥ al-Qadīr. Then know that the fāsid purchaser (the item) is good (pure) for the buyer and good for the one to whom ownership transfers from him because the second owns it with a valid contract, unlike the first buyer – for he is allowed to dispose of it but it is not good for him because he owns it with a corrupted contract. And if he enters the abode of war with amān and takes the property of a harbī without his consent and brings it out to dār al-Islām, he owns it but it is not good for him; fatwa is given for return and judgment is given; his sale is valid but it is not good for the buyer just as it is not good for the first, contrary to the fāsid sale; thus al-Usbījābī mentioned it. And as for after him, he is like other creditors as they explicitly stated in the chapter of interdiction, end. If you say: If the buyer dies after the seller has taken possession, he has nothing left from the deceased's side to be like the other creditors, I say: It is interpreted as when what the seller took is the named price without its value, so he becomes like the other creditors in what remains of the full value because the obligation in a fāsid sale is only the value, not the price? This is how it should be understood, otherwise it is problematic, end. His statement: (upon the buyer) – meaning the buyer who buys fāsidly. His statement: (then he is allowed to dispose) – al-Ramlī said: its correct wording is 'he is not allowed'. His statement: (and it is not good for the buyer) – Imām al-Sarakhsī mentioned in Sharḥ al-Siyar al-Kabīr in the 105th chapter: And if a person buys that from him, the sale is valid even though he is at fault because he sold his own property; the corruption of the reason does not prevent the establishment of ownership. Then the buyer is ordered to do as the seller was ordered, i.e., return to the people of war. Unlike the fāsid purchaser when he sells to another with a valid sale – the second is not ordered to return even though the seller was ordered, because the cause for return has ceased with his sale; the obligation of return due to the corruption of the sale is restricted to the buyer and has ceased by the sale to another. But here the obligation of return was only due to regard for their ownership and the betrayal of amān, and this meaning is present in the ownership of the buyer just as in the ownership of the seller who took it out; therefore fatwa is given for return as it is given for.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn phrase 'أو يهب' (or gives as a gift). It states that if the buyer of a fāsid sale gives the item as a gift (hiba), the corruption of the sale is removed and the original seller loses the right to annul. However, al-Hidāya requires delivery (taslīm) for the gift to be effective, unlike a sale where ownership transfers by contract. The principle from Jāmiʿ al-Fuṣūlayn is then quoted: when an impediment is removed (e.g., a pawn is freed, a gift is revoked, a mukātab becomes unable to pay, or a sold item is returned due to a defect after qadi judgment), the seller's right of annulment revives only if the qadi did not already judge the value. This is because these contracts did not cause a complete annulment in every respect. Then the passage discusses the concept of 'purity' (tayyib) of ownership: the fāsid buyer may dispose of the item, but it is not pure (i.e., it is not considered lawful in the sense of being blessed) because he acquired it through a corrupted contract. However, if he sells it to a second buyer with a valid sale, the second buyer's ownership is pure because his contract is sound. The passage then compares this to the case of an item taken from a harbī in dār al-ḥarb under amān: the taker owns it but it is not pure for him; if he sells it, the second buyer also does not get purity, unlike in the fāsid sale case. This is because the obligation to return it to the original owners is based on the betrayal of trust, and this obligation transfers to the buyer. A parenthetical note addresses the issue of death: if the buyer dies after the seller has taken possession (of the named price but not the full value), the seller is like other creditors for the remaining amount. The passage ends with a correction by al-Ramlī: the correct wording of 'يحل له التصرف' is 'لا يحل' (he is not allowed to dispose), indicating that the fāsid buyer should actually refrain from disposing, though the earlier text said it is permissible. Finally, a quote from Sarakhsī in Sharḥ al-Siyar al-Kabīr explains the difference: in the war booty case, the second buyer is also ordered to return the item to the people of war, whereas in a fāsid sale, the second buyer is not ordered to return. The reason is that the obligation to return in the fāsid sale is specific to the original buyer and ends when he sells; in the war booty case, the obligation persists because the essence of the right of the original owners remains.

Key Terms
يطيب (yaṭīb): It is pure or lawful in the sense of being blessed; the item is not considered tainted by the corrupted acquisition.
الهبة (al-hibah): A gift; in this context, a transfer of ownership without compensation that removes the right to annul the original fāsid sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person in dār al-ḥarb with amān takes property from a harbī without consent and sells it in dār al-Islām, what is the ruling on the second buyer?
A) The second buyer owns it purely and cannot be forced to return.
B) The second buyer must return the item to the people of war.
C) The second buyer is not required to return it because he bought it validly.
D) The second buyer is sinful but keeps the item.

Key Takeaway
A gift from a fāsid buyer removes the seller's right to annul, but the ownership remains impure; war booty taken by betrayal never becomes pure even after sale.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
