Ruling on Buyer's Disposition in an Invalid Sale
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the ruling of invalid sale, explaining the buyer's right to dispose and the exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a buyer's disposition in an invalid sale prevents rescission, identify the two exceptions, and distinguish the rules of invalid lease and gift.

Core Idea
A buyer in an invalid sale who takes possession can sell it, but selling back to seller or coercion annuls this right.

The Hanafi school holds that an invalid sale (bay' fasid) transfers ownership upon possession, allowing the buyer to dispose of the item. This lesson explores the general rule that such a disposition (e.g., selling to a third party) prevents the original seller from rescinding the contract, with two critical exceptions. We also examine how invalid lease differs and how a gift removes the invalidity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Understand that in a fasid sale, after the buyer takes possession, his sale to a third party is binding and blocks the seller's right to rescind.
This is based on the Zahir al-Riwayah as cited in al-Bazzaziyyah.
2
Recognize the Two Exceptions
Memorise that if the buyer sells the item back to his original seller, it is treated as a rescission; and if the original sale was invalid due to coercion, all of the buyer's dispositions are void.
These exceptions are explicitly stated in the text.
3
Distinguish Invalid Lease and Gift
Analyse the difference: a lessee in an invalid lease may sublet after possession, but the original lessor can annul the sublease due to excuses. A gift by the buyer removes the invalidity but requires delivery for ownership.
The gift rule is from al-Hidayah; the lease discussion is from Jami' al-Fusulayn.
4
Apply the Principle of Removal of Obstacles
Understand that when an obstacle (e.g., mortgage, gift, defect claim) is removed, the seller's right to rescind revives—unless a price was fixed, as the earlier contracts did not necessitate full rescission.
This principle is articulated in Jami' al-Fusulayn and supported by Fath al-Qadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب البيع الفاسد اشتراه ثانياً وسيأتي في الضابط. وقيد ببيع المشتري لأن البائع لو باعه بعد قبض المشتري وادعى أن الثاني كان قبل فسخ الأول وقبضه وزعم المشتري الثاني أنه كان بعد الفسخ والقبض من الأول فالقول له لا للبائع وينفسخ الأول يقبض الثاني؛ كذا في البزازية. ويستثنى من لزومه بالبيع مسألتان: الأولى لو باعه لبائعه فقدمنا أنه يكون رداً وفسخاً للبيع. والثانية لو كان فاسداً بالإكراه فإن تصرفات المشتري كلها تنقض بخلاف سائر البياعات الفاسدة؛ كذا في البزازية. قيد بالبيع الفاسد إحترازاً عن الإجارة الفاسدة لما في جامع الفصولين: قيل ليس للمستأجر فاسداً أن يؤجره من غيره إجازة صحيحة إستدلالاً بما ذكر إلى آخره. وقيل يملكها بعد قبضه كمشتر فاسداً له البيع جائزاً وهو الصحيح إلا أن للمؤجر الأول نقض الثانية لأنها تنفسخ بالأعذار قوله: (أو يهب) يعني إذا وهبه المشتري ارتفع الفساد ولا يفسخ لما قدمناه في البيع. وشرط في الهداية التسليم فيها لأنها لا تفيد الملك إلا به بخلاف البيع. وفي جامع الفصولين: ثم الأصل أن المانع إذا زال كفك رهن ورجوع هبة وعجز مكاتب ورد مبيع على المشتري بعيب بعد قبضه بقضاء فللبائع حق الفسخ لو لم يقض بقيمة لأن هذه العقود لم توجب الفسخ من كل وجه في حق الكل اهـ. ولا فرق في الرجوع في الهبة بين القضاء وغيره كما في فتح القدير.
Book of Sale: Chapter on Invalid Sale. He bought it secondly, and it will come in the rule. It is restricted to the buyer's sale because if the seller sells it after the buyer's possession and claims that the second (sale) was before the rescission of the first and its possession, and the second buyer claims that it was after the rescission and possession from the first, then the word is his (the second buyer's), not the seller's, and the first is rescinded by the second's possession; as in al-Bazzaziyyah. Two matters are excepted from its binding by sale: first, if he sold it to his seller, we have stated that it is a return and rescission of the sale. Second, if it was invalid due to coercion, then all the buyer's dispositions are annulled, unlike other invalid sales; as in al-Bazzaziyyah. It is restricted to invalid sale to exclude invalid lease, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn: it was said that a lessee in an invalid lease cannot lease it to another with a valid lease, by analogy to what was mentioned to the end. And it was said that he owns it after possession, like an invalid buyer has the right to sell, and this is correct, except that the first lessor can annul the second because it is annulled by excuses. His statement: (or he gives) means if the buyer gives it as a gift, the invalidity is removed and it is not rescinded, as we have stated in the sale. And in al-Hidayah, delivery is required in it because it does not confer ownership except with it, unlike sale. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: then the principle is that when an obstacle is removed, like releasing a mortgage, returning a gift, inability of a mukatib, and returning the sold item to the buyer due to a defect after his possession by judgment, then the seller has the right to rescind if he did not stipulate a price, because these contracts did not necessitate rescission in every respect with regard to everyone. There is no difference in returning a gift between judgment and other than it, as in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling of invalid sale. It establishes that a buyer who acquires an item through an invalid sale (بيع فاسد) has the right to dispose of it, including selling it to a third party. Such a sale by the buyer is binding and prevents the original seller from rescinding the invalid sale, except in two cases: (1) if the buyer sells the item back to the original seller, which constitutes a rescission, and (2) if the invalid sale was due to coercion, where all the buyer's actions are nullified. The author then distinguishes invalid lease (إجارة فاسدة) from invalid sale: the correct view is that a lessee in an invalid lease can sublet with a valid lease after taking possession, but the original lessor retains the right to annul the second lease because lease can be terminated due to excuses (أعذار). The discussion also covers gift (هبة): if the buyer gives the item as a gift, the invalidity is removed and the seller cannot rescind, provided the gift is delivered (since gift requires delivery for ownership). The principle from Jami' al-Fusulayn is that when an obstacle to rescission is removed (e.g., release of mortgage, return of gift, inability of mukatib, or return of defective item by court order), the seller's right to rescind revives. However, this does not apply if a price was fixed, because the earlier contracts did not necessitate full rescission. The Hanafi position is that the right to rescind after such obstacles returns, as detailed in Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is valid in essence (i.e., contract formation) but invalid in description (e.g., defective price or delivery), leading to ownership but with a right of rescission for the seller.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract, especially the seller's right to undo the invalid sale due to its defect.
إجارة فاسدة (ijarah fasidah): An invalid lease, analogous to invalid sale but with different rules regarding subleasing and rescission due to excuses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling when a buyer in an invalid sale sells the item to a third party?
A) The sale is void and the original seller can rescind.
B) The sale is binding and the original seller cannot rescind.
C) The sale is valid only if the third party is unaware of the defect.
D) The sale is valid but the original seller can still rescind.

Key Takeaway
A buyer in an invalid Hanafi sale can transfer ownership through sale or gift, but the seller's right to rescind revives in specific exceptions and when obstacles are removed.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Lawfulness for the Buyer in Invalid Sale: First vs Second
Explains the ruling on tayyib for first and second buyer in invalid sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the permissibility (tayyib) of an item for the first and second buyer in an invalid sale, distinguish it from the war booty case, and apply the correction by al-Ramli.

Core Idea
In an invalid sale, the first buyer may dispose but cannot use; the second buyer gains tayyib through a valid contract.

This lesson examines a subtle distinction in Hanafi fiqh: when a sale is invalid, the item becomes 'lawful' (tayyib) for a subsequent purchaser who buys it in a valid contract, but not for the first buyer. We will also contrast this with property taken from Dar al-Harb under aman, where even a second buyer does not gain tayyib. The correction by al-Ramli clarifies a key phrase in the original text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two buyers
Note the distinction: the first buyer (al-mushtari al-awwal) purchases via an invalid sale; the second buyer purchases from the first via a valid sale. Each has a different ruling regarding tayyib.
The first buyer owns the item but cannot use it lawfully; the second can.
2
State the ruling for each
The text says the first buyer may dispose (e.g., sell) but the item is not tayyib for him. Al-Ramli corrects that he is not even permitted to dispose. The second buyer, however, acquires tayyib because his ownership stems from a valid contract.
This is the core distinction; the first buyer's ownership is defective in permissibility.
3
Compare with war booty
If someone enters Dar al-Harb with aman and takes property without consent, he owns it but it is not tayyib. Even a second buyer does not gain tayyib, unlike the invalid sale case. The reason is that the obligation to return the property persists due to the breach of aman.
This analogy helps understand the underlying principle: the cause of the defect (ghadr al-aman) continues to affect permissibility.
4
Understand the rationale
In an invalid sale, the invalidity of the cause (fasad al-sabab) does not prevent ownership, but it prevents the item from being tayyib for the party whose ownership is defective. When a valid contract intervenes, the new ownership is pure.
Contrast with batil (void) sales where ownership does not transfer at all.

قال الإمام الخصاف في أحكام الأوقاف: لو اشترى أرضاً بيعاً فاسداً وقبضها ووقفها وقفاً صحيحاً وجعل آخرها للمساكين فقال: الوقف فيها جائز وعليه قيمتها للبائع من قبل إنه استهلكها حين وقفها وأخرجها عن ملكه اهـ. وهكذا في الاسعاف ولم يذكر المؤلف من التصرفات القولية غير ذلك ففاته الرهن لأنه من العقود اللازمة فيمنع حق الرد فإذا فك أو فسخ قبل القضاء بالقيمة عاد حق الاسترداد. وفاته أيضاً الوصية فإذا وصى به المشتري ثم سقط الفسخ لأن المبيع انتقل عن ملكه إلى ملك الموصى وهو ملك مبتدأ فصار كما لو باعه بخلاف ما إذا مات المشتري فإن لوارثه الفسخ وللبائع أيضاً لأن الوارث قائم مقام المورث؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج. قالوا: كل تصرف قولي فإنه يمنع الفسخ إلا الإجارة والنكاح فلا يمنعانه لأن الإجارة تفسخ بالأعذار ورفع الفساد الأعذار والنكاح ليس فيه الإخراج عن الملك، ولكن إذا ردت الجارية إلى البائع وانفسخ البيع هل ينفسخ النكاح؟ قال في السراج الوهاج: إنه لا ينفسخ لأنه لا يفسخ بالأعذار وقد عقده المشتري له من البائع . اهـ ملخصاً. وقال بعده في الباب الثاني والستين بعد المائة: فإن لم يرده بعد ما أفتى به وأراد بيعه يكره للمسلمين أن يشتروا ذلك منه لأنه ملك خبيث بمنزلة المشتري فاسداً إذا أراد بيع بعد القبض يكره شراؤه منه، وإن كان مالكاً نفذ فيه بيعه وعتقه لأنه ملك حصل له بسبب حرام شرعاً اهد. وهذا مخالف لما هنا. وقد يجاب بأن ما أخرجه من دار الحرب لما وجب رده على المشتري أيضاً تمكن فيه الخبث فلم يطب له بخلاف المشتري فاسداً فلذا طاب له وإن كان شراؤه مكروهاً تأمل.
Then know that the buyer in an invalid sale, it becomes lawful (tayyib) for the buyer and lawful for the one to whom ownership transfers from him, because the second owns it by a valid contract, unlike the first buyer; for he is permitted to dispose of it but it is not lawful for him, because he owns it by an invalid contract. And if he entered Dar al-Harb with aman and took the property of a harbi without his consent and brought it out to Dar al-Islam, he owns it but it is not lawful for him, and a fatwa is given for its return, and a judgment is given for him; his sale is valid, but it is not lawful for the buyer just as it is not lawful for the first, unlike an invalid sale; as mentioned by al-Ispijabi. As for after him, he is like other creditors, as they stated in the chapter on interdiction. If you say: when the buyer dies after the seller's possession, he has nothing from the deceased to be like other creditors, I say: it is interpreted as when what the seller took is the named price without its value, so he is like other creditors regarding what remains of the full value, because the obligation in an invalid sale is only the value, not the price? This is how it should be understood, otherwise it is problematic. His statement: (upon the buyer) meaning the buyer in an invalid sale. His statement: (for he is permitted to dispose) al-Ramli said: the correct is that he is not permitted. His statement: (and it is not lawful for the buyer) etc. Imam al-Sarakhsi mentioned in Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir in the fifth chapter after one hundred: if a person buys that from him, the purchase is valid even if he is sinful, because he sold his own property; for the invalidity of the cause does not prevent the establishment of ownership. Then the buyer is ordered to do what the seller was ordered to do: return it to the people of war. Unlike an invalid buyer who sells it to another with a valid sale, the second is not ordered to return, even though the seller was ordered to return, because the cause for return has been removed by his sale; for the obligation of return due to the invalidity of the sale is a ruling confined to the buyer, and its like is eliminated by the sale to another. As for here, the obligation of return was only due to regard for their ownership and the breach of aman, and this meaning exists in the buyer's ownership just as in the ownership of the seller who brought it out; therefore a fatwa is given for return just as a fatwa is given for it. His statement: (or he manumits) means the buyer frees the slave as we have stated, and the corollaries of manumission are like it: tadbir, istilad, and kitabah. In Jami' al-Fusulayn, istilad is explicitly stated: if she becomes pregnant from him, she becomes umm walad; the commentator and others explicitly state kitabah, and I have not seen anyone explicitly state tadbir. And if the mukatib becomes unable, the obstacle to recovery is removed. He indicated by 'tahrir' to waqf, but in Jami' al-Fusulayn it says: if he endows it or makes it a mosque, his right is not invalidated as long as he does not build. So it is known that waqf is not like manumission, and it should be interpreted as before judgment; if judgment is given, then the invalidity is removed due to its binding nature. And it appears that what is in Jami' al-Fusulayn following al-Umadi is not correct. Imam al-Khassaf said in Ahkam al-Awqaf: if someone bought land in an invalid sale, took possession, endowed it as a valid waqf, and made the ultimate beneficiaries the poor, he said: the waqf is valid and he owes its value to the seller because he consumed it when he endowed it and removed it from his ownership. And thus in al-Is'af. The author did not mention from verbal dispositions other than that, so he missed mortgage, for it is a binding contract that prevents the right to return; if it is released or rescinded before judgment for the value, the right to recovery returns. He also missed will; if the buyer bequeaths it, then the rescission falls, because the sold item transfers from his ownership to the ownership of the legatee, which is a new ownership, so it becomes as if he sold it. Unlike if the buyer dies, then his heir has the right to rescind, and the seller also, because the heir stands in the place of the testator; as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. They said: every verbal disposition prevents rescission except lease and marriage; they do not prevent it, because lease is rescinded due to excuses and the removal of invalidity is by excuses, and marriage does not involve removal from ownership. But if the slave-girl is returned to the seller and the sale is rescinded, is the marriage rescinded? He said in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: it is not rescinded, because it is not rescinded by excuses, and the buyer married her from the seller. Summarized. And he said after that in the sixty-second chapter after one hundred: if he does not return it after the fatwa and wants to sell it, it is disliked for Muslims to buy that from him, because it is impure property like an invalid buyer; if he wants to sell after possession, it is disliked to buy from him. And even though he is an owner, his sale and manumission are effective, because it is property acquired through a prohibited means. This contradicts what is here. And it may be answered that what is brought out from Dar al-Harb, since its return becomes obligatory on the buyer as well, impurity is present, so it is not lawful for him, unlike an invalid buyer, so it is lawful for him even if buying from him is disliked. Ponder.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on invalid sale by addressing the permissibility (طيب) of the item for the buyer and subsequent purchasers. According to the Hanafi school, the item purchased through an invalid sale becomes lawful for a second buyer who acquires it via a valid contract, because the ownership is established anew. However, the first buyer does not enjoy this permissibility; though he may dispose of the item (e.g., sell it), the item remains 'unclean' for his own use because his ownership originates from an invalid contract. The author then contrasts this with property taken from Dar al-Harb under aman: the taker owns the property but it is not lawful for him, and a fatwa is given for its return. If he sells it, the sale is valid but the second buyer also does not benefit from permissibility, unlike the invalid sale case. A subtle point is raised: if the buyer dies, the seller becomes like other creditors regarding the value owed. The commentary by al-Ramli corrects a phrase: the buyer is 'not permitted' (لا يحل) to use it, contrary to the text. Al-Sarakhsi's view from al-Siyar al-Kabir is cited: the second buyer in the war booty case is still ordered to return the item, because the cause for return (protecting the enemy's ownership and the breach of aman) continues, unlike the invalid sale where the cause is confined to the first buyer. The passage then moves to dispositions: manumission, tadbir, istilad, kitabah, waqf, mortgage, and will. The general rule is that any verbal disposition (تصرف قولي) prevents rescission, except lease and marriage. Lease is terminable by excuses, and marriage does not involve removal from ownership. If a slave-girl is married by the buyer and then the sale is rescinded, the marriage does not dissolve. The author notes a contradiction with Sarakhsi's later statement about buying from someone who brought property from Dar al-Harb being disliked, and resolves it by distinguishing that the impurity (خبث) remains in the war booty case due to the obligation to return, whereas in invalid sale, the impurity is removed once the item is sold validly. The overall teaching is that ownership through an invalid cause does not necessarily make the item lawful for use, and the liability to return may persist or be transferred depending on the nature of the transaction.

Key Terms
طيب (tayyib): Lawful or permissible for use and consumption, as opposed to merely permissible disposition; an item that is pure in terms of ownership.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory under non-Muslim control where aman (safe conduct) may apply; property taken from there under certain conditions may be owned but not lawful.
فساد السبب (fasad al-sabab): The cause (contract) is defective in description but not in essence; ownership transfers but permissibility is affected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of an item purchased in an invalid sale (fasid) for a second buyer who acquires it via a valid contract?
A) The item remains unlawful for the second buyer because the original sale was invalid.
B) The item becomes lawful for the second buyer because his ownership stems from a valid contract.
C) The second buyer is obliged to return the item to the original seller.
D) The second buyer cannot validly purchase the item.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, the first buyer's use is unlawful, but a valid subsequent transfer cleanses the item for the second buyer.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Verbal Dispositions and Their Effect on Rescission in Invalid Sales
Expands on the rule that verbal dispositions prevent rescission of invalid sales, with exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the verbal dispositions that prevent rescission of an invalid sale, explain why lease and marriage are exceptions, and apply the rule to new cases.

Core Idea
Every verbal disposition that transfers ownership prevents rescission of an invalid sale, except lease and marriage.

This lesson focuses on a practical rule: once an invalid sale has taken place, certain acts by the buyer can prevent the seller from rescinding the transaction. These acts are called 'verbal dispositions' (tasarruf qawli), such as manumitting a slave or endowing property. However, lease and marriage are exceptions—they do not block rescission. We will examine why and look at specific examples from the text, including the case of a slave-girl and the effect of the buyer's death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage states: 'كل تصرف قولي يمنع الفسخ' – every verbal disposition prevents rescission. This means if the buyer performs any such act, the seller loses the right to rescind the invalid sale.
The rule applies to dispositions that effect a transfer of ownership or rights.
2
List the covered dispositions
The text specifically mentions: manumission (itq), tadbir (promise of freedom upon owner's death), istilad (making a slave-girl umm walad by having a child), kitabah (contract of manumission), waqf (endowment), mortgage (rahn), and will (wasiyya). These all prevent rescission.
Some dispositions have sub-rulings; e.g., waqf is effective only if the property is physically built upon (as per Jami' al-Fusulayn).
3
Note the exceptions
Lease (ijarah) and marriage (nikah) do NOT prevent rescission. Lease can be rescinded due to excuses; marriage does not remove the slave-girl from ownership.
If the buyer marries a slave-girl bought in an invalid sale, and the sale is later rescinded, the marriage remains valid but does not prevent the return of the slave-girl to the seller.
4
Examine the effect of death
If the buyer dies before the sale is rescinded, the seller's right to rescind passes to the buyer's heirs; the seller also retains the right. Death does not eliminate the possibility of rescission.
This is unlike the case where a will is made, which transfers ownership anew and prevents rescission.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on invalid sale by addressing the permissibility (طيب) of the item for the buyer and subsequent purchasers. If he sells it, the sale is valid but the second buyer also does not benefit from permissibility, unlike the invalid sale case. The general rule is that any verbal disposition (تصرف قولي) prevents rescission, except lease and marriage. Lease is terminable by excuses, and marriage does not involve removal from ownership. If a slave-girl is married by the buyer and then the sale is rescinded, the marriage does not dissolve. The author notes a contradiction with Sarakhsi's later statement about buying from someone who brought property from Dar al-Harb being disliked, and resolves it by distinguishing that the impurity (خبث) remains in the war booty case due to the obligation to return, whereas in invalid sale, the impurity is removed once the item is sold validly. The overall teaching is that ownership through an invalid cause does not necessarily make the item lawful for use, and the liability to return may persist or be transferred depending on the nature of the transaction.

Key Terms
تصرف قولي (tasarruf qawli): A verbal disposition such as sale, gift, manumission, etc., that transfers ownership or rights; as opposed to physical acts.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of a contract; annulment of the transaction.
إجارة (ijarah): Lease contract; an exception that does not prevent rescission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following verbal dispositions does NOT prevent rescission of an invalid (fasid) sale?
A) Manumission (itq)
B) Endowment (waqf)
C) Marriage (nikah)
D) Will (wasiyya)

Key Takeaway
Verbal dispositions that transfer ownership block rescission of a fasid sale, but lease and marriage are exceptions that preserve the seller's right.

Effect of Itaq and Similar Acts in a Fasid Sale
Ruling on buyer's itaq preventing seller's right of return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which acts of disposition by the buyer in a fasid sale prevent the seller's right to reclaim, and explain why itaq is effective despite the defective contract.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, buyer's itaq extinguishes seller's right to reclaim—it's a consumptive transfer overriding the defective contract.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Ibn Nujaym unpacks the ruling that when a buyer in a fasid (voidable) sale frees the purchased slave, the seller loses the right to reclaim it. This ruling extends to similar acts like tadbir, istiwlad, and kitabah. The passage clarifies that waqf does not automatically prevent return, and discusses other omitted dispositions like rahn and wasiyyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the opening: the buyer's itaq (emancipation) in a fasid sale prevents the seller from reclaiming the slave. Note that this is a Hanafi position based on istihsan.
This is the main legal point.
2
List the companions of itaq
Find that tadbir, istiwlad, and kitabah are explicitly mentioned as having the same effect. Check the sources cited (Jami' al-Fusulayn, al-Siraj).
Tadbir was initially doubted but later confirmed.
3
Contrast waqf with itaq
The text states that waqf does NOT prevent return unless accompanied by building or a judicial ruling. Note the difference: waqf is not a consumptive act.
This is a common point of confusion.
4
Recognize other omitted dispositions
Rahn and wasiyyah also prevent return, but the author of Kanz did not mention them. Understand why: rahn is a binding contract, wasiyyah transfers ownership.
These are additional cases.
5
Note the exception for mukatib
If the mukatib (contractually enslaved) becomes incapable of paying, the seller's right to reclaim returns.
This shows the rule is not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أو يحرر) أي يعتق المشتري العبد لما قدمناه وتوابع الإعتاق كهو من التدبير والاستيلاد والكتابة صرح في جامع الفصولين بالاستيلاد فقال : إذا حبلت منه صارت أم ولده؛ وصرح الشارح وغيره بالكتابة ولم أر من صرح بالتدبير. وإذا عجز المكاتب زال المانع من الاسترداد. وأشار بالتحرير إلى الوقف ولكن قال في جامع الفصولين: فلو وقفه أو جعله مسجداً لا يبطل حقه ما لم يبن .اهـ. فعلم أن الوقف ليس كالتحرير وينبغي أن يحمل على ما قبل القضاء به، أما إذا قضى به فإنه يرتفع الفساد للزومه والظاهر أن ما في جامع الفصولين تبعاً للعمادي ليس بصحيح فقد قال الإمام الخصاف في أحكام الأوقاف: لو اشترى أرضاً بيعاً فاسداً وقبضها ووقفها وقفاً صحيحاً وجعل آخرها للمساكين فقال : الوقف فيها جائز وعليه قيمتها للبائع من قبل إنه استهلكها حين وقفها وأخرجها عن ملكه اهـ. وهكذا في الاسعاف ولم يذكر المؤلف من التصرفات القولية غير ذلك ففاته الرهن لأنه من العقود اللازمة فيمنع حق الرد فإذا فك أو فسخ قبل القضاء بالقيمة عاد حق الاسترداد. وفاته أيضاً الوصية فإذا وصى به المشتري ثم سقط الفسخ لأن المبيع انتقل عن ملكه إلى ملك الموصى وهو ملك مبتدأ فصار كما لو باعه بخلاف ما إذا مات المشتري فإن لوارثه الفسخ وللبائع أيضاً لأن الوارث قائم مقام المورث ؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج.
His saying: (or he frees) meaning the buyer emancipates the slave, as we previously mentioned. And the companions of emancipation, like tadbir, istiwlad, and kitabah. In Jami' al-Fusulayn, he stated regarding istiwlad: 'If she becomes pregnant from him, she becomes an umm walad.' The commentator and others stated kitabah, but I have not seen anyone who stated tadbir. If the mukatib becomes incapable, the impediment to reclaiming (the slave) is removed. He indicated by 'tahrir' waqf, but in Jami' al-Fusulayn he said: 'If he makes a waqf or makes it a mosque, his right is not invalidated as long as he does not build.' So it is understood that waqf is not like emancipation, and it should be interpreted as before a judicial ruling; if a ruling is made, then the nullity is lifted due to its binding nature. It appears that what is in Jami' al-Fusulayn following al-Imadi is not correct. Imam al-Khassaf said in Ahkam al-Awqaf: 'If one buys land in a fasid sale, takes possession, and makes a valid waqf, making its end for the poor, the waqf is valid and he must pay its value to the seller because he has consumed it by making waqf and removed it from his ownership.' Similarly in al-Is'af. The author did not mention other verbal dispositions besides that, so he omitted rahn because it is a binding contract that prevents the right of return; if it is released or rescinded before a ruling for the value, the right of return returns. He also omitted wasiyyah: if the buyer makes a bequest of it, then the rescission falls, because the sold item has transferred from his ownership to the ownership of the legatee, which is a new ownership, so it is as if he sold it. Unlike if the buyer dies, then his heir has the right to rescind, and the seller also, because the heir stands in the place of the testator; thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'or he frees' from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discussing the effect of buyer's emancipation (itaq) in a fasid sale. The ruling: when the buyer in a fasid sale frees the slave, the seller's right to reclaim the slave (istirdad) is extinguished. This applies equally to analogous acts: tadbir (promise of freedom upon death), istiwlad (making a female slave an umm walad by having a child), and kitabah (contract of manumission). The author notes that istiwlad is explicitly mentioned in Jami' al-Fusulayn, and kitabah is mentioned by the commentator (al-Marghinani) and others, but he had not seen tadbir explicitly stated. Later he cites al-Nahr showing tadbir is indeed in al-Siraj. If the mukatib fails to pay, the impediment to return is removed. The text also discusses waqf: the author initially indicates that 'tahrir' may refer to waqf, but Jami' al-Fusulayn states that waqf does not prevent return unless the building is constructed; the commentator refutes this, citing Imam al-Khassaf that waqf does prevent return because it removes the item from ownership. The author then lists verbal dispositions the author of Kanz omitted: rahn (mortgage) and wasiyyah (bequest) also prevent rescission because they are binding transfers, except in specific cases. The overall legal principle: any verbal disposition that transfers ownership or creates a binding right prevents the seller from rescinding the fasid sale.

Key Terms
إعتاق (itaq): Emancipation of a slave by the owner, which in a fasid sale is considered a valid act that extinguishes the seller's right to reclaim.
تدبير (tadbir): A promise to free a slave upon the owner's death; in a fasid sale, it prevents return like itaq.
استيلاد (istiwlad): Making a female slave an umm walad by having a child; this prevents return in a fasid sale.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract of manumission where the slave pays for freedom; in a fasid sale, it prevents return unless the mukatib becomes incapable.
رهن (rahn): Mortgage or pledge; in a fasid sale, it prevents return because it is a binding contract; if released, return is possible.
وصية (wasiyyah): Bequest; in a fasid sale, it prevents return as it transfers ownership to the legatee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale, if the buyer frees the slave, what is the effect on the seller's right to reclaim the slave?
A) The seller can still reclaim the slave because the sale is fasid.
B) The seller's right to reclaim is extinguished because the buyer's itaq is valid.
C) The seller can reclaim only if the buyer did not pay the price.
D) The seller's right is suspended until the buyer compensates.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, the buyer's itaq and similar consumptive acts prevent the seller's right to reclaim, while non-consumptive acts like waqf do not automatically prevent it.

Building in a Fasid Sale: Does It Prevent the Seller's Right of Return?
Ruling on buyer's building preventing seller's right of return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether a buyer's building in a fasid sale prevents the seller's right of return, and apply the legal reasoning (taslit vs. shuf'ah) to resolve the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
A buyer's building with the seller's permission cuts off the right of return in fasid sales, but the Sahibayn require demolition by analogy to preemption.

When a sale is fasid (defective), the buyer does not own the property. But what if the buyer constructs a building on the land? Does that change the seller's right to reclaim? The Hanafi masters disagree on this point, and their reasoning reveals deeper principles about permission, permanence, and analogy. In this lesson, we will unpack the dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Recognize that the buyer's building (bina') is a physical act in a fasid sale. The dispute is whether the building must be demolished or can remain, affecting the seller's right to reclaim the land.
The text specifically mentions building (bina') and planting (ghars), but the author only discusses building.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer pays the land's value and keeps the building. His proof: building is intended for permanence and occurred with the seller's permission (taslit), so it cuts off the right of reclamation by analogy to a valid sale.
This is based on istihsan (juristic preference).
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the building must be demolished. Their proof: analogy to the right of preemption (shuf'ah) — building does not nullify the preemptor's right, even without permission, so the seller's stronger right is even more resistant.
They use strict qiyas.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa distinguishes: the seller gave possession, making his right weaker than the preemptor's; thus, with permission, the building is effective. The Sahibayn's analogy fails because the illa (effective cause) differs.
The distinction hinges on taslit (permitting by delivery).
5
Determine the practical consequence and mufta bihi
Under Abu Hanifa, the building remains; under Sahibayn, it is demolished. The relied-upon position in the school is Abu Hanifa's view, based on istihsan.
Later Hanafi fatwa works generally adopt Abu Hanifa's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أو يبني) أي إذا بنى المشتري فاسداً فعليه القيمة عند أبي حنيفة رواه عنه يعقوب في الجامع الصغير ثم شك بعد ذلك في الرواية. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد: إنه ينقض البناء وترد الدار والغرس على هذا الإختلاف أن حق الشفيع أضعف من البائع حتى يحتاج فيه إلى القضاء ويبطل بالتأخير بخلاف حق البائع ثم أضعف الحقين لا يبطل بالبناء فأقواهما أولى. وله أن البناء والغرس مما يقصد به الدوام وقد حصل بتسليط من جهة البائع فينقطع حق الاسترداد كالبيع بخلاف حق الشفيع لأنه لم يوجد منه التسليط، ولهذا لم تبطل بهبة المشتري وبيعه فكذا ببثائه. وشك يعقوب في حظ الرواية عن أبي حنيفة، وقد نص محمد على الاختلاف. ولم يذكر المؤلف من الأفعال الحسية إلا البناء
His saying: (or he builds) meaning if the buyer builds in a fasid sale, then he owes the value according to Abu Hanifa, as narrated by Ya'qub from him in al-Jami' al-Saghir, then he doubted the narration later. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: he demolishes the building and returns the house and the planting. This difference is based on the fact that the right of the preemptor (shafi') is weaker than that of the seller, as it requires a court order and is nullified by delay, unlike the seller's right. Then the weaker of the two rights should not be nullified by building, so the stronger is more deserving. For him (Abu Hanifa), building and planting are intended for permanence and have occurred with the permission of the seller, so the right of reclamation is cut off, like a sale. Unlike the right of the preemptor, because there is no permission from him. For this reason, the buyer's gift and sale do not nullify (his right), so likewise his building. Ya'qub doubted the narration from Abu Hanifa, and Muhammad clearly stated the difference. The author did not mention from the physical acts except building.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when a buyer in a fasid sale constructs a building or plants trees on the purchased land. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute. According to Abu Hanifa (as narrated by Ya'qub, though with some doubt), the buyer owes the value of the land but does not have to demolish the building; the building remains and the seller's right to reclaim the land is extinguished. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the building must be demolished and the land returned. The reasoning: Abu Hanifa compares the buyer's building to a sale (which would transfer ownership), and since the seller implicitly permitted the building by handing over possession, the buyer's act is akin to a valid disposition. The Sahibayn argue by analogy to the right of preemption (shuf'ah): just as building by a buyer does not nullify the preemptor's right (because the preemptor never consented), similarly the seller's right should not be nullified because it is stronger. Abu Hanifa counters that the seller's right is different because he gave possession (taslit), so building with permission is effective. The author notes that Ya'qub doubted the narration from Abu Hanifa, but Muhammad confirmed the difference.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is defective in a condition not affecting its essence (e.g., uncertainty in price), but can become valid if the defect is removed.
بناء (bina'): Construction of a building; a physical act that is intended for permanence on land.
تسليط (taslit): Giving permission or authorization; in this context, the seller handing over possession implies permission for the buyer to act.
شفعة (shuf'ah): Preemption; the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase property that has been sold to an outsider.
قيمة (qimah): Value; the monetary compensation the buyer must pay to the seller if the building remains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The building must be demolished and the land returned.
B) The buyer keeps the building and pays the value of the land.
C) The seller can reclaim the land after paying for the building.
D) The building is considered a gift and the buyer must leave.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, a building constructed by the buyer with the seller's permission nullifies the seller's right of return, and the buyer retains the building by paying the land's value — this is the mufta bihi Hanafi position.

Effect of Fasid Sale on Subsequent Marriage of Jariyah
Ruling on whether marriage of jariyah remains valid after sale rescission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on whether a marriage contracted in a fasid sale remains valid after rescission, and apply the rule based on possession.

Core Idea
Marriage in a fasid sale remains valid if possession occurred; without possession the marriage is void.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of rescinding a fasid sale on a marriage the buyer contracted with the female slave. The key factor is whether the buyer had taken possession (qabdh) at the time of marriage. We will analyse the proofs from al-Siraj and al-Walwaliji to understand the distinction.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the general ruling that verbal dispositions prevent rescission except ijarah and nikah. Then note the specific question: is the marriage rescinded when the sale is rescinded? The position in al-Siraj and al-Fath is that it is not rescinded.
Marriage does not involve removal from ownership, so it is not affected by the sale's defect.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Al-Walwaliji quotes Abu Yusuf's view: if the marriage was contracted before the buyer took possession of the slave, and the sale is rescinded, the marriage is invalid. This is the chosen view (mukhtar) according to Abu Yusuf.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad/al-Siraj.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Abu Yusuf's reasoning: rescission before possession operates ab initio, so the sale is considered never to have existed. Therefore the buyer never owned the slave, making the marriage void.
This relies on the legal fiction that rescission ab initio erases the contract.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The text reconciles the views by distinguishing based on possession. Al-Siraj's ruling applies when the buyer had possession at the time of marriage; al-Walwaliji's case is before possession. Thus, if possession occurred, the marriage stands; if not, it is void.
This distinction preserves the validity of marriage after possession, protecting the stability of family relationships.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the buyer married the slave after taking possession, the marriage remains valid upon rescission. If before possession, the marriage is invalid and the slave is returned as if never married.
This affects issues like legitimacy of children, inheritance, and the husband's right to retain her.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

They said: Every verbal disposition prevents rescission except ijarah and nikah; they do not prevent it because ijarah can be rescinded due to excuses and removing the defect is an excuse, and marriage does not involve removal from ownership. But if the female slave is returned to the seller and the sale is rescinded, is the marriage rescinded? He said in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: It is not rescinded because it is not rescinded by excuses, and the buyer contracted it when she was in his ownership. Summary. And he said after that in the one hundred and sixty-second chapter: If he does not return it after giving the fatwa and wants to sell it, it is disliked for Muslims to buy it from him because it is impure property like that of a fasid purchaser when he wants to sell after possession; buying from him is disliked. And if he is the owner, his sale and emancipation are effective because it is ownership he acquired through something prohibited by sharia. This is contrary to what is here. It may be answered: What he took out of dar al-harb, since it must be returned to the buyer, also has impurity, so it is not permitted to him, unlike the fasid purchaser, so it is permitted to him even if buying from him is disliked. Contemplate. His saying: (I have not seen anyone who stated tadbir) He said in al-Nahr: I say, I have seen it, praise be to Allah. He said in al-Siraj, his words: If the sold item was a slave and the buyer freed him or made him mudabbar, his emancipation and tadbir are valid. Similarly, if it was a female slave and he made her an umm walad, she becomes an umm walad, and he pays the value and does not pay the 'aqr (compensation for intercourse) according to one narration in Kitab al-Buyu', and in another narration he returns the 'aqr. The narrations agreed that if he had intercourse with her and she did not concieve, he returns the slave and the 'aqr. His saying: (not correct) He said in al-Nahr: It appears that what is in al-Fusul is a narration. His saying: (He said in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that it is not rescinded) This is supported by what is in al-Fath where he said: If the buyer marries the female slave purchased in a fasid sale, the seller may reclaim her because the husband's right is in the benefit and does not prevent the seller's right in the corpus, and because he does not miss the ownership of that buyer while she is in his ownership. But this is problematic due to what al-Walwaliji mentioned in the first chapter of the book of marriage: If he marries the sold female slave before taking possession and the sale is rescinded, the marriage is invalid according to Abu Yusuf, which is the chosen view, because when the sale is rescinded before possession, it is rescinded ab initio, so it becomes as if it did not exist, thus the marriage is invalid. Unless it is interpreted that what is in al-Siraj is the view of Muhammad, or a distinction appears between them. His saying: (or he builds) meaning if the buyer builds in a fasid sale, then he owes the value according to Abu Hanifa, as narrated by Ya'qub from him in al-Jami' al-Saghir, then he doubted the narration later. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: he demolishes the building and returns the house and the planting. This difference is based on...
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage addresses whether a marriage (nikah) contracted by the buyer of a female slave in a fasid sale remains valid when the sale is rescinded. The general rule stated: all verbal dispositions prevent rescission except ijarah (lease) and nikah. Ijarah can be terminated by excuses, and nikah does not transfer ownership. However, there is a specific question: if the slave girl is returned to the seller and the sale is rescinded, does the marriage also dissolve? The text cites al-Siraj al-Wahhaj stating that the marriage does not rescind because it is not terminable by excuses, and the buyer owned her at the time of the marriage. This is supported by al-Fath: the husband's right is only to benefit, not ownership of the corpus, so the seller can reclaim the slave. But this contradicts a statement from al-Walwaliji that if the marriage occurred before possession (qabdh), and the sale is rescinded, the marriage is invalid because the rescission ab initio renders the sale as if it never existed. The text attempts reconciliation: al-Siraj's ruling applies when marriage occurred after possession, while al-Walwaliji's case is before possession. The passage also includes a side comment on tadbir, reiterating that it is valid in a fasid sale, and a note refuting a claim that tadbir is not mentioned. The section on building is repeated, but that belongs to the previous lesson. Overall, the marriage in a fasid sale remains valid after rescission if the buyer had possession, following the view of Muhammad or a distinction based on possession.

Key Terms
تصرف قولي (tasarruf qawli): A verbal disposition, such as sale, gift, emancipation, marriage, etc., which may affect the right of return in a fasid sale.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of the sale, which returns the sold item to the seller.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a female slave purchased in a fasid sale is married by the buyer after possession, and then the sale is rescinded, what happens to the marriage?
A) The marriage is automatically rescinded because the sale is invalid.
B) The marriage remains valid because the buyer owned her at the time of marriage.
C) The marriage becomes a valid nikah only if the seller consents.
D) The marriage is void because the sale was fasid from the start.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, the validity of the buyer's marriage to the sold female slave hinges on possession: after possession the marriage stands; before possession it is void.

Building on Property in a Fasid Sale: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on building and rescission in fasid sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a buyer's building in a fasid sale cuts off the seller's right to reclaim the property, and apply the key distinction of taslit (seller's permission) to similar cases.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: buyer's building stands; Sahibayn: must demolish — the crux is explicit vs. implicit permission.

In Hanafi fiqh, a fasid (corrupt) sale is voidable: the seller can rescind the sale and reclaim the property. But what if the buyer has already built on the land before rescission? The three Hanafi imams differ on whether the building must be demolished or can remain. This passage from al-Bahr al-Rai'iq unpacks the reasoning behind Abu Hanifa's view and the Sahibayn's objection, centering on the concept of taslit (the seller's implied permission by delivery).

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine the act (buyer builds on fasid purchase) and the contested consequence (does building cut off seller's right to reclaim?).
Focus on the fasid sale, not batil; fasid allows rescission.
2
State each imam's position
Abu Hanifa: buyer pays value of building, building remains. Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): building must be demolished, land returned.
Both agree rescission is possible; dispute is on effect of building.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
They analogize to shuf'a (pre-emption): even a weaker right (shuf'a) is not invalidated by building, so the stronger seller's right certainly is not.
Shuf'a is weaker because it requires judicial decree and lapses with delay.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
He distinguishes: building is permanent and done with seller's implied permission (taslit) when delivery occurred, cutting off the right to reclaim, like a valid sale. In shuf'a, no permission from the pre-emptor exists.
Key concept: taslit (authorization) transforms the nature of possession.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, buyer keeps building and pays value; under Sahibayn, buyer demolishes at own cost. Later Hanafi fatwa often prefers the Sahibayn for stronger seller protection.
The dispute is recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah, with doubt on Abu Hanifa's narration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أو يبني) أي إذا بنى المشتري فاسداً فعليه القيمة عند أبي حنيفة رواه عنه يعقوب في الجامع الصغير ثم شك بعد ذلك في الرواية. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد: إنه ينقض البناء وترد الدار والغرس على هذا الإختلاف أن حق الشفيع أضعف من البائع حتى يحتاج فيه إلى القضاء ويبطل بالتأخير بخلاف حق البائع ثم أضعف الحقين لا يبطل بالبناء فأقواهما أولى. وله أن البناء والغرس مما يقصد به الدوام وقد حصل بتسليط من جهة البائع فينقطع حق الاسترداد كالبيع بخلاف حق الشفيع لأنه لم يوجد منه التسليط، ولهذا لم تبطل بهبة المشتري وبيعه فكذا ببثائه. وشك يعقوب في حظ الرواية عن أبي حنيفة، وقد نص محمد على الاختلاف. ولم يذكر المؤلف من الأفعال الحسية إلا البناء قالوا : متى فعل المشتري بالمبيع فعلاً ينقطع به حق المالك في الغصب ينقطع به حق البائع في الاسترداد كما إذا كان حنطه [؟] معه عن بيتاً غير التحفة مثل ما أخذ المنفعة فإن مع الاسترداد النكاح قائم كما لو تزوجها البائع ، نعم يصير بحيث له منعها وعدم تبوئتها أنه إن ظفر بها له وطؤها اهـ. وهو صريح بعدم الانفساخ وصرح به أيضاً في غاية البيان وفي التبين ومثله في المجتبى حيث قال : إلا الإجارة وتزويج الجارية لكن الإجارة تنفسخ بالاسترداد دون النكاح .اهـ. وقال في التتارخانية نقلاً عن نوادر ابن سماعة : وعنه أيضاً فمن اشترى جارية شراء فاسداً وقبضها المشتري وزوجها من رجل ثم فسخ البيع بينهما بحكم الفساد وأخذها البائع مع ما نقصها التزويج ثم إن الزوج طلقها قبل الدخول بها كان على البائع أن يرد على المشتري النقصان قال : ألا من تری أنه لو لم يكن نقصان تزويج ولكن ابيضت إحدى عينيها في يد المشتري ثم إن المشتري ردها ورد معها نصف القيمة ثم ذهب البياض وعاد إلى الحال الأولى فإن البائع يرد على المشتري ما أخذ من نصف القيمة وطريقه ما قلنا .اهـ. ففيه مع إفادته بقاء النكاح فائدة أخرى فهذه نصوص كتب المذهب موافقة لما قاله في السراج .
His statement: 'or builds' — meaning if the buyer builds on a fasid purchase, he must pay the value according to Abu Hanifa, related by Ya'qub in al-Jami' al-Saghir, then he doubted the narration. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: he must demolish the building and return the house and the planting. The basis of this difference: that the right of the pre-emptor is weaker than that of the seller, as it requires judicial decree and is invalidated by delay, unlike the seller's right; the weaker of the two rights is not nullified by building, so the stronger is more entitled. His proof: that building and planting are intended for permanence, and they occurred with permission from the seller, so the right of reclaiming is cut off, like a sale; unlike the pre-emptor's right because no permission issued from him. Therefore, the buyer's gift and sale do not invalidate it, so also his building. Ya'qub doubted in the narration from Abu Hanifa, and Muhammad stated the difference. The author only mentioned building among physical acts. They said: whenever the buyer performs an act on the sold item that would cut off the owner's right in usurpation, it cuts off the seller's right to reclaim; for example, if he makes it into flour [؟] with him from a house not the masterpiece, like taking the benefit; then with the reclaim, the marriage remains as if the seller married her. Yes, he becomes such that he can prevent her and not give her a dwelling, and if he gets hold of her, he can have intercourse with her. This is explicit that it does not dissolve. He also stated it explicitly in Ghayat al-Bayan and al-Tabyin, and similarly in al-Mujtaba where he said: except for ijarah and marriage of the slavegirl, but ijarah dissolves with reclaim, not marriage. And he said in al-Tatar Khaniyya, quoting from Nawadir Ibn Sama'a: And also from him: Whoever buys a slavegirl in a fasid sale, takes delivery, marries her to a man, then the sale is rescinded due to its corruption, and the seller takes her back with the depreciation of marriage; then the husband divorces her before consummation; the seller must compensate the buyer for the depreciation. He said: consider if there was no depreciation of marriage but one of her eyes turned white in the buyer's hand, then the buyer returns her and pays half the value, then the whiteness goes away and it returns to its original state; then the seller must return what he took of half the value to the buyer. Its method is what we said. So in it, besides indicating the continuance of marriage, there is another benefit. These are texts of the madhhab consistent with what he said in al-Siraj.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling when a buyer builds on property purchased in a fasid (corrupt) sale. The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq says 'or builds,' and the commentator explains. At issue: does the buyer's construction cut off the seller's right to recover the property upon rescission? Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer must pay the value of the building but the building stands, and the seller cannot demand demolition. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the building must be demolished and the land returned. The commentator then provides the reasoning: the Sahibayn analogize to the right of pre-emption (shuf'a), which is weaker than the seller's right but is still not invalidated by building. Abu Hanifa argues that building is intended for permanence and the seller implicitly permitted it by delivering the item, so the seller's right to reclaim is cut off, similar to a valid sale. The commentary then extends this to other physical acts that would cut off the usufructuary's right in usurpation (ghasb). The passage further provides supporting evidence from the marriage of a slavegirl, citing numerous texts to show that marriage remains valid after rescission, which parallels the building case. The overall message is that certain acts by the buyer in a fasid sale, done with the seller's permission (by delivery), prevent the seller from revoking the transaction entirely.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is deficient in a pillar or condition but can be rectified or rescinded; the sale is voidable, not void ab initio.
الاسترداد (al-istirdad): The seller's right to reclaim the sold item upon rescission of a fasid sale.
التسليط (al-taslit): The seller's authorization to the buyer to take possession and use the item.
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): Pre-emption right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase property sold to a third party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis of Abu Hanifa's view that the buyer's building cuts off the seller's right to reclaim the property?
A) The building is more valuable than the land.
B) The seller delivered the property, implying permission for the buyer's use.
C) The buyer acted in good faith.
D) The Sahibayn's analogy to shuf'a is flawed.

Key Takeaway
The dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on building in a fasid sale hinges on whether the seller's implied permission (taslit) cuts off the right to reclaim, with later fatwa often favoring the Sahibayn.

Buyer's Right to Retain in Fasid Sale
Ruling statement with reasoning and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for the buyer's right to retain in a fasid sale and identify the condition (price paid) and exception (pre-existing debt).

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, the buyer may retain the item until the seller returns the price paid, like a pledge.

When a fasid sale is rescinded, the buyer who paid the price often wonders about his position. The Hanafi school grants him a retention right, treating the sold item as a security until the seller refunds the price. This lesson clarifies the scope and limits of that right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a buyer in a fasid sale after rescission. The ruling: he may retain the sold item until he receives his paid price.
This is a right, not an obligation.
2
Understand the condition
The right exists only if the buyer actually paid the price in cash. If the price was a pre-existing debt, there is no retention right.
The price must have been paid specifically for this sale.
3
Grasp the analogy to rahn
The item becomes like a pledge in the buyer's hand because it is countervalue for the price. This gives priority over the seller's heirs and creditors.
The analogy is explicitly made in the text.
4
Extend to analogous contracts
The same right applies in fasid ijarah, fasid rahn, and fasid qard with a pledge—wherever a price or equivalent was paid.
The text lists these applications.
5
Recognize the exception
If the buyer already owed the seller a debt unrelated to this sale, he cannot retain the item for that debt. The item is not exchanged for that debt.
This prevents abuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وله أن يمنع المبيع عن البائع حتى يأخذ الثمن أي للمشتري المنع بعد فسخ البيع لأن المبيع مقابل به فيصير محبوساً به كالرهن أشار المؤلف إلى أن البائع إذا مات كان المشتري أحق به حتى يستوفي الثمن لأنه يقدم عليه في حياته فكذا على ورثته وغرمائه بعد وفاته كالراهن وإلى أنه لو استأجر إجازة فاسدة ونقد الأجرة أو ارتهن رهناً فاسداً أو أقرض قرضاً فاسداً وأخذ به رهناً كان له أن يحبس ما استأجر وما ارتهن حتى يقبض ما نقد اعتباراً للعقد الجائز إذا تفاسخا، وكذا لو مات المؤجر أو الراهن أو المستقرض فهو أحق بما في يده من العين من سائر الغرماء، وإلى أن الثمن لو لم يكن منقود للبائع وإنما كان ديناً له على المشتري فليس له الحبس قالوا لو اشترى من مدينة عبداً بدين سابق له عليه شراء فاسداً وقبض العبد بإذن البائع فأراد البائع استرداد العبد بحكم الفساد ليس للمشتري أن يحبس.
His statement: 'And he has the right to prevent the sold item from the seller until he takes the price' — meaning the buyer has the right to prevent (retain) after rescission of the sale because the sold item is countervalue for it, so it becomes imprisoned thereby like a pledge. The author indicated that if the seller dies, the buyer has more right to it until he receives the price, because he had priority over him in his life, so likewise over his heirs and creditors after his death, like the pledgor. And that if he took a lease with a fasid contract and paid the rent, or pawned a fasid pledge, or gave a fasid loan and took a pledge, he has the right to retain the leased item and the pawn until he receives what he paid, by analogy with a valid contract when they annul. Similarly, if the lessor, pawner, or borrower dies, he has more right to the item in his hand over other creditors. And that if the price was not paid to the seller but was a debt owed to him by the buyer, he has no right to retain. They said: if he bought a slave from Madina on a pre-existing debt in a fasid sale, and took delivery of the slave with the seller's permission, then the seller wants to reclaim the slave due to the corruption, the buyer has no right to retain.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage states the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq that the buyer in a fasid sale has the right to retain the purchased item until the seller refunds the price he paid. The commentator explains the reasoning: because the item is the countervalue for the price, it becomes like a pledge (rahn) in the buyer's hand. If the seller dies, the buyer is preferred over other creditors for the item until he recovers his price, just as a pledgee is preferred. The same applies to analogous contracts: fasid ijarah, fasid rahn, and fasid qard with a pledge. However, if the price was not paid in cash but was a pre-existing debt owed by the buyer to the seller, the buyer has no right to retain the item, because the item is not a specific countervalue for that debt. The passage gives an example: buying a slave from someone who is already owed a debt by the buyer; if the sale is fasid, the buyer cannot retain the slave when the seller demands it back.

Key Terms
الحبس (al-habs): The buyer's right to retain possession of the sold item until the seller returns the price paid.
الدين السابق (al-dayn al-sabiq): A pre-existing debt owed by the buyer to the seller, which is not the price of the current fasid sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale where the buyer paid the price, what right does the buyer have after rescission?
A) The buyer must immediately return the item to the seller.
B) The buyer may retain the item until the seller refunds the price.
C) The buyer may keep the item without paying anything extra.
D) The buyer has no rights; the seller can take the item by force.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, the buyer who paid cash may retain the item like a pledge until refunded, but not for pre-existing debts.

Buyer's Right to Withhold in Void Sale
Stating the buyer's right to withhold in a void sale with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi ruling on the buyer's right to withhold in a void sale and DISTINGUISH between cases where the price was cash versus a debt.

Core Idea
In a void sale, the buyer may hold the item until the seller returns the cash price, but not if the price was a pre-existing debt.

After a void sale is annulled, a practical question arises: who keeps the item until the seller returns the price? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi position, which grants the buyer a right to withhold the item as a form of security, analogous to a pledge. However, this right is not absolute—it depends on how the price was paid, introducing a critical distinction between cash and debt.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the ruling: after annulment of a void sale where the buyer paid cash, the buyer may physically prevent the seller from taking the sold item until the seller returns the price.
The rule is derived by analogy to a pledge (rahn).
2
Find the proof (dalil)
The proof is qiyas: the sold item, held in exchange for the price, is like a pledge. Just as a pledgee may withhold the pledged item until the debt is paid, the buyer may withhold until the price is returned.
This is a jaliyy (clear) analogy.
3
Identify the exception
If the price was not paid in cash but was a pre-existing debt owed by the buyer to the seller, the buyer cannot withhold the item. The seller may reclaim it without any payment.
The reason lies in the impossibility of set-off (muqassah) in void sales.
4
Understand the rationale
In a valid sale, set-off can occur because both obligations are fixed and of the same type. In a void sale, the seller's claim is for the value of the item (which is not fixed), so set-off fails, and the buyer gains no right to withhold.
The commentary extends this rule to void leases and pledges as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله أن يمنع المبيع عن البائع حتى يأخذ الثمن أي للمشترى المنع بعد فسخ البيع لأن المبيع مقابل به فيصير محبوساً به كالرهن أشار المؤلف إلى أن البائع إذا مات كان المشتري أحق به حتى يستوفي الثمن لأنه يقدم عليه في حياته فكذا على ورثته وغرمائه بعد وفاته كالراهن وإلى أنه لو استأجر إجازة فاسدة ونقد الأجرة أو ارتهن رهناً فاسداً أو أقرض قرضاً فاسداً وأخذ به رهناً كان له أن يحبس ما استأجر وما ارتهن حتى يقبض ما نقد اعتباراً للعقد الجائز إذا تفاسخا، وكذا لو مات المؤجر أو الراهن أو المستقرض فهو أحق بما في يده من العين من سائر الغرماء، وإلى أن الثمن لو لم يكن منقود للبائع وإنما كان ديناً له على المشتري فليس له الحبس قالوا لو اشترى من مدينة عبداً بدين سابق له عليه شراء فاسداً وقبض العبد بإذن البائع فأراد البائع استرداد العبد بحكم الفساد ليس للمشتري أن يحبس العبد لاستيفاء ماله عليه من الدين بخلاف الصحيح، وله أن يسترد العبد قبل إيفاء الأجرة وليس للمستأجر الحبس بالأجرة بخلاف الصحيح، وكذا الرهن الفاسد لو كان بدين سابق. والفرق أن البيع إذا أضيف للدراهم لا يتعلق الملك في الثمن بمجرد العقد فإذا وجب للمديون على المشتري مثل الدين صار الثمن قصاصاً لاستوائهما قدراً ووصفاً فيصير البائع مستوفياً ثمنه بطريق المقاصة فاعتبر بما لو استوفاه حقيقة وثم للشتري حتى حبس المبيع إلى أن يستوفي الثمن فكذا هذا وفي الفاسد لم يملك الثمن بل تجب قيمة المبيع عند القبض والقيمة قبل القبض غير مقررة لاحتمالها السقوط كل ساعة بالفسخ، ولأن القيمة قد تكون من جنس الدين وقد لا تكون ودين المشتري على البائع مقرر والمقاصصه إنما تكون عند استواء الواجبين وصفاً ولذا لا تجب المقاصصة بين الحال والمؤجل والجيد والرديء، وإذا لم تقع المقاصصة لم يصر البائع مستوفياً الثمن أصلاً فلا يكون للمشتري حق حبس المبيع بعد فسخ البيع. ولو كان الرهن باطلاً بأن استقرض ألفاً ورهن أم ولد أو مديراً له أن قضاء الدين لعدم الانعقاد والكل من الكافي شرح الوافي وإلى أن الثمن لو كان دراهم وهي قائمة فإنه يأخذها بعينها لأنها تتعين في البيع الفاسد وهو الأصح لأنه بمنزلة الغصب، وإن كانت مستهلكة أخذ مثلها لما بينا؛ كذا في الهداية .
He has the right to prevent the seller from taking the sold item until he takes the price, meaning the buyer has the right to prevent after annulment of the sale because the sold item is in exchange for it, thus it becomes held in custody like a pledge. The author indicated that if the seller dies, the buyer has more right to it until he receives the price because he had priority during his life, similarly against his heirs and creditors after death like a pledgor. And that if one hires a void lease and pays the hire, or receives a void pledge, or lends a void loan and takes a pledge, he has the right to withhold the leased property and pledged item until he receives what he paid, by analogy to a valid contract when they mutually rescind. Similarly, if the lessor, pledgor, or borrower dies, he has more right to the property in his hand than other creditors. And that if the price was not paid to the seller but was a debt owed to him by the buyer, then he has no right to withhold. They said: if one buys from his debtor a slave for a pre-existing debt in a void purchase, takes possession of the slave with the seller's permission, then the seller wants to reclaim the slave due to voidness, the buyer does not have the right to withhold the slave to collect his debt, unlike in a valid sale. He may reclaim the slave before payment of the hire, and the lessee has no right to withhold for the hire, unlike a valid contract. Similarly, a void pledge if it was for a pre-existing debt. The difference is that in a sale tied to dirhams, ownership of the price is not established by mere contract; when the debtor owes the buyer something like the debt, the price becomes set off because they are equal in amount and description, so the seller becomes satisfied for his price by way of set-off, and it is considered as if he actually received it, and then the buyer had the right to withhold the sold item until he receives the price; similarly here. In a void sale, the seller does not own the price; rather, the value of the sold item at the time of receipt is owed, and the value before receipt is not fixed because it may fall at any moment due to annulment, and because the value may or may not be of the same type as the debt, and the buyer's debt to the seller is fixed, and set-off only occurs when both obligations are equal in description, hence set-off is not required between prompt and deferred, good and bad; and when set-off does not occur, the seller is not considered to have received the price at all, so the buyer has no right to withhold the sold item after annulment of the sale. If the pledge was invalid, e.g., he borrowed a thousand and pledged an umm walad or a mudabbar, he may collect the debt due to lack of validity of the pledge. All this is from al-Kafi Sharh al-Wafi. And that if the price was dirhams and they are still in existence, he takes them specifically because they are defined in a void sale, and this is the more correct view because it is like usurpation; if they are consumed, he takes their like as we explained; as in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the buyer's right to withhold the sold item in a void sale until the seller repays the price. The principle is that after annulment of a void sale, the buyer can retain the sold item as a form of custody (habs) until he receives the price he paid. This is by analogy to a pledge. The commentary extends this to other void contracts like lease and pledge. However, if the price was a pre-existing debt owed by the buyer to the seller, the buyer does not have this right; the seller can reclaim the item without payment because set-off does not operate in void sales. The difference between valid and void sales is explained: in a valid sale, ownership of the price is established by contract, and set-off can occur, so the buyer's right to withhold exists both before and after annulment. In a void sale, the seller only has a claim for the value of the item, which is not fixed, and set-off is not applicable, so the buyer cannot withhold.

Key Terms
الحبس (al-habs): The right of the buyer to retain physical possession of the sold item after annulment until the seller returns the price.
المقاصة (al-muqassah): Set-off of mutual debts; requires equality in type, amount, and description.
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is valid in essence but defective in description; requires annulment but not completely void.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a void sale, the buyer paid 100 dirhams in cash. After annulment, the seller refuses to return the cash. Which statement is correct?
A) The buyer must return the item and sue for the cash.
B) The buyer may keep the item until the cash is returned.
C) The buyer may keep the item only if the seller dies.
D) The buyer may keep the item but must pay rent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the buyer's right to withhold in a void sale is contingent on the price being paid in cash; a debt price does not permit withholding.

Increments in a Void Sale
Explaining the legal treatment of increments in a void sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify increments in a void sale (attached/generated) and state the legal ruling for each type.

Core Idea
In a void sale, attached non-generated increments stay with the seller; separate generated increments are liable if destroyed.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rules for increments (zawa'id) that arise in a void sale. The classification of these increments—whether they are attached or separate, generated or non-generated—determines who bears the loss and what the seller can reclaim. Understanding this distinction is crucial for resolving disputes when a void sale is annulled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the increment's connection
Determine if the increment is attached (muttasil) or separate (munfasil). Examples: dyeing is attached; offspring is separate.
2
Determine if it is generated
Is it an independent creation (mutawallid) like fruit, or a non-generated improvement like sewing?
3
Apply the corresponding ruling
For attached non-generated: does not prevent annulment, seller takes with item but it is not pure for him. For separate generated: buyer liable if he destroys them (dispute). For separate non-generated: seller takes with item but not pure.
Refer to the text for details.
4
Handle perishment scenarios
If the sold item perishes but increments survive, rules differ for generated vs non-generated. For separate generated, increments become buyer's property; for non-generated, seller can take them.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa holds no liability for destroying separate generated increments; the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold liability. The mufta bihi is the view of the two.
This dispute is based on whether possession in void sale is trust or guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زوائد المبيع فاسداً لا تمنع الفسخ إلا متصلة لم تتولد كصبغ وخياطة و لت سويق ولو منفصله له متولدة تضمن بالتعدي لا بدونه ولو هلك المبيع لا المتولدة فللبائع أخذ الزوائد وقيمة المبيع ولو منفصلة غير متولدة فله أخذ المبيع مع هذه الزوائد ولا تطيب له ولو هلكت في يد المشتري لم يضمن، ولو أهلكها ضمن عندهما لا عند أبي حنيفة . ويماثلها زوائد الغصب. ولو هلك المبيع لا الزوائد فهي للمشترى بخلاف المتولدة كما يفترقان في الغصب فيضمن قيمة المبيع فقط، وأما حكم نقصانه فلو نقص في يد المشتري بآفة سماوية فللبائع أخذه مع أرش نقصه، وكذا لو بفعل المشتري أو المبيع. ولو بفعل البائع صار مسترداً . لو هلك عند المشتري ولم يوجد منه حبس عن البائع هلك على البائع ولو بفعل أجنبي يخير البائع إن شاء أخذه . الجاني وإن شاء اتبع الجاني وهو لا يرجع على المشتري كالغصب اهـ .
Increments of the sold item in a void sale do not prevent annulment except if they are attached and not generated, like dyeing, sewing, and pounding of sawiq. If they are separate and generated, he is liable for them by transgression, not without. If the sold item perishes but not the generated increments, the seller may take the increments and the value of the sold item. If the increments are separate and not generated, he may take the sold item with these increments, and they are not lawful for him. If they perish in the buyer's hand, he is not liable; if he destroys them, he is liable according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), not according to Abu Hanifa. This is similar to increments of usurped property. If the sold item perishes but not the increments, they belong to the buyer, unlike the generated ones, just as they differ in usurpation: he only pays the value of the sold item. As for the ruling on its deficiency: if it is deficient in the buyer's hand by a heavenly calamity, the seller may take it with compensation for the deficiency; similarly if by the buyer's or the sold item's act. If by the seller's act, it becomes reclaimed. If it perishes with the buyer and there is no detention from the seller, it perishes at the seller's expense. If by a stranger, the seller may choose: if he wishes, he takes the offender, and if he wishes, he follows the offender, and the offender does not recourse against the buyer, like usurpation.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage details the rules for increments (zawa'id) and deficiencies (nuqsan) in a void sale. Increments that are attached and not generated (like dyeing, sewing) do not prevent annulment; the seller can reclaim the sold item along with these increments but the increments are not pure for him (he should give them in charity). If the increments are separate and generated (e.g., offspring), the buyer is liable for them only if he acted wrongfully (ta'addi). If the sold item perishes, the seller can claim the increments and the value of the item. If the increments perish in the buyer's possession without his fault, he is not liable; if he destroys them, Abu Hanifa holds he is not liable (since the possession is trust), while the two disciples say he is liable (since possession in void sale is like guarantee). This mirrors the rules for usurpation. For deficiencies, if the item decreases in value due to an act of God, the buyer can return it with compensation for the loss; if the buyer caused the loss, he pays the difference; if the seller caused it, he can take it back without compensation. If the item perishes while with the buyer without any wrongful act from him, the loss falls on the seller (since the buyer was in trust possession). If a third party destroys it, the seller can choose to demand damages from the third party or take the item and then the third party pays; the third party cannot recourse against the buyer.

Key Terms
الزوائد (al-zawa'id): Increments that accrue to the sold item, such as offspring, fruit, or improvements like dyeing.
المتبدل (al-mutabaddal): Generated increments (e.g., offspring); they are considered independent property.
أرش النقص (arsh al-naqs): Compensation for the reduction in value due to deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If separate generated increments are destroyed by the buyer in a void sale, what is the mufta bihi position?
A) The buyer is not liable (Abu Hanifa).
B) The buyer is liable (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
C) The buyer is not liable according to all.
D) The buyer is liable only if he acted wrongfully.

Key Takeaway
In a void sale, increments are classified by connection and generation; the buyer's liability differs based on type and destruction.
