Deficiencies in a Void Sale
Explaining the legal treatment of deficiencies in a void sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for deficiency compensation in a void sale.

Core Idea
In a void sale, the seller bears deficiency from acts of God; the buyer compensates if he causes it.

This lesson covers the treatment of deficiencies (nuqsan) that occur to the sold item in a void sale. The key is identifying who caused the deficiency—heavenly calamity, buyer, seller, or a third party—and applying the corresponding rule of compensation or loss. Understanding these distinctions prevents disputes when the sale is annulled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause of deficiency
Is it a heavenly calamity (act of God), an action of the buyer, an action of the seller, or an action of a stranger?
2
Apply the ruling for heavenly calamity
If deficiency occurs by act of God (e.g., flood, fire), the seller may take the item back with compensation for the deficiency (arsh al-naqs).
3
Apply the ruling for buyer's or item's own act
If the buyer causes the deficiency (e.g., breaks it) or the item itself causes its own deficiency (e.g., animal dies), same as heavenly calamity: seller takes with compensation.
4
Apply the ruling for seller's act
If the seller causes the deficiency after the sale, the item is considered reclaimed (mustaradd) and the seller takes it back without compensation.
5
Handle perishment or third-party destruction
If the item perishes with the buyer and no detention by seller, loss falls on seller. If a third party destroys it, seller can choose to take compensation from the third party or take the item and the third party pays; the third party cannot recourse against the buyer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If by the seller's act, it becomes reclaimed. If it perishes with the buyer and there is no detention from the seller, it perishes at the seller's expense. If by a stranger, the seller may choose: if he wishes, he takes the offender, and if he wishes, he follows the offender, and the offender does not recourse against the buyer, like usurpation.

This passage details the rules for increments (zawa'id) and deficiencies (nuqsan) in a void sale. Increments that are attached and not generated (like dyeing, sewing) do not prevent annulment; the seller can reclaim the sold item along with these increments but the increments are not pure for him (he should give them in charity). Key Terms
أرش النقص (arsh al-naqs): Compensation for the reduction in value due to deficiency.
الحبس (al-habs): Detention of the item by the seller, which would shift liability for perishment to the seller.
المسترد (al-mustaradd): Item that becomes reclaimed (by the seller) when he causes the deficiency.

Buyer's Right to Detain Sold Item in Defective Sale
Explains the difference in detention rights between valid and defective sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the buyer cannot detain the sold item for a debt in a defective sale and distinguish this from a valid sale.

Core Idea
In a defective sale, no set-off occurs, so the buyer has no right to detain the sold item.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a critical distinction between valid and defective sales: the buyer's right to detain the sold item for a debt owed by the seller. In a valid sale, the price is owned immediately, enabling set-off (muqassa) and giving the buyer a security-like right of detention. But in a defective sale (bay' fasid), ownership of the price does not transfer, no set-off occurs, and the buyer loses that right. We will follow the reasoning (ta'lil) behind this rule, which relies on istihsan (juridical preference) to differentiate the two cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the buyer's detention (habs) of the sold item for a debt the seller owes him. The ruling: in a valid sale, the buyer may detain; in a defective sale, he may not.
Focus on the key difference: ownership of the price.
2
Locate the dalil
The text uses rational reasoning (istihsan) rather than a primary scriptural source. The proof is the logical distinction in the nature of price ownership between valid and defective sales.
This is a ta'lil (causal reasoning) argument.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In a valid sale, the price (thaman) is owned immediately upon contract. If the buyer owes the seller a similar debt, the price is set off (muqassa) as if paid. Thus, the seller becomes a creditor for the price, and the buyer can detain the item until payment. In a defective sale, the seller does not own the price; only the market value (qimah) at receipt is owed, which is uncertain and may differ from the debt. Hence, no set-off, and no detention right.
The key factor is the certainty and equality of the two obligations.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in a valid sale, the buyer can secure his debt by detaining the item; in a defective sale, the seller can reclaim the item immediately, and the buyer cannot hold it.
This applies to defective rent and defective pawn as well.
5
Evaluate the ruling and its exceptions
The text also notes that if the price (e.g., dirhams) still exists, the seller takes them in kind because currency becomes determinate in a defective sale (most correct view). If consumed, a similar amount is owed.
This is an exception to the general rule, likened to usurpation (ghasb).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب البيع الفاسد العبد لاستيفاء ماله عليه من الدين بخلاف الصحيح، وله أن يسترد العبد قبل إيفاء الأجرة وليس للمستأجر الحبس بالأجرة بخلاف الصحيح، وكذا الرهن الفاسد لو كان بدين سابق. والفرق أن البيع إذا أضيف للدراهم لا يتعلق الملك في الثمن بمجرد العقد فإذا وجب للمديون على المشتري مثل الدين صار الثمن قصاصاً لاستوائهما قدراً ووصفاً فيصير البائع مستوفياً ثمنه بطريق المقاصة فاعتبر بما لو استوفاه حقيقة وثم للشتري حتى حبس المبيع إلى أن يستوفي الثمن فكذا هذا وفي الفاسد لم يملك الثمن بل تجب قيمة المبيع عند القبض والقيمة قبل القبض غير مقررة لاحتمالها السقوط كل ساعة بالفسخ، ولأن القيمة قد تكون من جنس الدين وقد لا تكون ودين المشتري على البائع مقرر والمقاصصه إنما تكون عند استواء الواجبين وصفاً ولذا لا تجب المقاصصة بين الحال والمؤجل والجيد والرديء، وإذا لم تقع المقاصصة لم يصر البائع مستوفياً الثمن أصلاً فلا يكون للمشتري حق حبس المبيع بعد يسترد قبل فسخ البيع. ولو كان الرهن باطلاً بأن استقرض ألفاً ورهن أم ولد أو مديراً له أن قضاء الدين لعدم الانعقاد والكل من الكافي شرح الوافي وإلى أن الثمن لو كان دراهم وهي قائمة فإنه يأخذها بعينها لأنها تتعين في البيع الفاسد وهو الأصح لأنه بمنزلة الغصب، وإن كانت مستهلكة أخذ مثلها لما بينا؛ كذا في الهداية .
Chapter on Sale, Section on Defective Sale. The slave (is taken) to recover what is owed to him (the seller) of debt, unlike the valid (sale). And he has the right to reclaim the slave before payment of the rent, and the lessee does not have the right to detain for the rent, unlike the valid (sale). Similarly, a defective pawn (rahn) if it was for a previous debt. The difference is that if the sale is added to dirhams, ownership in the price is not established by the mere contract. So if what is owed to the debtor from the buyer is similar to the debt, the price becomes a set-off (qisas) due to equality in quantity and description, so the seller becomes receiving his price by way of set-off; it is considered as if he actually received it. And then the buyer has the right to detain the sold item until he receives the price; thus this (is the rule). In the defective (sale), he does not own the price, but rather the value of the sold item at the time of receipt is obligated, and the value before receipt is not established due to the possibility of dropping every moment by cancellation. Also, the value may be of the same type as the debt or not, and the buyer's debt to the seller is established; set-off only occurs when the two obligations are equal in description, so set-off is not obligatory between immediate and deferred, and good and bad. If set-off does not occur, the seller does not become a recipient of the price at all, so the buyer has no right to detain the sold item after it is reclaimed before cancellation of the sale. And if the pawn was void (batil), such as borrowing a thousand and pawning an umm walad or a mudabbar, he has (the right) to pay the debt because of non-conclusion (of the contract). All of this is from al-Kafi sharh al-Wafi. And (it is mentioned) that if the price is dirhams and they are still in existence, he takes them in kind because they become determinate in a defective sale, and this is the most correct view because it is like usurpation. If they are consumed, he takes a similar amount as we have explained. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school on the difference between a valid and defective sale regarding the buyer's right to detain the sold item for his debt. The author explains that in a valid sale, if the buyer owes the seller a debt similar to the price, the seller can detain the sold item until payment, because the price is set off against the debt immediately. In a defective sale, the seller does not own the price; instead, the market value at the time of receipt is owed, which is uncertain and may differ from the debt. Therefore, no set-off occurs, and the buyer has no right of detention. The same applies to defective rent and defective pawn. Finally, if the price is dirhams that still exist, the seller takes them in kind because currency becomes determinate in a defective sale according to the most correct view, likening it to usurpation (ghasb). If consumed, a similar amount is taken.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is defective due to missing a condition of validity, such as uncertainty or illegality, but still has some legal effects.
المقاصة (al-muqassa): Set-off: cancellation of mutual debts between two parties when equal in type and description.
التعين (al-ta'ayyun): Determination: the process by which a generic item (like dirhams) becomes specified as a particular item in a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the buyer not have the right to detain the sold item in a defective sale for a debt owed to the seller?
A) Because the contract is void from the beginning.
B) Because the seller does not own the price immediately, so no set-off occurs.
C) Because the buyer's debt is not yet due.
D) Because the seller has already received the price in full.

Key Takeaway
In a defective sale, the buyer cannot detain the sold item for a debt because the price is not owned immediately, preventing set-off (muqassa).

12 min
Profit in Defective Sale and Determination of Currency
Resolves the intra-Hanafi dispute on profit and currency determination in defective sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on profit in a defective sale for seller and buyer, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether currency becomes determinate in such a sale.

Core Idea
In a defective sale, seller's profit on price is permissible; buyer's profit on sold item requires charity.

This lesson tackles a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute about the determination of currency in a defective sale. The basic ruling is clear: the seller's profit on the price is permissible, while the buyer's profit on the sold item is not, so the buyer must give charity. But why the difference? The answer lies in whether currency becomes determinate in such contracts. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq refines the earlier discussions, reconciling apparent contradictions in the transmitted views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
State the ruling for the seller and the buyer regarding profit in a defective sale: seller's profit on price is permissible; buyer's profit on sold item is not, so buyer gives charity.
This is from al-Jami' al-Saghir.
2
Understand the distinction between price and sold item
Recognize that the sold item is determinate (muta'ayyin), so the contract attaches to it, allowing malignancy (khubth) to take effect. Currency is non-determinate in commutative contracts, so no malignancy attaches to the price.
This is the root of the difference.
3
Analyze the two types of malignancy
Distinguish between malignancy due to non-ownership (ghasb) and malignancy due to defective ownership (fasid). The former is stronger; the latter is a mere semblance (shubhah).
This hierarchy determines whether profit is permissible.
4
Examine the dispute on determination of currency
Some Hanafi scholars hold that currency becomes determinate in a defective sale, requiring the seller to give charity on his profit. Others, including the relied-upon view, say it does not, so the seller's profit is pure.
The apparent contradiction is resolved by considering two aspects: return of the specific thing vs. profit.
5
Synthesize the reconciled position
Understand that currency is determinate for the purpose of returning the specific thing (like usurpation), but not determinate for the purpose of profit (like a valid sale). Thus, seller's profit is permissible.
This is the mufta bihi position.

قوله: (وطاب للبائع ما ربح لا للمشتري أي طاب للبائع ما ربحه في ثمن الفاسد ولا يطيب للمشتري ربح المبيع فلا يتصدق الأول ويتصدق المشتري. والفرق أن المبيع مما يتعين فتعلق العقد به فتمكن الخبث فيه والنقد لا His statement: 'And permissible for the seller is what he profited, not for the buyer' means that what the seller profited in the price of the defective (sale) is permissible, and the buyer's profit from the sold item is not permissible. So the former does not give charity, and the buyer gives charity. The difference is that the sold item is something determinate, so the contract attaches to it, thus malignancy (khubth) becomes effective in it. Currency does not become determinate in commutative contracts, so the second contract did not attach to its specific object, so malignancy did not become effective, thus charity is not obligatory. Restricted to defective sale because what the usurper (ghasib) profited belongs to the buyer. His statement: 'unlike the valid (sale)' is omitted from the copies, and the phrasing in al-Zaylai is as follows:... And his statement 'unlike the valid' means that if the sale was valid or the rent was valid, then the contract is cancelled with (the buyer having) the right to detain the sold item until he recovers the debt owed by the seller... The difference: the difference between the valid and the defective contract. His statement: 'because the malignancy in the first' i.e. in the defective, and his statement 'in the second' i.e. in usurpation, and its clarification in the commentaries of al-Hidayah, and the wording of Idah al-Islah by Ibn al-Kamal. The basis for this is that wealth is of two types: a type that does not become determinate in contracts like dirhams and dinars, and a type that becomes determinate like goods. Malignancy is also of two types: one based on ownership and the other on defective ownership. So malignancy based on non-ownership as in usurpation causes the reality of malignancy in what is determinate, and the semblance (shubhah) of malignancy is non-, and the depositary after paying the guarantee is not permissible for him absolutely according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf, because malignancy in the first is due to defective ownership, and in the second due to its non-existence for the attachment of the contract in what is determinate as reality, and in what is not determinate as semblance in that the safety of the sold item or estimation of the price attaches. When ownership is defective, reality turns into a semblance and the semblance descends to a semblance of a semblance. The semblance is considered, not what is lower. Then know that their statement following al-Jami' al-Saghir that profit is permissible for the seller in the price cash is evidence that currency does not become determinate in a defective sale according to the most correct view, and their statement that it becomes determinate according to the most correct view contradicts it. If one considers the correction of determination, then charity upon the seller is obligatory, and the transmission is contrary. I have not seen anyone who clarified it from the commentators, and it became clear to me that there is no contradiction between them. They said previously that it becomes determinate according to the most correct view in terms of the obligation to return the specific thing taken, and they said here it does not become determinate in terms of the permissibility of his profit. So it is determinate from the perspective of defective ownership like usurped property, and not determinate from the perspective that defective commutative contracts are like valid ones. So consider: the first aspect is for the necessity of returning the specific thing received, and the second for the permissibility of his profit. It was not reversed because of the evidence of Abu Yusuf: 'al-kharaj bi al-daman'. Its meaning as in al-Faiq and al-Qamus: the yield of the slave belongs to the buyer if he returns it after discovery of the defect, due to a cause in what is not determinate according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad... Then his statement: 'Then know that their statement etc.' said in al-Nahr: This is only sound according to the transmission of non-determination of currency, and it has passed that the transmission of determination is the most correct. So then the most correct is the obligation of charity upon the seller for what he profited, except that the detail given in the book is the explicit transmission in al-Jami' al-Saghir, and then the most correct is that dirhams do not become determinate in the defective (sale); thus in al-Fath summarized. Sadr al-Shari'ah said: Reconciliation is possible by saying that this contract has two semblances: a semblance to usurpation and a semblance to sale. If the (dirhams) are still existent, the semblance of usurpation is considered in an effort to lift the defective contract; if they are not existent and he bought something with them, the semblance of sale is considered up to the defect to its substitute. Ya'qub Pasha said: This reconciliation only provides evidence for the issue; it is not subject to what is criticized. So the appropriate thing to say is that the statement of the author of al-Hidayah on the last issue is according to the correct transmission, not that it becomes determinate in a defective sale, as indicated in al-'Inayah, unless it is said that the one who says determination which is the most correct means determination in the case when they are existent, not absolute determination, but in the defective (sale) it is contrary to what they stated. And his phrase in al-'Inayah: 'This is only consistent with the correct transmission which is that they do not become determinate, not the most correct which is that they become determinate.' Said in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah: There is discussion in this; for non-determination whether in usurped property or the price of a defective sale is (only) in the second contract and does not harm its determination in the first. So his statement 'it is only consistent' has something in it. And the author of al-Bahr adopted the statement of Ya'qub Pasha except that it is said... And what he answered with in...
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the commentary on the rule that in a defective sale, the seller's profit from the price is permissible while the buyer's profit from the sold item is not. The author examines the distinction between currency (naqd) and goods ('urud) and the nature of malignancy (khubth) and its semblance. He then navigates the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf regarding the depositary's profit, and the apparent contradiction in the transmitted views on whether currency becomes determinate in a defective sale. The resolution offered is that currency is determinate for the purpose of returning the specific thing, but not determinate for the purpose of the profit being permissible. The discussion continues with attempts at reconciliation by later scholars such as Sadr al-Shari'ah, Ya'qub Pasha, and commentary on al-'Inayah. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that currency does not become determinate in a defective sale, so the seller does not need to give charity on his profit; the buyer must give charity on his profit from the sold item.

Key Terms
الخبث (al-khubth): Malignancy or illegitimacy in ownership due to defective contract or lack of ownership.
الشبهة (al-shubhah): Semblance or ambiguity that confers some degree of legality, e.g., in contracts that are defective but not void.
التصدق (al-tasadaqu): Giving charity (sadaqa) to purify profit that is not permissible to keep.
التعيين (al-ta'ayyun): Determination: becoming a specific identifiable object in a contract, as opposed to being a liability (dayn).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the seller's profit from the price (thaman) in a defective sale?
A) It is permissible and he does not need to give charity.
B) It is impermissible and he must give charity.
C) It is impermissible but no charity is required.
D) It is permissible only if the price is still in existence.

Key Takeaway
In a defective sale, the seller's profit on the price is permissible; the buyer's profit on the sold item requires charity, because currency is not determinate for profit purposes.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
8 min
Profit from a Void Sale and Specificity of Currency
Intra-Hanafi dispute on profit in void sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on profit from a void sale, identify the positions of the three imams, and apply the rule of ta'yeen to determine when profit is permissible.

Core Idea
In a void sale, profit from non-specific currency is permissible because its impurity is at the lowest level (shubhat al-shubha).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when a sale is void (fasid), is the seller allowed to keep the profit from the price he received? The answer hinges on whether the currency (thaman) becomes specific property (muta'ayyin) or remains a liability in the dhimma. You will learn the positions of the three leading Hanafi imams and how later scholars reconciled apparent contradictions in the transmitted rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the act
The act is receiving profit (ribh) from the price (thaman) taken in a void sale (bay' fasid). The core question: is this profit lawful (tayyib) for the seller?
A void sale is one that is valid in substance but defective in description, e.g., a sale with uncertainty in delivery.
2
State the positions of the Hanafi imams
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani: profit is permissible for the seller from the price. Abu Yusuf: profit is not permissible absolutely. Identify who holds which view.
The two (al-sahiban) often refer to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but here 'the two' means Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Check the text: 'عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف'.
3
Explain the basis of the dispute: ta'yeen and grading of shubha
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that in a void sale, cash is not specific (ghayr muta'ayyin). The contract attaches to the dhimma, so the impurity (khabath) from defective ownership is at the weakest level (shubhat al-shubha), which is not legally considered. Abu Yusuf relies on the maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' (profit is in return for liability), arguing the seller does not bear full liability, so profit is impure.
The Hanafi school grades shubha: shubha (from defective ownership) is stronger; shubhat al-shubha (from defective ownership of non-specific items) is weaker and not considered.
4
Trace the reconciliation attempts by later commentators
The passage discusses conflicting narrations: one says cash is specified (muta'ayyin) in a void sale, another says it is not. Sadr al-Shari'ah and Ya'qub Pasha offer harmonizations: specification applies for returning the exact item (ayn), but not for profit permissibility. Understand the distinction.
The author of al-Bahr leans toward the view that profit from non-specific cash is permissible, and the more correct view (al-sahih) is that dirhams are not specific in a void sale.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical effect
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that profit from the price is permissible to the seller unless he took the exact sold item (muthman), in which case charity is required. This is based on the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir and the preference of later scholars.
Be aware that some later authorities considered currency specific, requiring charity even from profit of the price. However, the majority allow the profit.

غير أن التفصيل الواقع في الكتاب هو صريح الرواية في الجامع الصغير وحينئذ فالأصح أن الدراهم لا تتعين في الفاسد؛ كذا في الفتح ملخصاً. قال صدر الشريعة : ويمكن التوفيق بأن لهذا العقد شبهين شبهاً بالغصب وشبهاً بالبيع، فإذا كانت قائمة اعتبر شبهة الغصب سعياً في رفع العقد الفاسد، وإذا لم تكن قائمة فاشترى بها شيئاً يعتبر شبهة البيع حتى الفساد إلى بدله. قال يعقوب باشا : هذا التوفيق إنما يفيد دليلاً للمسألة لا يرد عليه ما يرد عليها فالمناسب أن يقال: إن كلام صاحب الهداية في المسألة الأخيرة على الرواية الصحيحة لا على أنها تتعين في البيع الفاسد كما يشير إليه في العناية إلا أن يقال : مراد القائل بالتعيين الذي هو الأصح التعيين في صورة كونها قائمة لا تعيينها مطلقاً لكنه في الفاسد خلاف ما صرحوا به اهـ. وعبارته في العناية هذا إنما يستقيم على الرواية الصحيحة وهي أنها لا تتعين لا على الأصح وهي التي صاحب البحر قول يعقوب باشاه إلا أن يقال الخ» اهـ. وما أجاب به في لا الأم يسري لأصح وهي
And the depositor after paying the guarantee, it is not lawful for him absolutely according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf, because the impurity in the first is due to defective ownership, and in the second due to its absence because of the attachment of the contract to that which is specified in reality and to that which is not specified by way of shubha, in that the safety of the sold item or the determination of the price attaches to it. When ownership is defective, the reality turns into shubha, and shubha descends to shubhat al-shubha, and shubha is what is considered, not what is lower. Then know that their statement, following what is in al-Jami' al-Saghir, that profit is lawful for the seller in the spot price, indicates that cash is not specified in a void sale according to the more correct view, and their statement that it is specified according to the more correct view contradicts it. If the correctness of specification is relied upon, then the seller must give in charity, and the narration is contrary to it, and I have not seen anyone who clarified it among the commentators. It has become clear to me that there is no contradiction between them. They said earlier that it is specified according to the more correct view regarding the obligation to return the exact thing he took, and they said here that it is not specified, i.e., regarding the lawfulness of his profit. So it is specified from the aspect of defective ownership like a usurped thing, and not specified from the aspect that void commutative contracts are like valid ones. The first aspect is considered regarding the obligation to return the exact thing received, and the second regarding the permissibility of its profit. This was not reversed because of Abu Yusuf's evidence: al-kharaj bi al-daman. Its meaning, as in al-Faiq and al-Qamus, is that the yield of a slave belongs to the buyer if he returns him after discovering a defect. Because that which is not specified by specification, the contract does not attach to it but attaches to what is in the dhimmah; it is only a means in one way, so it necessitates a shubha of impurity, and shubha is considered. Hence lawfulness is absent due to lack of ownership in both properties. Impurity due to defective ownership begets shubha in that which is specified, because the impurity of defective ownership is lower than the impurity of lack of ownership, and it begets shubhat al-shubha in that which is not specified, and shubhat al-shubha is not considered. Therefore, the one who took the sold item gave charity from the profit, and the one who took the price did not. His statement: (Then) know that their statement... said in al-Nahr: This is only sound according to the narration of non-specification of cash, and it has passed that the narration of specification is the more correct. Thus, the more correct view is the obligation of charity upon the seller for what he profited, except that the detail found in the book is the explicit narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir. Hence, the more correct view is that dirhams are not specified in a void sale; such is in al-Fath concisely. Sadr al-Shari'ah said: Reconciliation is possible because this contract has two similarities: similarity to usurpation and similarity to sale. If the items are still extant, the similarity to usurpation is considered to raise the void contract; if they are not extant and he bought something with them, the similarity to sale is considered so that the voidness goes to its substitute. Ya'qub Pasha said: This reconciliation only provides a proof for the issue, not a refutation of what is objected to it. The appropriate thing is to say that the speech of the author of al-Hidayah on the last issue is based on the sound narration, not on the view that they are specified in a void sale, as indicated in al-Inayah, unless it is said that the proponent of specification intends specification in the case when it is extant, not absolute specification. But in the void sale, it is contrary to what they stated. His expression in al-Inayah: This is only sound according to the sound narration, which is that they are not specified, not according to the more correct view, which was presented that they are specified. It was said in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah: There is research here, because non-specification, whether in a usurped item or the price of a void sale, only applies to the second contract, and does not harm its specification in the first. So his statement 'only sound' has something in it. The author of al-Bahr took the statement of Ya'qub Pasha, except that it may be said... and what was answered with in [؟] is not clear.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the ruling of profit in a void sale and whether the currency (cash) taken by the seller becomes specific property (ta'ayyun) or not. The core issue is the nature of impurity (khabath) arising from defective ownership (fasad al-milk) versus absence of ownership. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, when currency is not specific (ghayr muta'ayyin) in a void sale, the contract attaches to the dhimma, and the impurity is lower-level (shubhat al-shubha), which is not legally considered. Therefore, the profit from the price (thaman) is permissible to the seller and he does not need to give it in charity. However, if the sold item itself (muthman) is taken, the impurity is stronger because of defective ownership, and profit must be given in charity. Abu Yusuf differs, relying on the principle 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' (profit is in return for liability). The commentators then discuss conflicting narrations: one holds that currency becomes specific in a void sale (al-sahih), while another says it does not (riwayat al-jami' al-saghir). They attempt reconciliation by noting that specificity is relevant for returning the exact item (ayn) but not for the permissibility of profit. Later scholars like Sadr al-Shari'ah and Ya'qub Pasha offer harmonizations. The relied-upon position is that non-specific currency in a void contract makes profit permissible.

Key Terms
تعيين (ta'yeen): The quality of being specific or identified; in this context, whether the currency (thaman) in a void sale becomes a specific item owned by the seller or remains a liability in the dhimma.
شبهة (shubha): A legal ambiguity that prevents a ruling of absolute prohibition or permissibility. In this passage, shubha is graded: shubha (from defective ownership) and shubhat al-shubha (a weaker ambiguity).
الخبث (al-khabath): Moral impurity or unlawfulness. In the context of void sales, it applies to income derived from a transaction lacking valid ownership.
الخراج بالضمان (al-kharaj bi al-daman): A legal maxim meaning 'profit is in return for liability' – the one who bears the risk of loss is entitled to the benefits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on profit from the price (thaman) in a void sale?
A) The profit is prohibited (haram) and must be given in charity.
B) The profit is permissible (mubah) and the seller may keep it.
C) The profit is permissible only if the seller did not know the sale was void.
D) The profit must be given to the buyer.

Key Takeaway
In a void sale, profit from the cash price (thaman) is permissible for the seller because the currency is not specific, and the impurity from defective ownership is too weak to prohibit it.

Profit from Money Received Under False Debt Claim
Ruling on profit from money paid under a false debt claim, with qiyas analogy and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on profit from money received under a false debt claim and explain the role of al-tasmia (naming) in establishing ownership for permissibility.

Core Idea
Profit from money paid under a false debt claim is permissible because the debt was established by naming (tasmia), not by the truth of the claim.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a subtle but important fiqh issue: when someone claims a debt and receives payment, but later both agree the debt was false, is the profit from that money permissible? The answer hinges on a key Hanafi principle—al-tasmia (the naming of the debt in the claim) creates a legal obligation, making the received payment a valid substitute. Even if the claim was false, the naming forms the basis of ownership at the time of receipt, so any profit earned is lawful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the ruling
The case: a claimant demands dirhams, receives payment, then both agree the debt was false. The ruling: profit from those dirhams is permissible (yateeb).
2
Understand the basis for permissibility: al-tasmia
The debt was established by naming (tasmia) in the claim, making it wajib in the claim. Payment is a substitute (badal) for that named debt, and the substitute is considered owned.
The impurity (khabath) from defective ownership is weak because the dominant cause was the naming.
3
Analyze the qiyas analogy
The text analogizes to a sale where a buyer emancipates a slave, then the slave-girl (consideration) is claimed: emancipation remains valid because ownership at the time was based on the nominal cause. Similarly, ownership of the dirhams at receipt was based on the named debt.
This is clear qiyas (jaliy) as the illa is ownership based on a nominal cause.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute with Fath al-Qadir
Fath al-Qadir argues that if the claimant deliberately lied from the start, he does not own the money at all. The author of al-Bahr disagrees, saying the naming is the decisive factor, not the claimant's intention. Evidence from oath cases supports this.
The oath example: if one pays a debt with usurped money, the oath is not broken, even though the money was not his—the naming in the oath suffices.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view is that profit is permissible regardless of the claimant's knowledge, as long as the naming existed. The naming creates a legal obligation, and the substitute is owned.
This is the position preferred by the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو ادعى على آخر دراهم فقضاها إياه ثم تصادقا أنه لا شيء له عليه طاب له ربحه) أي ما ربحه في الدراهم لأن الخبث لفساد الملك ها هنا لأن الدين وجب بالتسمية ثم استحق بالتصادق وبدل المستحق مملوك فلا يعمل فيما لا يتعين ألا ترى أنه لو باع عبداً بجارية فأعتقه المشتري ثم استحقت الجارية لا يبطل العتق في العبد، ولولا أنه مملوك لبطل لأنه لا عتق فيما لا يملكه ابن آدم وكذا لو حلف أن لا يفارق غريمه حتى يستوفي منه دينه فباعه عبد الغير بالدين فقبضه الحالف وفارقه ثم استحق العبد مولاه ولم يجز البيع لم يحنث الحالف لأن المدين ملك ما في ذمته بالبيع وهو بدل المستحق ولا يحنث الحالف بالاستحقاق. وفي فتح القدير : واعلم أن ملكه باعتبار زعمه أنه قبض الدراهم بدلاً عما يزعم أنه ملكه، أما لو كان في أصل دعواه الدين متعمداً الكذب فدفع إليه لا يملكه أصلاً لأنه متيقن لأنه لا ملك له اهـ . وظاهر إطلاقهم خلافه لأن المنظور إليه وجوبه بالتسمية لا زعم المدعي، ويدل عليه مسألة الحلف فإنه لو غصب دراهم وقضى بها دينه ثم تبين أنها مغصوبة فإنه لا حنث عليه، وكذا لو غصب عبداً وباعه بدينه
His statement: (If someone claimed against another dirhams, and he paid them to him, then they both agree that nothing is owed to him, his profit is permissible for him) meaning his profit in the dirhams, because the impurity due to defective ownership here is because the debt was determined by naming, then it became due by agreement, and the substitute for the due item is owned, so it does not operate in what is not specific. Do you not see that if he sold a slave for a slave-girl, and the buyer emancipated the slave, then the slave-girl was claimed, the emancipation of the slave is not invalidated. Were it not that the slave is owned, it would be invalid, because there is no emancipation of what a human does not own. Similarly, if he swore that he would not part from his creditor until he receives his debt from him, and he sold a slave belonging to someone else for the debt, and the swearer took possession and parted, then the slave's owner claimed ownership and the sale was not valid, the swearer does not break his oath because the debtor owned what was in his dhimma by the sale, and it is a substitute for the due item, and the swearer does not break his oath by the claim. And in Fath al-Qadir: Know that his ownership is based on his claim that he received the dirhams as a substitute for what he claims he owns. However, if he originally claimed the debt deliberately lying, and was given, he does not own it at all, because he is certain that he has no ownership. The apparent meaning of their general statement is contrary to that, because the consideration is its being obligatory by naming, not the claimant's claim. Evidence for this is the issue of the oath: if he usurped dirhams and paid his debt with them, then it became clear they were usurped, he does not break his oath; likewise, if he usurped a slave and sold him for his debt.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning a case where a person claims a debt from another, receives payment, and later both agree the debt was false. The question is whether the recipient (who initially claimed ownership) may keep the profit he earned from that money. The answer is yes, his profit is permissible (yateeb). The reasoning: the debt was established by the naming (tasmia) in the claim, and the payment was made as a substitute (badal) for that debt. When they later agree it was false, the debt is annulled, but the substitute is considered owned because it was received as a replacement. The impurity (khabath) from defective ownership is weak because the dominant cause (the naming) was the basis, not the false claim. The text provides analogies: (1) If someone sells a slave for a slave-girl, the buyer emancipates the slave, then the slave-girl is found to belong to someone else, the emancipation remains valid because the slave was owned at the time. (2) If a person swears an oath about debt payment, and receives an invalid item, the oath is not broken. Then it quotes Fath al-Qadir: if the claimant originally lied deliberately in his claim, then he does not own the money at all. But the author of al-Bahr disagrees with that, saying the dominant factor is the naming, not the liar's intention. He provides further evidence from oaths: if someone uses usurped money to repay a debt and later it is found usurped, he does not break his oath. The ruling is that the profit is permissible for the person who received the payment, because the principal means (the naming) is valid, even if the underlying claim turned out false.

Key Terms
التسمية (al-tasmia): The naming or specification of a debt in the claim. It creates a legal obligation (wujub) in the claim, even if the underlying fact is false.
بدل المستحق (badal al-mustahaq): The substitute or replacement for something that is due. In this case, the dirhams paid are considered the substitute for the debt that was claimed.
الحنث (al-hinth): Breaking an oath. The text uses oath examples to show that if the nominal debt was the basis, actions based on it do not break oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a claimant falsely claims a debt, receives payment, and later both agree it was false, why is the profit from that money permissible?
A) Because the payment was made voluntarily.
B) Because the debt was established by naming (tasmia) and the money is considered a valid substitute.
C) Because the claimant repented.
D) Because the money was not owned by the payer.

Key Takeaway
Profit from money received under a false debt claim is permissible because the debt was established by naming (tasmia), making the payment a valid substitute owned by the recipient at the time of receipt.

Najash: The Ruling on Auction Rigging in Hanafi Fiqh
Definition and ruling of najash with evidence and condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define najash, state its ruling (makruh tahrimi), explain why the sale remains valid, and apply the condition regarding market value.

Core Idea
Najash is prohibited, but the sale is valid because the prohibition is external, not intrinsic.

In this lesson, we examine the concept of najash (auction rigging) as a prohibited act in sales. The Hanafi school distinguishes between prohibitions that affect the validity of a contract and those that only affect the sinfulness of the act. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the ruling in real transactions.

Guided Path
1
Define najash
Identify the linguistic and technical definitions of najash from the passage. Note that it involves bidding up a price without intending to buy, to deceive others.
The term also includes collusion with the seller by praising the item.
2
Identify the evidence
Locate the hadith cited ('لا تناجشوا') and note its source (Sahihayn). Recognize that it is a clear prohibition.
3
Understand the Hanafi classification
Distinguish between intrinsic prohibitions (which invalidate the contract) and external prohibitions (which do not). Najash is an external prohibition, so the contract is valid but the act is sinful (makruh tahrimi).
Review the usul principle: every prohibited thing is bad; if bad in itself -> invalidity; if bad due to external factor -> karaha tahrim with validity.
4
Apply the condition about market value
Note the condition from al-Jawhara: if the commodity has not reached its market value, najash is not prohibited because it benefits the seller without harming anyone. Consider how this affects the ruling.
This is an intra-Hanafi nuance; the mufta bihi is that najash is prohibited when the price is at or above market value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وكره النجش شروع في مكروهات البيع. ولم يكن المكروه دون الفاسد أخره، وليس المراد بكونه دونه في حكم المنع الشرعي بل في عدم فساد العقد وإلا فهذه كلها تحريمية لا نعلم خلافاً في الاثم ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وقد بحث هنا بحثاً لا طائل تحته تركته عمداً وقد تقرر في الأصول أن كل منهي عنه قبيح فإن كان لعينه أفاد بطلانه، وإن كان لغيره فإن كان لوصف كبيع الربا والبيع بشرط مفسد أفاد فساده، وإن كان لمجاور كهذه البيوع المكروهة أفاد كراهة التحريم مع الصحة والنجش بفتحتين ويروى بالسكون أن تسام السلعة بأزيد من ثمنها وأنت لا تريد شراءها ليراك الآخر فيقع فيه، وكذا في النكاح وغيره . ولا تناجشوا لا تفعل ذلك وأصله من نجش الصيد وهو إثارته؛ كذا في المغرب. وفي القاموس : النجش أن تواطئ رجلاً إذا أراد بيعاً أن تمدحه أو أن يريد الإنسان أن : يبيع فتساومه بها بثمن كثير لينظر إليك ناظر فيقع فيها، أو أن تنفر الناس عن الشيء إلى غيره. وإثارة الصيد والبحث عن الشيء وإثارته والجمع والاستخراج والإنقاذ والاسراع كالنجاشة بالكسر اهـ. وحديث النهي لا تناجشوا في الصحيحين وقيده أصحابنا كما في الجوهرة بما إذا كانت السلعة إذا بلغت قيمتها أما إذا لم تبلغ فلا منع منه لأنه نفع للمسلم من غير اضرار بأحد . بياعة السعدية ذكره الرملي قبل اطلاعه عليه وقال: وأنا في عجب عجيب من فهم هؤلاء الأجلاء التناقض من مثل هذا مع ظهوره فإنه بمنزلة النقود لا تتعين في العقود الفاسدة، ولا شك أن المشتري شراء صحيحاً بما قبضه في الفاسد إذا صحيح شرعي خال عن الشبهة العدم تعين ذلك ذلك العقد. قوله : (وظاهر إطلاقهم خلافه قال في النهر وأقول: قد صرحوا في الإقرار النقد في ربح فقد بعقد ربح بأن المقر له إذا كان يعلم أن المقر كاذب في إقراره لا يحمل له أخذه عن كره منه، أما لو اشتبه الأمر
His statement: And najash is disliked, beginning the disliked sales. He delayed it not because the disliked is below the void in ruling of legal prohibition, but in not invalidating the contract. Otherwise, all these are prohibited; we know no disagreement about sin. This is in Fath al-Qadir. And there is here a research with no benefit; I intentionally left it. It is established in usul that every prohibited thing is bad: if it is bad in itself, it causes invalidity; if it is bad due to something else, if it is due to a description like riba sale or sale with an invalid condition, it causes voidness; if it is due to an external factor like these disliked sales, it causes prohibition (karaha tahrim) while preserving validity. Najash (with two fatha or sukun) is that you bid up the commodity at more than its price while you do not intend to buy, so that another sees you and falls into it. Similarly in marriage and other things. 'Do not practice najash' – do not do that. Its origin is from driving out game, i.e., stirring it up; thus in al-Mughrib. In al-Qamus: Najash is that you collude with a man – when he wants to sell – that you praise it, or that a person wants to sell so you bargain with him at a high price so that an observer sees you and falls into it, or that you drive people away from something to something else. And stirring up game, searching for something and stirring it, gathering, extracting, rescuing, and speeding like najasha (with kasra) end. And the hadith of prohibition 'Do not practice najash' is in the Sahihayn. Our companions, as in al-Jawhara, restricted it to when the commodity reaches its value; if it does not reach it, there is no prohibition, because it is a benefit for a Muslim without harming anyone. The seller from Sa'diyya, mentioned by al-Ramli before his knowledge, said: I am in wonder at the understanding of these scholars of contradiction in such an apparent matter. It is like cash that is not specific in void contracts. There is no doubt that the buyer makes a valid purchase with what he received in a void sale if it is a valid purchase free from ambiguity. The non-specificity of that contract. His statement: (And the apparent meaning of their absolute statement is contrary to it) he said in al-Nahr: I say: They have explicitly stated in acknowledgment...
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage defines and gives the ruling on najash (auction rigging), which is one of the 'disliked sales' (makruhat al-bay'). The classification is important: najash is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) but it does not invalidate the contract. The text explains that the prohibition is because of an external factor (mujawir), not intrinsic to the contract itself. The sale remains valid. Najash is defined as bidding up a price without intending to buy, to trick others into paying more. The textual evidence is the hadith 'لا تناجشوا' in the Sahihayn. The Hanafi scholars, as in al-Jawhara, restrict the prohibition to when the commodity reaches its market value; if the price is below market, it is not prohibited because it benefits the seller without harming anyone. The passage then includes a comment about a seller from Sa'diyya and a discussion about non-specificity of currency, which seems to be a digression. The concluding part returns to a different issue about acknowledgment, but the core lesson is the definition and ruling of najash.

Key Terms
النجش (al-najash): Bidding up a commodity at a higher price without intending to buy, in order to deceive others into paying more. Also can refer to collusion with the seller to praise the item.
مكروه تحريمي (makruh tahrimi): An act that is forbidden (haram) but does not invalidate the contract when related to a sale due to an external factor.
كراهة التحريم (karaha al-tahrim): Prohibitive dislike; equivalent to haram. Used when the imperfection is external to the act's essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the sale contract in which najash (auction rigging) occurred?
A) The contract is void (batil).
B) The contract is valid but the person who practiced najash commits a sin.
C) The contract is suspended until the real buyer confirms.
D) The contract is valid and there is no sin.

Key Takeaway
Najash is a forbidden act of deception, but the contract stands; the sin lies with the bidder who rigs the auction. The condition of market value may limit the prohibition.

Profit Permissible When Debt Admitted False
Explains the ruling that profit from dirhams is permissible when both parties later agree there was no debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on profit from a debt later admitted false and explain the role of tasmia (naming) in establishing ownership of the substitute.

Core Idea
Profit from a debt later admitted false is permissible because the debt was established by the naming (tasmia), not by actual ownership.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle: the effect of naming (tasmia) in contracts. Even if a debt is later admitted false, the profit from the paid dirhams is permissible. The proof is grounded in qiyas (analogy) to the case of a slave sold for a female slave that is later reclaimed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Start by reading the matn: 'If one claims dirhams against another and he pays them, then they both agree that nothing is owed to him, his profit is permissible.' The ruling is permissibility (mubah).
2
Understand the reason for permissibility
The commentator explains that the impurity (khabath) is due to the corruption of ownership, but here the debt was established by the naming (tasmia) in the claim. Even though the debt is later admitted false, the substitute (badal) is owned.
Tasmia (naming) creates a legal obligation regardless of the underlying reality.
3
Trace the analogy (qiyas)
The author gives an analogy: if someone sells a slave for a female slave, the buyer frees him, then the female slave is reclaimed, the manumission is still valid. This illustrates that the substitute for an invalidly held item is owned.
The asl (original case) is the slave sale; the far' (new case) is the debt payment. The illa (effective cause) is that the substitute for an invalidly held item is owned.
4
Apply the analogy to the debt case
The dirhams paid are the substitute (badal) for the debt. Since the debt was established by tasmia, the substitute is owned. Thus, the recipient owns the dirhams and any profit from them is permissible.
The oath case further supports: if someone pays a debt with usurped goods, the oath is not broken because the debtor owned the substitute.
5
Note the nuance from Fath al-Qadir
Fath al-Qadir suggests that if the claimant intentionally lied in his original claim, he does not own the payment. However, the preferred view (mufta bihi) is that the obligation is based on the naming, not the claimant's assumption, so the ruling of permissibility remains.
The text states: 'The apparent meaning of their unrestricted statements is contrary to this, because the consideration is its obligation by the naming, not the claimant's assumption.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو ادعى على آخر دراهم فقضاها إياه ثم تصادقا أنه لا شيء له عليه طاب له ربحه) أي ما ربحه في الدراهم لأن الخبث لفساد الملك ها هنا لأن الدين وجب بالتسمية ثم استحق بالتصادق وبدل المستحق مملوك فلا يعمل فيما لا يتعين ألا ترى أنه لو باع عبداً بجارية فأعتقه المشتري ثم استحقت الجارية لا يبطل العتق في العبد، ولولا أنه مملوك لبطل لأنه لا عتق فيما لا يملكه ابن آدم وكذا لو حلف أن لا يفارق غريمه حتى يستوفي منه دينه فباعه عبد الغير بالدين فقبضه الحالف وفارقه ثم استحق العبد مولاه ولم يجز البيع لم يحنث الحالف لأن المدين ملك ما في ذمته بالبيع وهو بدل المستحق ولا يحنث الحالف بالاستحقاق. وفي فتح القدير : واعلم أن ملكه باعتبار زعمه أنه قبض الدراهم بدلاً عما يزعم أنه ملكه، أما لو كان في أصل دعواه الدين متعمداً الكذب فدفع إليه لا يملكه أصلاً لأنه متيقن لأنه لا ملك له اهـ . وظاهر إطلاقهم خلافه لأن المنظور إليه وجوبه بالتسمية لا زعم المدعي، ويدل عليه مسألة الحلف فإنه لو غصب دراهم وقضى بها دينه ثم تبين أنها مغصوبة فإنه لا حنث عليه، وكذا لو غصب عبداً وباعه بدينه
His saying: (If one claims dirhams against another and he pays them, then they both agree that nothing is owed to him, his profit is permissible) i.e., whatever profit he made from the dirhams, because the impurity is due to the corruption of ownership here, because the debt was established by the naming (tasmia) and then it was found to be invalid by mutual agreement, and the substitute for the invalidly held item is owned, so it does not operate in what is not specified. Do you not see that if someone sold a slave for a female slave, and the buyer freed him, then the female slave was reclaimed (found to be owned by another), the manumission of the slave is not invalidated? Had it not been owned, it would be invalid, because there is no manumission in what is not owned by a human. Similarly, if someone swore not to separate from his creditor until he received his debt, then he sold someone else's slave for the debt, and the swearer took possession and separated, then the slave's owner reclaimed him and the sale was not valid, the swearer is not guilty of perjury, because the debtor owned what was in his liability by the sale, and it is the substitute for the reclaimed item, and the swearer is not guilty by the reclamation. In Fath al-Qadir: 'Know that his ownership is based on his assumption that he received the dirhams as a substitute for what he assumes he owns. But if in his original claim of the debt he intentionally lied, then it is given to him, he does not own it at all because he is certain that he has no ownership.' The apparent meaning of their unrestricted statements is contrary to this, because the consideration is its obligation by the naming, not the claimant's assumption. This is supported by the oath case: if he usurped dirhams and paid them as his debt, then it became clear they were usurped, he is not guilty of perjury. Similarly, if he usurped a slave and sold him for his debt.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on the permissibility of profit when a person pays a debt he claimed, and later both parties agree there was no debt. The commentator clarifies that the profit is permissible because the debt was established by the contract (tasmia) — the payment was made based on the naming of the price, not on the actual existence of the debt. When they agree there was no debt, the payment becomes invalid, but the substitute (the dirhams paid) is owned by the recipient because it is not specifically identifiable; thus the profit from it is permissible. To illustrate, he gives an analogy: if someone sells a slave for a female slave, the buyer frees the slave, then the female slave is reclaimed (found to be owned by another), the manumission is still valid because the buyer owned the slave as a substitute. Similarly, an oath is not broken if the property used to pay the debt is later reclaimed. The text then discusses a nuance from Fath al-Qadir: if the claimant knew he was lying from the start, he does not own the payment. However, the general view of the Hanafi school is that despite the intentional lie, the obligation is based on the naming, not the assumption, so profit is still permissible. This is supported by the case where one pays a debt with usurped goods: the oath is not broken even though the goods were not owned.

Key Terms
بدل المستحق (badal al-mustahaqq): The substitute given for something that is reclaimed (due to invalidity or third-party claim). In this context, it refers to the dirhams paid as a debt that is later agreed to be non-existent.
تسمية (tasmia): The naming or specification of the price in a contract. Here it means the debt was defined by the naming of the amount in the claim and payment, not by the actual underlying obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a person falsely claims a debt and is paid, then they both agree there was no debt, what is the ruling on the profit made from that money?
A) It is impermissible because the money was obtained through false pretense.
B) It is permissible because the debt was established by the naming in the contract.
C) It is makruh because of the false claim.
D) It is permissible only if the claimant was ignorant of the truth.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the obligation of a debt is established by the naming (tasmia) in the contract, so even if the debt is later admitted false, the substitute (badal) is owned and profit from it is permissible.

Najsh: Disliked Price Inflation Without Intent to Buy
Defines najsh and explains its ruling as makruh tahrimi with conditions and hadith proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on najsh (makruh tahrimi), explain why the sale remains valid, and apply the condition (price reaching value) to determine when the prohibition applies.

Core Idea
Najsh is prohibited as makruh tahrimi, but the sale contract is valid because the prohibition attaches to the act of deception, not to the contract itself.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq, the commentator explains the ruling on najsh – artificially inflating a price without intending to buy. While this deceptive practice is prohibited, the underlying sale remains legally valid. Let’s examine the definition, the proof from the hadith, and the key distinction between prohibitions that affect contractual validity and those that do not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is najsh? According to the text, it is negotiating a price higher than the value without intending to buy, to deceive another into purchasing. The ruling is makruh tahrimi (prohibitory disliked).
The term najsh originally means to flush out game.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the prophetic hadith: 'لا تناجشوا' (Do not practice najsh), found in Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim.
The hadith is ahad, making its transmission zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'لا تفعلوا' indicates prohibition. In Hanafi usul, a prohibition that does not clearly imply invalidity (like those due to an external cause) results in karahah tahrimiyyah with the contract remaining valid.
4
Note the condition
The Hanafi school restricts the prohibition to when the commodity has reached its fair value. If the price is below value, najsh is not prohibited because it benefits the seller without harming anyone.
This condition is from al-Jawhara, cited in Fath al-Qadir.
5
Understand the validity of the contract
The passage explains that prohibitions due to an adjacent matter (like najsh) do not invalidate the contract. The sale is sahih but sinful for the one practicing najsh.
This distinguishes from prohibitions inherent to the contract (like riba) which cause fasād or bāṭil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وكره النجش شروع في مكروهات البيع. ولما كان المكروه دون الفاسد أخره، وليس المراد بكونه دونه في حكم المنع الشرعي بل في عدم فساد العقد وإلا فهذه كلها تحريمية لا نعلم خلافاً في الاثم ; كذا في فتح القدير. وقد بحث هنا بحثاً لا طائل تحته تركته عمداً وقد تقرر في الأصول أن كل منهي عنه قبيح فإن كان لعينه أفاد بطلانه، وإن كان لغيره فإن كان لوصف كبيع الربا والبيع بشرط مفسد أفاد فساده، وإن كان لمجاور كهذه البيوع المكروهة أفاد كراهة التحريم مع الصحة والنجش بفتحتين ويروى بالسكون أن تسام السلعة بأزيد من ثمنها وأنت لا تريد شراءها ليراك الآخر فيقع فيه، وكذا في النكاح وغيره . ولا تناجشوا لا تفعل ذلك وأصله من نجش الصيد وهو إثارته؛ كذا في المغرب. وفي القاموس: النجش أن تواطئ رجلاً إذا أراد بيعاً أن تمدحه أو أن يريد الإنسان أن يبيع فتساومه بها بثمن كثير لينظر إليك ناظر فيقع فيها، أو أن تنفر الناس عن الشيء إلى غيره. وإثارة الصيد والبحث عن الشيء وإثارته والجمع والاستخراج والإنقاذ والاسراع كالنجاشة بالكسر اهـ. وحديث النهي لا تناجشوا في الصحيحين وقيده أصحابنا كما في الجوهرة بما إذا كانت السلعة إذا بلغت قيمتها أما إذا لم تبلغ فلا منع منه لأنه نفع للمسلم من غير اضرار بأحد
His saying: (And najsh is disliked) is the start of the disliked things in sales. Since the disliked is less than the invalid, he delayed it. It is not meant that it is less in terms of legal prohibition, but in terms of the contract not being invalid. Otherwise, all of these are prohibitory; we know of no difference of opinion in the sin. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And he researched here a pointless research, which I intentionally omitted. And it is established in usul that every prohibited thing is ugly. If it is prohibited for its own essence, it necessitates its invalidity. If it is prohibited for something else, then if it is for a description like usury sales and a sale with a corrupt condition, it necessitates its corruption. And if it is for an adjacent matter like these disliked sales, it necessitates disapproval of prohibition while the contract is sound. And najsh (with two fatha or with sukun) is that you negotiate the price of a commodity at a price higher than its value while you do not intend to buy it, so that someone else may see you and make the purchase. Similarly in marriage and other matters. 'Do not practice najsh' means do not do that, and its origin is from flushing out game. Thus in al-Mughrib. In al-Qamus: najsh is that you collude with a man when he wants to sell, that you praise it, or that a person wants to sell and you negotiate with him at a high price so that a looker may see you and fall into it, or that you divert people from something to something else. Flushing out game, searching for a thing and stirring it up, gathering, extracting, rescuing, and hastening is also called najasha with kasrah. And the hadith of prohibition 'Do not practice najsh' is in the two Sahihs. Our companions restricted it, as in al-Jawhara, to when the commodity has reached its value. But if it has not reached its value, there is no prohibition, because it is a benefit for the Muslim without harming anyone.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage introduces the subject of disliked (makruh) transactions, specifically najsh. The commentator explains that these are haram (prohibitive makruh), but the contract remains valid. Najsh is defined as bidding up the price of an item without intending to buy, solely to trick another person into buying at a higher price. This is prohibited based on the hadith 'Do not practice najsh'. However, Hanafi scholars restrict this prohibition to cases where the item's price has already reached its fair value. If the price is below market value, najsh is allowed because it benefits the seller without harming anyone. The passage also clarifies that the prohibition is for the act (i.e., the deception), not for the contract itself, so the sale is valid but sinful.

Key Terms
النجش (al-najsh): To negotiate a price for a commodity at an inflated price without intending to buy, so that someone else sees and buys at that price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on najsh (bidding up price without intent to buy) according to the Hanafi school?
A) The sale is invalid.
B) The sale is valid but the act is prohibited.
C) It is permissible if the commodity is below market value.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Najsh is prohibited (makruh tahrimi) but the sale is valid; the prohibition is conditional on the commodity reaching its fair value.

Prohibited Transactional Harm: Bidding Over Bids, Meeting Caravans, and Townsman-Bedouin Sales
Explains three prohibited sales with hadith proofs and harm condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three prohibited sales practices, identify their hadith proofs, and apply the Hanafi condition of harm to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
A sale is valid but sinful when it causes market harm through bid interference, caravan interception, or town-bedouin exploitation.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains three transactions that the Prophet ﷺ explicitly prohibited: bidding over a settled bid, intercepting incoming traders before they reach the market, and a townsman selling on behalf of a bedouin. The common thread is harm (ḍarar) to the market participants. Understanding the condition of harm is essential to apply these rulings correctly, as the prohibition lifts when no harm results.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three practices and their proofs
Locate each practice in the text: bidding over another's bid, meeting caravans (talaqqī al-jalb), and townsman selling for bedouin (bay' al-ḥāḍir lil-bādī). Note the hadith proof cited for each from Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
The hadith text is: 'لا يستام الرجل على سوم أخيه... ونهى عن تلقي الركبان... ولا يبيع حاضر لباد'
2
Understand the condition of harm
Read the commentator's explanation that the prohibition is tied to harm (ḍarar). When no harm results—e.g., in auction bidding, or when markets are not affected—the practice is permissible. This is the key Hanafi nuance.
Harm can be to the seller, buyer, or the local market.
3
Distinguish auction from bid interference
The prohibition on bidding over another's bid applies only when the original bidder and seller have reached an agreement (tarāḍiyan). Auction bidding (bay' al-muzāyada) is exempt because there is no settled bid.
The text says: 'إذا لم يركن أحدهما على الآخر فهو بيع من يزيد' (if they haven't settled, it is auction bidding).
4
Analyze the two interpretations of townsman selling
The passage gives two interpretations: (1) the townsman buys the goods from the bedouin and resells (becomes owner), or (2) the townsman acts as a broker (simsār). The second interpretation aligns better with the hadith and is considered more correct (asahh).
Ibn Abbas explained 'لایکون له سمسار' (he should not be a broker).
5
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on profit
In the townsman-bedouin scenario, if the townsman buys the goods, there is a difference between Muhammad (allows profit if later agreed debt was settled) and Abu Yusuf (likely prohibits profit). The muftā bihi is that profit is permissible only when no harm or deception occurred.
The text mentions 'عند محمد خلافاً لأبي يوسف'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والسوم على سوم غيره للحديث لا يستام الرجل على سوم أخيه، ولا يخطب على خطبة أخيه» (۱) ولأن في ذلك إيحاشاً واضراراً. وهذا إذا تراضى المتعاقدان على مبلغ ثمن في المساومة، فإذا لم يركن أحدهما على الآخر فهو بيع من يزيد ولا بأس به على ما نذكره وما ذكرناه محمل النهي في النكاح أيضاً. وفي القاموس : السوم في المبايعة كالسوام بالضم سمت بالسلعة وساومت بالسلعة واستمت بها وعليها غاليت واستمته إياها وعليها سالته سومها اه قوله : (وتلقي الجلب) لحديث الصحيحين عن ابن عباس نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن تتلقي الركبان وأن يبيع حاضر لباد فقلت لابن عباس : ما قوله حاضر لباد قال : لا يكون له سمسار. أو لبس أما إذا انتفيا فلا بأس به. وفي المغرب : جلب الشيء جاء به من بلد إلى بلد للتجارة جلباً، والجلب المجلوب ومنه نهى عن تلقي الجلب اهـ. قوله : ( وبيع الحاضر للبادي) لما تقدم من النهي وهو مقيد كما في بعضهم بعضاً»(۲). ولذا في المجتبى : هذا التفسير أصح ذكره في زاد الفقهاء لموافقة الحديث، وعلى هذا فتفسير ابن عباس بأن لا يكون له سمسار ليس هو تفسير بيع الحاضر للبادي وهو صورة النهي بل من قال بهم، عليه حل له الأخذ عند محمد خلافاً لأبي يوسف كما سيأتي، وحينئذ فلا يطيب له ربحه، ويحمل كلامه هنا على ما إذا ظن أن عليه ديناً بإرث من أبيه مثلاً ثم تبين أن وكيله أوفاه لأبيه فتصادقا أن لا دين حينئذ يطيب له وهذا فقه حسن فتدبره اهـ. ونقله عنه الرملي وأقره. قول المصنف: (والسوم على غیره) قال الرملي : لا يخفى عليك أنه تدخل فيه الإجارة إذ هي بيع المنافع وهي واقعة الفتوى . قوله: (وفسره في الاختيار الخ قال الرملي ويشهد لصحة التفسير الأول ما في الفصول العمادية
His saying: (And bidding over the bid of another) due to the hadith: 'A man should not bid over the bid of his brother, nor propose over his proposal.' And because there is in that estrangement and harm. This is when the two contracting parties have agreed on a sum of price in the negotiation. If they have not settled on one another, then it is auction bidding, and there is no problem with it as we will mention. What we have mentioned is the meaning of the prohibition in marriage as well. In al-Qamus: 'as-saum' in transactions is like 'as-suwaam' with damma: you priced the commodity, and you bargained over the commodity, and you sought it, and you overpaid for it, and you asked its price. His saying: (And meeting the caravans) due to the hadith in the two Sahihs from Ibn Abbas: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade meeting the riders and that a townsman should sell for a bedouin.' I said to Ibn Abbas: 'What is his saying: a townsman should sell for a bedouin?' He said: 'He should not be a broker for him.' There are two forms of the interceptor: one is that the buyers meet them to buy food from them in a year of need, so that they sell it to the townspeople at an increase; and the second is that they buy from them cheaper than the city price while they are unaware of the price. The understanding of the prohibition with us is when it harms the townspeople or the market. If it is absent, then there is no problem. In al-Mughrib: 'jalb' of a thing means to bring it from one country to another for trade. Al-jalb is the thing brought, and from it is the prohibition of meeting the caravans. His saying: (And the townsman selling for the bedouin) due to the preceding prohibition, and it is restricted, as in al-Hidayah, to when the townspeople are in drought and need, and he sells to them out of greed for the high price because there is harm. But if it is not so, then there is no problem due to the absence of harm. It was interpreted in al-Ikhtiyar as: the bedouin brings the commodity, and the townsman takes it to sell it for him later at a price higher than the price at the time of bringing. So according to the first interpretation, the townsman is owner and seller, and the bedouin is buyer; according to the second, the townsman is a broker and the bedouin is the owner of the commodity. The last part of the hadith supports the second: 'Leave the people, Allah provides for some through others.' Thus in al-Mujtaba: 'This interpretation is more correct' as mentioned in Zad al-Fuqaha' due to its agreement with the hadith. On this, Ibn Abbas's interpretation that 'he should not be a broker for him' is not an interpretation of 'townsman selling for bedouin' which is the prohibited form, but whoever says to them... then his taking is permissible according to Muhammad contrary to Abu Yusuf as will come, and then his profit is not permissible. His statement here is interpreted as when he thought he had a debt from inheritance from his father, for example, then it became clear that his agent had paid his father, and they agreed there was no debt, then his profit is permissible. This is good fiqh, so ponder it. Al-Ramli transmitted it and approved it. The author's statement: (And bidding over another) al-Ramli said: It is not hidden from you that it includes ijarah because it is the sale of usufructs, and it is the matter of fatwa. His saying: (And he interpreted it in al-Ikhtiyar...) al-Ramli said: Supporting the validity of the first interpretation is what is in al-Fusul al-Imadiyyah.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage covers three prohibited practices: (1) Bidding over another's bid (السوم على سوم غيره) - it is prohibited when the parties have already agreed on a price; auction bidding is allowed. (2) Meeting caravans (تلقي الجلب) - intercepting traders before they reach the market to buy cheap and sell dear is prohibited if it harms the local market. (3) Townsman selling for bedouin (بيع الحاضر للبادي) - a townsman acting as a broker or buying from a bedouin to resell at a higher price is prohibited when it causes harm to the community, such as in times of drought. The commentator explains two interpretations: either the townsman buys the goods and becomes owner, or he acts as a broker. The second is more correct as it aligns with the hadith. The passage also discusses the permissibility of profit when the parties later agree there was no debt (returning to the earlier ruling). Finally, al-Ramli notes that the prohibition on bidding over another's bid also applies to ijarah (leasing) since it is the sale of usufructs.

Key Terms
السوم على سوم غيره (al-saumu 'ala saumi ghairihi): Bidding over a bid that another potential buyer has already made, when the seller and that buyer have agreed on a price or are in serious negotiation.
تلقي الجلب (talaqqi al-jalb): Going out to meet incoming traders (caravans) before they reach the market to buy their goods cheaply, taking advantage of their ignorance of local prices.
بيع الحاضر للبادي (bay' al-hadiri lil-badi): A townsman selling goods for a bedouin (rustic/traveler) either by buying first or acting as broker, often at inflated prices.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it permissible for a townsman to buy from a bedouin and resell at a higher price?
A) Always impermissible.
B) Permissible in all cases.
C) Permissible when there is no harm to the townspeople.
D) Only if the bedouin agrees to the higher price.

Key Takeaway
The Ḥanafī school prohibits these three sales only when they cause harm; otherwise they are permissible, and the contract remains valid in all cases.

Bidding Over Another's Bid: Prohibition and Exception
States ruling on bidding over another's bid with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bidding over another's bid, identify the hadith proof, and explain the exception for auction sales.

Core Idea
Bidding over another's bid is prohibited when harm is caused, but permissible in auction sales.

In this passage, al-Marghinani presents a clear ruling on a common transaction scenario: offering a higher price after the buyer and seller have already agreed. The ruling is rooted in a prophetic hadith and a concern for social harmony. We will examine the proof, the rationale, and the crucial exception that allows auction sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is 'bidding over another's bid' (al-saum 'ala saum ghayrihi). The ruling is prohibition, classified as makruh tahrimi (prohibited, close to haram).
The ruling is not absolute haram because of exceptions.
2
Locate the Dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'The man should not bid over the bid of his brother, nor propose over the proposal of his brother.' This is the primary proof.
The hadith is narrated in Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Understand the Rationale
The reason (illah) for the prohibition is that it causes enmity (eehaash) and harm (idraar). The prohibition exists to protect social harmony.
The rationale is derived from the text itself.
4
Determine the Scope
The prohibition applies only when the two parties have mutually agreed upon a price. If no agreement is reached, it is a permissible auction sale (bay' min yazeed).
Auction sales are explicitly excepted.
5
Apply the Same Principle to Marriage
The author notes that the same prohibition applies to proposing to a woman who has already been proposed to, as per the hadith's second part.
This shows consistency across different areas of fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والسوم على سوم غيره) للحديث لا يستام الرجل على سوم أخيه، ولا يخطب على خطبة أخيه» (۱) ولأن في ذلك إيحاشاً واضراراً. وهذا إذا تراضى المتعاقدان على مبلغ ثمن في المساومة، فإذا لم يركن أحدهما على الآخر فهو بيع من يزيد ولا بأس به على ما نذكره وما ذكرناه محمل النهي في النكاح أيضاً. وفي القاموس : السوم في المبايعة كالسوام بالضم سمت بالسلعة وساومت بالسلعة واستمت بها وعليها غاليت واستمته إياها وعليها سالته سومها اه
His saying: (And bidding over another's bid) due to the hadith: 'The man should not bid over the bid of his brother, nor propose over the proposal of his brother.' And because in that there is enmity and harm. This if the two contracting parties have mutually agreed on a price amount in the negotiation. So if one of them does not rely on the other, it is a sale by auction and there is no harm in it as we will mention. And what we have mentioned is also the meaning of the prohibition in marriage. In al-Qamus: al-saum in transactions is like al-sawam (with dammah): you bid on a commodity, you bargained on a commodity, you sought to buy it and haggled over it, and you asked its price.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This is a ruling statement with proof from hadith explaining the prohibition of bidding over another's bid in a sale. The hadith explicitly prohibits a person from bidding over the bid of his brother (fellow Muslim). The rationale is that it causes enmity and harm. The ruling applies when the parties have already come to a mutual agreement on a price. If no agreement has been reached and the seller is simply accepting bids, then it is a permissible auction sale. The same principle applies to marriage proposals. The passage also defines the term 'saum' from al-Qamus.

Key Terms
السوم (al-saum): The act of offering a price in a transaction; bidding.
السوم على سوم غيره (al-saum 'ala saum ghayrihi): Bidding over the bid of another; offering a higher price after the buyer and seller have already agreed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In all sales
B) Only when the seller and buyer have mutually agreed on a price
C) Only in auction sales
D) Only in marriage proposals

Key Takeaway
Bidding over another's bid is prohibited when it causes harm after a mutual agreement, but auction sales remain permissible due to the absence of prior agreement.
